MUSTDIE-
KILLS FLIES
The Red Page
War
John Masefield has written the finest
account that has yet appeared of the Gal-
lipoli adventure. Sydney de Logue’s The
Straits Impregnable remains the most im-
pressive personal narrative; but Masefield’s
imagination is so remarkable that from a
few months’ sojourn with the Red Cross at
Gallipoli he is enabled to present what
seems to be the circumstantial testimony of
an eyewitness present during the whole
campaign. In Gallipoli (Ileinemann, per
Dymock’s Library) Masefield presents him-
self as the ideal historian, combining a won-
derful grasp of the complicated strategy of
the campaign with the grim realism that
enforces the human personal note. The
reader-is not merely following the history of
this forlorn adventure; he is fighting in
the ranks beside John Masefield. And the
story is written with so charming a style
that its place in our literature should be
permanent. Take a glorious passage like
this, describing the departure of the fleet of
transports from Lemnos on the way to The
Landing:—•
“Ship after ship, crammed with soldiers,
moved slowly out of harbor in the lovely
day, and felt again the heave of the sea.
No such gathering of fine ships has ever-
been seen upon this earth, and the beauty
and the exultation of the youth upon them
made them like sacred things as they moved
away They had said good-bye to home
that they might offer their lives in the
cause we stand for. In a few hours at most,
as they well knew, perhaps a tenth of them
would have looked their last on the sun, and
be a part of foreign earth or dumb things
that the tides push. Many of them would
have disappeared for ever from the know-
ledge of men, blotted from the book of life
none would ever know how- —by a fall or
chance shot in the darkness, in the blast of
a shell, or alone, like a hurt beast, in some
scrub*or gully, far from comrades and the
English speech and the English singing. And
perhaps a third of them would be mangled,
blinded or broken, lamed, made imbecile or
disfigured, with the color and the taste of
life taken from them, so that they would
never more move with comrades nor exult in
the sun... .But... .all they felt was a glad-
ness of exultation that their young courage
was to be used. They went like kings in a
pageant to the imminent death....”
Masefield can write like that —and there
are many more passages as beautiful—
because to him “this failure is the second
grand event of the war; the first was Bel-
gium’s answer to the German ultimatum.”
And for the Anzacs he can hardly find
enough words of praise. “They were the
finest body of j ? oung men ever brought
together in modern times. For physical
beauty and nobility of bearing they sur-
passed any men I have ever seen; they
walked and looked like the kings in old
poems and reminded me of the line in
Shakespeare: J Baited like eagles having
lately bathed .’ As their officers put it, ‘they
were in the pink of condition and didn’t
care a damn for anybody.’ There was no
thought of surrender in those marvellous
young men; they were the flower of this
world’s manhood, and died as they had lived,
owning no master on this earth When a
big shell was coming men of other races
would go into their dugouts, but these men
paused only to call it a bastard, and then
went on with their work.”
Of the greatness of the adventure Mase-
field has no doubts. “No army in history
has made a more heroic attack ; no army in
history has been set such a task. No other
body of men in any modern war has been
called upon to land over mined and wired
waters under a cross fire of machine guns.
Our men achieved a feat without par-
allel in war, and no other troops in the
world (not even Japanese or Ghazis in the
hope of Heaven) would have made good
those beaches on the 25tli of April.” And
clearly he puts down the reasons why that
great emprise just missed being a great vic-
tory. It all comes back to the old cry:
“We had not the men nor the munitions.”
“Had the 50,000 tnen who came there in
July and August been there in June, our
men could have kept on striking. But
our victory could not be followed up. More
than a month passed before it could be
followed up. During that month the Turks
dug themselves new fortresses, brought up
new guns, made new stores of ammunition
and remade their army. Their beaten troops
were withdrawn and replaced by the very
pick and flower of the Turkish Empire.
When we attacked again we found a very
different enemy; the jron was cold, and we
had to begin again from the beginning.”
And early in May Sir lan Hamilton had
learnt that the Gallipoli affair, instead of
being a part of a concerted scheme, was to
be the only attack upon the Turks in that
quarter. In its later stages the scheme
called for an attack by the Russians from
the Black Sea, which would keep the main
Turkish army at Constantinople, instead of
letting it overwhelm the Mesopotamia expe-
dition and hold back the Grand Duke in
the Caucasus. But Russia found that she
could not give that necessary aid, and w r e
had to hold up the whole Turkish army on
Gallipoli. Masefield has no answer to the
question why more men and more high
explosives were not sent—in time. All he
can say is that the delay lost us a cam-
paign that we had practically won.
Of the tragical Suvla Bay muddle, Mase-
field presents a vivid and accurate picture.
The tactical scheme of that confused battle
is made clear —for the first time in print;
and, thus presented, it is of absorbing inter-
est. It is a terrible story of mistakes and
delay—not Anzac mistakes, not Anzac delay.
The English at last reached the top of
Chanuk. “Victory was flooding up over
Chanuk like the Severn tide ; our men had
scaled the scarp, and there below them lay
the ditch, the long- grey streak of the
Hellespont, the victory and the reward of
victory. The battle lay like a field ripe to
the harvest; our men had but to put in the
sickle. The third column was the sickle of
that field, that third column which had lost
its way in the blackness of the wilderness.”
But it was coming up the hill at that
moment, when our own guns shelled the
crest for some minutes too long and prac-
tically wiped out that brave handful of vic-
tors !
Masefield brings home to his reader the
grim realities of that campaign as hitherto
no other writer except Sydney de Logue has
done. He has many new things to tell, such
as his explanation of the inaction of the
Turks at the two evacuations; his history of
the campaign is . complete and lucid, but his
admiration for the soldiers who did the im-
possible remains the clearest note in this
pcean of heroism.
“In the haze of the full moon the men
filed off from the trenches to the beaches,
and passed away from Gallipoli, from the
unhelped attempt which they had given their
bodies and their blood to make. They had
lost no honor. They were not to blame,
that they were creeping off in the dark, like
thieves in the night. Had others (not of
their profession), many hundreds of miles
away, but been as they, as generous, as wise,
as foreseeing, as full of sacrifice, those
thinned companies with the looks of pain in
their faces, and the mud of the hills thick
upon their bodies, would have given thanks
in Santa Sophia three months before. They
had failed to take Gallipoli, and the mine-
fields still barred the Hellespont, but they
had fought a battle such as has never been
seen upon this earth. What they had done
will become a glory for ever, wherever the
deeds of heroic unhelped men are honored
and pitied and understood. They went up
at the call of duty, with a bright banner of a
?^ fc i e ' cry ’ a £ ainst an impregnable fort.
Without guns, without munitions, without
help, and without drink, they climbed the
scarp, and held it by their own glorious
manhood, quickened by a word from their
chief. Now they were giving back the scarp,
and going out into new adventures, wherever
the war might turn.”
Hilaire Belloc’s Second Phase of A General
Sketch of the European War (Nelson, per
Sydney Book Club) reconstructs for us in
detail and with a remarkable number of
plans and explanatory maps the battle of
the Marne. It is this battle that, in the
judgment of Belloc and that other excellent
war historian, de Souza, will prove to have
been the decisive battle of the war. For both
these critics agree that since this battle
smashed the original plan of the German
Staff, it is a defeat not only of their armies
but of their entire strategy. Here, almost
miraculously, the assassin stumbled as he
struck his blow. For Belloc’s analysis of
the contending forces shows that the Ger-
mans at the Marne had 75 divisions against
51—-practically more than seven men to the
Allies’ five. The superior force was defeated
by _an inferior force/ owing to the error
which kept the main body of the Germans
massed near Verdun. On the rest of the
line, where the action took place, the forces
were nearly equal.
Incidentally, 'Belloc confirms a Swiss
critic’s assertion that the Germans missed
their great chance when they decided to come
via Belgium, instead of smashing through
Verdun. They would have had short com-
munication lines through friendly territory.
The Germans could have smashed Verdun
with the ease with which they smashed
Liege. For it was that lesson of Libge,
learned by the French with such marvellous
swiftness, that saved Verdun. Through the
Verdun gate the masses of the Hun, with
both flanks protected, would have triumph-
antly poured straight down upon Paris. But
the Bosche thought of Verdun too late.
What happened at the Marne, as Belloc’s
reader will be surprised to find, was quite
simple. The Germans meant to curve down
in a gigantic sweep to the west, with their
eastern wing relatively stationary, and, dis-
regarding Paris, sharply coil their western
flank around the French army, and sur-
round it. But the Hun, in the midst of his
triumphant southerly sweep, was surprised to
find a new French army upon his flank.
There was then only one thing to do, to thin
out his line to the west, .to support the
threatened wing, while the German centre
continued its pre-arranged plan to smash the
French centre, and thus dislocate the whole
French army. In that effort, which so nearly
succeeded, the Germans left a gap in their
line, and through that gap Foch triumph-
antly stormed, severing the German army
and compelling it incontinently to retire all
along the line. “The whole victory turns
out to be a defeat suffered by a greatly
superior force at the hands of a greatly
inferior force, not through surprise nor
through the sudden massing of men against
a weak spot, but from a spontaneous dis-
memberment of the superior force, of which
dismemberment the inferior force at once
takes advantage.” That error in German
tactics “will prove to have determined all
the future of the modern world.”
Belloc pleasantly forecasts the judgment
of the historian on the result if the Germans
had broken the French centre—and how very
nearly they did! “The French, with the con-
tingent of their British Allies, attempted to
redress the desperate situation due to their
inferiority of number by a surprise attack
with insufficient force upon the German
right flank. But the Ist German army
parried this effort in time, enveloped the
Allied left wing in their turn, and meanwhile
broke the Allied centre, thereby deciding the
whole issue in their favor. The Allied army
was cut in two fragments, one of which was
pushed back upon Paris and contained, the
other enveloped towards the eastern fron-
tier and destroyed. Upon the western rem-
nant thus contained, after the destruction of
the eastern half, the whole mass of the in-
vaders next turned in numbers three to one,
and this amazing campaign of three weeks
was at an end.”
Jt
The three phases of the battle are studied
and explained by Belloc with extreme lucid-
ity. The author points out one minor weak-
ness in the German Higher Command which
contributed to the defeat. This was “an
eye to dramatic effect.” A sort of triumphant
entry into Nancy had been planned for the
Emperor. “On the critical day of the action
the little, aged figure of that unfortunate
man whose physical disabilities were, per-
haps, in part responsible for the war, was to
be seen from the French lines watching the
battle.... He was distant from the nearest
observers by more than the common range of
a field-piece; he was caught only by the care-
ful scanning of glasses... .Grouped about
was the ‘brilliant staff’ of the newspapers
and the stage; and the White Cuirassiers,
which was to be the escort of his triumph,
were massed to the left and behind. He had
also put on for that day the white uniform
of that corps and the silver helmet. It was
pathetic and a little grotesque.” At the end
of that fatal day the Kaiser went home and
took off his gorgeous uniform.
But though the battle was woU by the
Allies it was not the complete defeat that
Joffre had planned. Whose the fault was is
not yet clear—may never be clear; but if the
British could have come up earlier or the
French army on the left wing could have
delayed their flanking movement, the Ger-
man right wing under Kluck would have
been destroyed.
Belloc ably disposes of the idea that the
new phase of trench warfare was foreseen
and provided for by the Germans. Their
provision of machine guns was expressly for
active fighting in the field and for the check-
ing of local counter-offensives; their giant
guns were for the induction of fortresses.
But both, to their surprise, proved invalu-
able in the trench-warfare to which they
were pinned. The line the Germans held so
long unchanged was not the line they chose,
but the line they were compelled to choose,
else why should - they have abandoned the
possession of the Channel ports?
Finally, in one sentence Belloc states the
grave defect in the German machine, the
defect chiefly responsible for the defeat at
the Marne: “A sort of routine incapable of
correcting itself in time; the momentum, as
it were, of a huge engine working smoothly,
enormous in design, but lacking elasticity
altogether.”
Billzac
His first hefty prints on the sands of time
were noticed in April, 1915, and, in the
manner of his coming he puts on recoi’d his
right to stay. A young, full-blooded animal,
tasting his first big fight, and carrying in
his hands the good name of a young and
untried country, he wrote that name in defi-
nite characters on the roll of nations. Above
his love of adventure he fights for what he
considers right, merely because in his judg-
ment it is right, and without either hope of
reward or fear of consequences, but it is
questionable if, finding himself in wrong, he
would not still bog in; for although not a
lover of scrap for its own sake, .once in he
hates to leave it unfinished. Generous him-
self, with the true generosity that forgets
the gift given or service rendered imme-
diately it is accomplished, he is a trifle in-
tolerant of those whom custom and environ-
ment have made more cautious. He owns a
healthy pride in his country, which, while
not blatant, he will cheerfully demonstrate
to any who would belittle, in his hearing,
the land of his birth. A good sport bihjself,
he recognises a brother sport anywhere, and
will meet him on terms of equality, even if
he happens to be an earl —or a fish-hawker.
His sense of humor is the last one he loses,
and if rather grim at times, the fact that
he sees a joke in a cobber’s downfall does
not make his practical sympathy less spon-
taneous. His code with women, in fact, his
whole moral code, can be summed up in a
sentence—“Do a fair thing all round”—and
his shortcomings in this respect are those of
the male all the world- over, no better and
no worse. Loyal in the main to those in
authority, over him, if he considers that a
superior is not as good a man as himself he
doesn’t get headaches planning how to hide
the fact. His motto, if he had time for such
a kindergarten appliance, would be: “Be
dinkum, you !” A new-born race of men.
their eyes newly opened, in the most terrible
of all schools, to broader views of men and
things, they are full men’s size, and would
improve, the stock of any nation on the
planet, if the progeny is as good as its fore-
bears, Which is doubtful.
—Bill Badd.

“Bill. Badd”: “Copabella’s” brilliant exposition of
the social problem is unfortunate in that it gets no-
where in particular. If sex relation is to become a casual
conversational subject such as dancing or the advan-
tages of the tote, there is all the material for a
gorgeous hatful of trouble for the community making
the experiment. Flapperdom is admittedly well
versed in the subject, but the very fact that the
knowledge is clandestine makes for restraint. Re-
move that and the only unexplored field for the flapper
in search of wisdom is personal experience. Results
are fairly obvious. The main difficulty is with the
flapper. Wearing no man’s particular label, she is
fair hunting for any predatory male, and if her
knowledge of the pursuit and its object are to be
accepted as an ordinary characteristic, and socially
approved, she must be otherwise protected. The
only logical sequence is to make seduction illegal,
and to say that the law is powerless to prevent it is
piffle. I know several highly-respected merchants who
are only restrained from plain swindling because the
law says no. It doesn’t follow that there are no
Swindlers, and there would still be seducers, but the
frisky flapper would have a definite knowledge of the
result of disregarding the regulations of society,
which with all her vaunted shrewdness she hasn’t got
now. One law on the statute book is worth a whole
constitution of the unwritten article.
“An Ignorant Innocent” writes this amaz
ing, but genuine letter:—
Re “Copabella’s” assertion that the
aren’t any innocents over 10 years of age
(B. 2S/9/T6). I wonder what he would
think (I suppose he’s a he) of a girl— 01
should I say, a woman—of 23 being an inno-
cent of innocents; a greenhorn as regards
anything to do with sex matters? What the
White Slave Trade is, or the Red Plague, or
any of the other things that happen I haven’t
the faintest conception.
Of a baby, and the necessary things to be
done if one is wanted, or to be avoided if one
isn’t, I know nothing. In fact, my knowledge
of a baby is not advanced from the time two
decades ago when I asked in a piping treble
how I was born. Then I was told I was born
of the dust. And ever afterwards for years
whenever I saw dust bowling along the street
I would say to my mother: “What a lot of
babies are being born now!”
Perhaps “Copabella” would say I was a
numbskull, a brainless ninny, to have no more
knowledge than a 10-years’-old child of these
days. But I’m neither one nor the other. I
don’t say I don’t want to know. I would like
to know. I think I ought to know. But how
am I to know? These things are not learnt
by intuition.
I have often been led to think there must be
something extremely objectionable about the
whole concern of marriage. There is so
much mystery about it all. Babies just
happen. And no questions must be asked.
Why? One would think that the coming of
a baby would be such a noble, natural, glori-
ous thing that it would be spoken about as
any natural thing should be. Why isn’t it?
What is wrong about it? Or is it that it’s
so holy; and we do not speak about holy
things? Then why punish a girl who is the
bearer of a holy thing because she has not
repeated a bald formula before a clergyman?
It is all very perplexing. So I don’t bother
to think of it often. Thinking about it won’t
give me knowledge.
I have three brothers, a sister, a father
and a mother. I went to a convent till I
was 15, and then to the Sydney High School
for a couple of years ; and for the remaining
number of years till now have lived at a
surfing suburb within a few miles of the
“wicked” city of Sydney. And yet, notwith-
standing that since I could read, and many
years before, that demoralising publication
The Bulletin has been a weekly visitant
to our home, I’m as ignorant of the vital
things of life as a five-year-older. I’m a
great reader, too. It’s my one passion. But
I have not gone in for books with purple
patches. I read your criticisms, dear Red
Page, and that suffices. I am old-fashioned
and behind the times with my books as with
everything else.
I am glad there are many things a girl
can do these days besides marrying. So I
suppose I will jog through the world as
unknowing as I now am; keeping men at
arm’s length. For so my intuition —my only
teacher—tells me I shall escape any evil.
What the evil is I don’t know.
(Naturally I could not sign my name to
this outpouring. But, believe me, I am
genuine ; though “Copabella”—l nearly write
Copper Top each time —might not think so.
He would be astounded at my ignorance if
he knew the full extent of it. But then I
never go to enlightening picture-shows. Four
years ago saw my last entry into one. They
don’t appeal to this child. They’re too ludi-
crously melodramatic.)
The almost incredible thing about this letter is _
the writer’s admission that she has a mother. What
is a mother for if she does not instruct her daughter
in the elementary facts of sexual knowledge? The
writer is first referred to her mother for the informa-
tion she seeks and should have. Failing that source,
she can purchase, say at Dymoek’s, any of the excel-
lent books on sex matters written in such a way as
to give no offence to the most sensitive mind. This
page recommends, in this order, such books as Her-
self, by Dr. E. B. Lowry; What a Young Woman
Ought to Know, by Dr. Mary Wood-Allen; or Tok-
ology, by Alice B. Stockman; and, for information
about syphilis, the cheap edition of Brieux’ Damaged
Goods, the production of which at the Criterion is
expressly designed to enlis-hten the public on this
scourge.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soai)
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the' breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
ETJDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.

MR. GEORGE GOODMAN.
The following testimony is from Mr.
George Goodman, better known as
“Goody” in the profession, and one of
the most widely and professionally
known of a band of brilliant theatrical
managers who form an artistic link
between the theatrical past and
present.
Mr. Goodman has been associated with
the present firm of J. C. Williamson,
Ltd., for a long number of years, ex-
tending back to its inception, when
as “Williamson, Garner and Mus-
grove,” it occupied, as it does to-day,
the foremost position in the theatrical
world of Australia, and was respon-
sible for the finest imported produc-
tions of Grand Opera and versatile
drama and comedy.
Mr. Goodman's experience with these
productions and association with the
many “stars” imported under the Wil-
liamson management, is proverbial,
and the following letter will be of
considerable interest to the many
members of the theatrical profession
throughout the Commonwealth, who,
physically exhausted by the exacting
nature of their many roles, will find
in the medicine Mr. Goodman advo-
cates a nerve rejuvenator and health
restorer of remarkable power and cer-
tainty. Mr. Goodman writes from his
Sydney residence thus: —

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Gossiping Flowers.
I know there was talk in the garden last
night,
When the man and the maiden were there,
His hand on her hand, and her rounded
throat white,
In the dusk that was dark as her hair.
Though they said not a word there was
gossip, I vow—
Sweet speech breathing passion and
praise—
’Twixt the rose on the hedge and the rose on
the bough,
And ’twas done in the simplest of ways.
With the spirit of love round about through
the house,
Oh, how could the roses be blind?
And they said quite a lot in the way of the
flowers,
As they nodded and swayed on the wind.
Though the folk that know all, knowing little,
allege
That ’tis idle to make roses speak,
Said the rose on the bough to the rose on the
hedge:
“She has borrowed my bloom for her
cheek!”
Said the rose on the bough, as it nodded and
bent
To the rose on the hedge, hanging there:
“Since budhood I think that it surely was
meant
I should drowse in the dusk of her hair!”
Said the rose on the hedge, as it yielded one
flake,
And watched that flake circle and float:
“I, too. Damask One, have been robbed for
her sake,
For my cream is the cream of her throat.
“And if I were but there as a love-gift, a
pledge,
My hue and its hue would be one,
And for this I would lose all the green of
my hedge
And the lights of the stars and the sun.
“Though the visiting bee and the gold o’
the noon,
And the moths and the zephyrs are much,
It were more for one moment to languish
and swoop
In the silk and the fire of her touch.”
But, alas for the roses, their scent and their
sighs,
And the lights they had known and the
dews!
For the night hid away from the light of her
eyes
All the damask and cream of their hues.
And the lovers went off caring little, I vow,
For the pain of the roses behind —-
For the rose on the hedge and the rose on
the bough.
As they nodded and swayed in the wind.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Per Hospital Ship.
Thin, ashen face above the linen white,
Dark eyes, fast closing on their last drear
sight
Of worlds war-worn—cramp’d ward walls,
swaying yet
As slow’d the ship, sad freighted with regret,
And weariness of shattered men, whose name
Was honor to their land. Helpless he came,
And almost came too late to know the ship
That bore the soul a-tremble on his lin
Was anchored by his own fair harbor side.
But Death, who stepp’d aboard at ebb of tide,
Paused, that a gentle hand might reach him
first,
And near his lips lay violets, that erst
Had drunk the morning dew and on their
breath
They bore a Soldier’s soul past smiling
Death !
VICTORIA.
M. B. PATON.
The Braemar Road.
The road that leads to Braemar winds ever
in and out,
It wanders here and dawdles there, and trips
and turns about
Like a child upon an errand that play has
put to rout.
By the road that leads to Braemar the grey-
beard poplars stand,
And on the sky pale tapestries are broidered
in a band
With the flashing, frosty needle that gleams
in Winter’s hand.
There are haggard apple-orchards on either
side the way
That once flung scented largesse to every
summer’s day,
To mingle with the incense where the hot
pine-needles lay.
And down the road’s long vista, the shadows
spread like wings
As lightly spun and purple as the shade the
evening brings
For circling children’s eyelids round with
mystic, drowsy rings.
The rutty road to Braemar, all weather-worn
and brown,
Goes stumbling on its journey until it nears
the town,
Then with glory of the wattle-bloom its arms
are weighted down!
Oh, the long, long road to anywhere seems
haply without end,
But who shall call it weary with the love of
some good friend
To greet him like the wattle as he turns the
final bend?
N.S.W.
NINA MURDOCH.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.

For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have Tiie
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Humber, to any

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, I'erse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Neivspaper Clippings.. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs In Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same

but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1916.
THE KIND OF LABOR PARTY
AUSTRALIA WANTS.
The yarn that the Labor party in State and
Federal politics has been turned into a dog-
fight by the conscription issue is too thin.
The party had been in hard training a long
time for this or some other sort of rumpus.
When Chancy of the Overflow comes down to
the city and has seven beei-s before break-
fast and intercepts 17 more before midday, he
usually feels that the time is ripe for another.
Whereupon Chancy gets a trip in the patrol
waggon with half a ton of law sitting on his
chest. Ever afterwards the memoirs of
Chancy are filled with denunciation of that
final beer. It did all the damage. He forgets
the couple of dozen that went before. Con-
scription was the Labor party’s 25th pot, and
everybody connected with the movement,
whether he be a “leader,” one of the mob or
among the crowd of hungry camp followers,
knows that this is so. The reasons
why the Labor party has arrived at the
stage of disruption are multitudinous.
Partly they are the outcome of plain
cussedness, partly the consequences of
trying to work with blunt tools, of mistaking
the shadow for the substance, of chasing the
fatted calf and imagining that the Prodigal
Son has turned up again, of trying to graft
on to a system of representative government
a scheme of regulation bv wirepull which
gave the people an excessive supply of repre-
sentation by marionettes. But the rock-
bottom reason for the Labor party’s steady
demoralisation has been the readiness with
which almost every ideal that gave it birth
was abandoned. It early fell victim to the
fatal delusion that the test of its strength
and usefulness was to be looked for in mere
success at the polls—that Labor policies
were justified when a sufficiently large num-
ber of Labor politicians were returned to
Parliament to form a Ministry. In the per-
fection of plans for seizing upon the appar-
atus of government adherence to principle
was lost sight of, and every time the Labor
politician made friends with the Mammon of
Unrighteousness he brought nearer the day
when Labor would have to go back to its
altar fires or be an adventurer and time-
server. To-day the political parties that are
called Labor are in every State of Australia
living from hand to mouth, mistrusting them-
selves find distrusted by tneir followers.
That a Parliamentary party should be
broken matters nothing. It is usually a good
thing, and when it happens the occurrence is
generally good evidence that the event .was
overdue. Principles are the things that
matter, not the parties or cliques that
manage to get control of the Parliamentary
machine. And The Bulletin suspects that
there are very few men in politics or out who
were not persuaded long ago that the public
affairs of their country would gain much and
lose little when the time arrived for Labor
to cleanse itself and be in its right mind. If
the testimony of Labor organisations, of
Labor politicians, of secret juntas and those
who either obey or defy them, of writers in
the Labor press, and the promoters of fusions
are to be believed, this is the one point upon
which there is unanimity of opinion. That
the accumulation of calamity is being
brought to a head by the turmoil over con-
scription is only an incident. Every word
that is spoken and written on the subject
proves nothing so much as consciousness
within the Labor party that it made a false
start, and has been wandering in the bush.
The conscription issue has merely expedited
what was inevitable. Fisher felt it in his
bones—and bolted. Spence was under no
delusion about what was coming when he
was relieved of the Postmastei'-Generalship.
The performances of Holman and Jack
Scaddan were irreconcilable with anything
except early collapse. All the current
upheavals in the vicinity of various Parlia-
ments are only symptoms of what is going
on among the electors. For politicians to
talk about “saving the party by unity” is
blither. The Labor movement is not going
to be saved or assisted in any way by in-
trigues among politicians whose apostasy
has been flagrant and continuous.
■* * * *
The Labor party was born at an hour
when the appearance of some such force in
politics was imperative. It was a party of
high aims. It stood for the fair deal and
the decent thing. It was wanted then. It
is wanted now. The Old Brigade was swept
away because of its crimes. Its successors
are being driven into new camps because of
their crimes and follies and evasions. This
paper has no doubt that men of the Holman
type were sincere in their professions and
programmes when they entered politics. Nor
has it any doubt that so long as they re-
mained a critical and suggestive force in the
country’s politics they discharged a useful
function in public affairs. But that in
constructive add administrative field they
have been failures they themselves will
hardly deny. They know that it is so—
know, too, that the system they have tried to
work on has been an impossible one, that it
was galloping from bad to worse, and that
between the intentions of the Labor party
and its achievements there is as much differ-
ence as between the waratah and the prickly-
pear. But the demand for the fair deal and
the decent thing is still strong—the oppor-
tunities for Labor to stand as the enemy of
parasitism, of privilege, of muddle and
waste and shirk more numerous than ever.
The work to be done for Australia was at no
time so great as it is now. But it can never
be done by men who shut their eyes and ears
to every other consideration than hanging
on to office —who when they enter Parlia-
ment drop their principles on the doormat
and turn the Legislature into a school of log-
rolling and the national cashbox into an elec-
tioneering institution. Nor will it be done if
the electors among whom Labor looks for
support are content to be fed on doles and
shams while the nation’s future is pawned
and its strength sapped away for the tem-
porary advantage of the unscrupulous and
the unclean. The Bulletin watches the
twistings of the politicians and the writh-
ings of Labor organisations with a satisfac-
tion made mournful by recollecting that it
has hundreds of times warned Labor men
they were sliding down a steep place. It is
no good groaning over the past. It is useless
to moan about the future. But it is just
plain madness to disregard either the
one or the other. The Labor 1 move-
ment has before it the choice of two
roads. One is hard, the other easy. The
first means work and economy ; the protec-
tion of the man who toils; the maintenance
of principle; insistence on social service
being something more than a catch-cry; the
building Up of patriotic citizenship. The
second means shirk and leaning up against a
post and dodging to-morrow; the encourage-
ment of sharks and boodlers; the bartering
of principle. This road leads just where it
has been leading from the start —straight to
ruin. That there must be a Labor party in
some form is certain. If it goes one way it
can do big things for Australia. If it goes
the other way—well, the fair deal and the
decent thing will be a long way off.
THE NARK.
Whatever the final count of the votes may-
reveal, it is plain that, but for N. S. Wales,
the Commonwealth would have carried the
Referendum comfortably. For some reason
the State which is alleged to mother all
the rest is the Nark of the whole continent,
and presents a broad, dull buffer against
which reform and progress perpetually land
with a sickening thud. Multitudes of read-
ers have been amazed at the opinions ex-
pressed of Botany Bay and the surrounding
country by the first Europeans who set eyes
on these shores. They could not see how a
white man could make a decent home in such
a place. Yet the things they advanced against
his possible residence there, such as the pre-
valence of lightning and the forlorn aspect of
the country, did not seem to justify _ their
pessimism. Possibly the real explanation of
their feelings may be found in some occult
prophetic thrill which went through those
fathers of discovery standing face to face
with the future of a continent. It may be
that they foresaw a kaleidoscope of changing
referenda and bursting parties, with the
focus of every disturbance within a radius
of 50 miles of their landing, and believed
that for the good of the race it would be an
excellent thing if the whole neighborhood
could be quietly chloroformed out of exist-
ence to give the rest of Australia a chance.
No one to-day would deny that_N. S. Wales
is quite a possible place for existence, if its
politicians could be gently hanged, its politi-
cal ideas put in a bath of strong antiseptic,
and administrative work wholly remodelled;
but, even then, there remains a deadly sus-
picion that the soil or the air or something
might continue to produce crops of the same
kind. Scientists have argued that peoples
are what they eat, that a limestone country
will, produce .men, apd a granite
country granite people, while a flat, watery
land gets mud into the constitution and
develops fat, unemotional folk with large, flat
feet. On such a theory, the fact that Syd-
ney and the surrounding area, which is to
all intents and purposes N. S. Wales, are
set oru, crumbling sandy rock, may account
for the sand-blight in the popular imagina-
tion and the shifty feeling about the political
boots that is so characteristic of the region.
Whatever the cause, N. S. Wales appears
to exist for the sole purpose qf narking the
Commonwealth. The chieT reason the Com-
monwealth hasn’t the powers to-do a whole
lot of things for the general good 'is that
N. S. Wales turned down previous requests
submitted at successive referenda. The chief
reason Australia hasn’t a capital city and
has to get along as best it can with a muddle
of wasted effort and bad feeling at Canberra
is that N.' S. Wales raised a whole lot of
tin-pot objections, arid wouldn’t be satisfied
with-'any Federal city that was more than a
suburb of Sydney. The chief reason Aus-
tralian industries have never been decently
protected, and that so much good cash went
out of the country to help the Kaiser
buy cannon, is that a mob of im-
porters of shoddy in N. S. Wales got their
grip on the throat of the continent and
twisted it from the decent and patriotic pur-
pose of establishing an effective tariff. All
along the line, and whenever a question of
vital importance has been raised, N. S. Wales
has contrived to get up against the main
stream of national thought; and now, at the
gravest crisis of all, it is N. S. Wales that
has voted emphatically to prolong the war
and lessen the punch which Australia has
it in her . power to give the German.
Westralia, Victoria and Tasmania have
voted strongly for conscription; the Queens-
land vote’ at the finish will be no worse than
a tie; South Australia, with a big portion
of its population of German extraction, leans
to the negative side. N. S. Wales alone of
the States stands out with big figures as the
anti-nationalNark that it has always shown
itself to be.
The break of railway gauges, which is
such a vital matter in connection with Aus-
tralian defence, and which has so steadily
hampered inter-State traffic all the time,
illustrates the attitude of N. S. Wales
towards the rest of the continent. In 1846
Gladstone advised Australia to take to a
gauge of 4ft. Biin. —a gauge which was
afterwards adopted as the standard in
Britain. Six years later N. S. Wales passed
an Act that all gauges in the State should
be sft. 3in. Victoria and South Aus.,
against the British advice, resolved to fol-
low N. S. Wales for the sake of uniformity,
and Victoria started to build railways with
the rails that distance apart. But N. S.
Wales couldn’t help being a Nark. Regard-
less of everything, she went back to a
gauge of 4ft. B£in., and has continued to
build lines of that pattern ever since; and
now, when a proposal is made to standardise
the gauges, N. S. Wales with the old impu-
dence asks the rest of the Commonwealth
to adopt the thing that represents its own
breach of faith. Probably, if it could get
the others to adopt it, it would rush off for
a big loan while the metals were being
shifted and return to sft. 3in. on the plea
that its people had grown too fat to sit in
narrower compartments.
* * * *
Put it on the very lowest ground, is it
going to pay N. S. Wales to be the perpetual
Nark? Does it pay anybody to be? Take
what happened over the Commonwealth Con-
stitution as an instance. N. S. Wales,
through the typical crumbling-sandstone
politician Reid, hung up the whole business.
Then the late George Turner, representing
the other States, “laid” for the Nark.
George Reid demanded the State capital for
N. S. Wales. Turner let him have it—
provided that Sydney was barred for ever
and that Melbourne had it to go on with.
Thus Sydney got the shadow and Melbourne
got the substance; and that is the sort of
thing that is always liable to happen to the
Nark. It is possible to be too greedy; and,
after all, that Sydney is a Nark isn’t due so
much to cussedness as to narrow selfishness.
In the recent Referendum that cry of selfish-
ness was raised: “We have done enough!
llow are we going to manage if we send
away more men?” This paper knows of no
quality that is less likely to make friends.
It knows of none that is so certain to assure
enemies when friends are most needed.
DAMAGED GOODS.
The tremendous dangers of certain forms of
disease, which our showmen, with so much
enthusiasm for the general good, are now
exhibiting in all their horror in the theatres,
have forced the average citizen to grave con-
sideration of the whole moral atmosphere
which surrounds him. That a chance kiss
in the warm dusk when all the airs of
Arcady are abroad should involve misery to
the third generation is enough to discount
Arcady forever; but that a badly-washed
beer glass, a public towel which has not
been boiled, or even the reckless eating of a
restaurant meal with a doubtful fork should
be as vicious as laughing Summer under the
fig-tree are facts which hedge us about with
shuddering suspicion. To imagine, however,
that such dangers are of one sort only and
can be grouped under a single specific dis-
ease name is to get the wrong spotlight on
the national sanitation altogether. Our
damaged goods are of infinite number and
variety, and the ravages of red plagues and
white plagues must not be allowed to obscure
the truth that in the kaleidoscope of destruc-
tion there are a lot of other colors. No
theatre has yet exploited these other con-
tributions to universal ruin, and even the
daily newspapers in their holy desire for the
weal of the right-thinking hardly refer to
them; yet when it comes to selecting the
heavyweight champion among diseases, the
virus which runs through the stage version
of “Damaged Goods” seems a mild and
interesting sort of poison compared with
some of the other stuff.
* * * *
Ever since Federation created a Common-
wealth and left the States to care for their
purely local affairs, the extent to which
politicians of all ranks have been disabled,
maimed, ruined, discredited or killed has
filled the ordinary citizen with wonder. The
political game looks comparatively easy, the
track to reforms seems well paved and free
enough from pitfalls; yet the fact remains
that it is about as dangerous as the front-
line trenches and that the number of casual-
ties is continually increasing. Some of the
people in the political hospital are in
grievous case, with open sores that will, take
a lot of healing; some among the politically
dead show scarcely a scar. Watching the
whole procession of State and Federation
as it limps by, there can be no mistake about
its condition. It is a parade of damaged
goods, of men shaken in reputation,
weakened in will and painfully anxious to
hide their crippled condition under a loud
mouthing of the catchwords of the hour.
Strong men there are in those ranks, but
they are not as strong as they once were;
and it is all too painfully evident that an
evil virus in the blood is working deeper and
deeper into the political system. Whether
it is due to dirty office tables or unwashed
election promises or insanitary selection
methods, there can be no doubt about the
rapid spread of the contagion and the im-
mense injury it works. Wise men shake
their heads over the brilliant aspirant after
Parliamentary honors; and grey mothers,
trembling for the fate of only sons, plead
with them to take up snake-charming or
high-wire acrobatics for a living rather than
run the risk of stumbling into the plague-
stricken ways of politics.
In order to find some cure for this dis-
tressing state of affairs it is necessary that
it should have full publicity. This we have
learned from the high-minded providers of
popular amusement. The politics of. the
country are the measure of its joint achieve-
ment ; and if there is dangerous disease ram-
pant in the field where they operate the
public should know about it in all its grim
detail. Just as the nobility of theatrical
directors has forced them to show the
prostitute soliciting in the streets, in order
that the public may properly repent of its
evil ways at six shillings per stall, so the
political prostitute should be shown as he
goes about his nefarious business disseminat-
ing the germs of a more destroying malady.
The damaged goods should be plainly labelled
and all attempt at concealment regarded as
criminal. Doubtless, even with such publi-
city, the infection will still continue; but
things will at least be known for what they
are and fools will cease to applaud the dis-
ease itself as something worthy of praise
and honor. As for the cure, Australia has
that in her own hands. The political disease
as well as the physical one can be dealt with,
and sooner or later the remedies must be
applied. If they are not, there need be no
difficulty about knowing where we are going.
The men in our politics cannot be uniformly
classed as damaged goods without in some
serious degree injuring the things they
handle; and, as it is Australia their infected
fingers touch, the present state of political
blood-poisoning cannot become chronic with-
out bringing about a time when “Damaged
goods” is -written large across the continent
itself. The damaged persons themselves will
see no hope Of curing the evil, because any
kind of cure involves their own retirement
from business; so the chance of remedy lies
with the awakened public which loathes and
fears the disease, and may insist when it
will on drastic surgical treatment.
* * * *
To be perfectly plain about the matter,,
the disease of party is as completely
destructive of the moral fibre as the other
malady is of the physical, and it is in every
way as infectious. Clean, honest politicians
with the best and most worthy intentions in
the world have set out bent on reform and
willing to make all sacrifices to bring their
ideals into the realm of the practical; but,
without exception, the party disease has got
them. Most of them are mere wrecks of
what they might have been; all of them
have the taint more or less in their blood;
some of the best of them are politically dead
before their time. Count the damaged goods
left along the road since Federation, and
shudder. Sum up the political values of all
the men now in the official limelight and
shudder some more. Mark the trend of the
people in the political swim who have not
yet been tested and the organisations push-
ing them along, and add to the shudder a
healthy curse for the unclean thing which
gets all in its clutches. There is no
sphere in which a patriotic Australian can
render such service to his country and his
fellow citizens as the political sphere; and
in no department of our national life is such
poor service rendered. The only explana-
tion of it all is the party disease. Our best
administrators are in turn lost to us; our
best enthusiasts for the popular cause shrink
and fall away into sheer impotency; our best
war cries become the mere purrings of the
prostitute begging custom. Lamely the
stricken array of damaged goods seeks in
new names and fresh combinations for an
escape from the horror; but there is no
escape to be found that way. The one clean
road of cure is the one which the diseased
politicians will not attempt to take until the
public forces them to do so. Happily, unlike
the red plague, which must be dealt with a
sufferer at a time, the party disease can be
swept away at a single stroke. One measure


embodying the principles of an elective Min-
istry, a Referendum with popular Initiative
and the right of Recall of members of the
Legislature who refuse to do their work,
would wipe party out of existence. There
would be no more contagion, no more dam-
aged goods, and Australia could get out of
hospital with a clean certificate of health
as soon as she pleased, fit and able for the
big work that lies before her.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Politician’s Readiness to Euchre
the People.
Of all the crooked deals the electors of
N. S. Wales have had, this coalition is one
of the worst. There is not a word to be
said for it. Up till the day before yester-
day the Wade following was furiously raging
about the iniquities of Holman and his
crowd—denouncing them as muddlers and
spendthrifts, “lost-money” specialists and all
the rest of it. And the HoLMAN-ites were
daily bursting into verbal eruptions about the
unscrupulous instincts of the WADE-ite and
calling on the heavens to disapprove
“these malignant attacks,” and filling the
air with sound and fury. A few months ago,
when the P.L.L. started to punch Holman
into a corner and give him something nasty
to drink, his most dismal wail was about
the horrors that would overwhelm the com-
munity if the other crowd seized the reins.
And now Holman announces that it is im-
perative for the safety of the nation to give
these dreadful people a chance. Wade’s
contribution to the farce is to remark:
“Hear, hear! We must show a united front
to the enemy and carry on the war to the
full extent of our power”—which is not
merely irrelevant; in the circumstances it
is indecent. Wh.at are the facts? Here are
Holman and some of his fuglemen at logger-
heads with the machine that made them,
and because of that he and Wade put then-
heads together and formulate a scheme for
sharing various jobs and taking away from
the electors their statutory right to elect a
new Parliament. There can be no objec-
tion to Holman joining either Wade or the
Seventh Hay Adventists. That is a matter
for him. But when the two of them conspire
to tamper with the rights of the people to
pass judgment upon the performances and
policies of their representatives, they do
something that is hard to differentiate from
a treasonable usurpation of authority. As
for the gag about, the war, Holman and
Wade have nothing to do with the war,
except as citizens. If the actions of one or
other as a citizen have involved him in diffi-
culties with his fellow-citizens, by what moral
right does he claim that, as a politician, he
is entitled to the protection of Parliament
against the consequences of his civic mis-
fortunes? They both of them challenged the
public verdict on a question of national
policy. Their attitude was the same as that
of this paper. Their attitude to-day is that
of whipped hounds, afraid to stand up to
their masters.

A Governor Who Spoke Too Soon.
In the 12-hour political crisis in N. §.
Wales last week his Ex. Strickland at
least started off with admirable intentions.
He set out to defend the people’s privileges.
Not being altogether blind, though he is a
Governor, he knew that a plot was hatching
to take one of their most important rights
from them. But he spoke too soon. It was
his duty to wait until either the Housq had
told him that it had iiot the confidence of
his Ministers or the plot had ripened into a
Bill for depriving the people of the right to
elect a Parliament for 12 months. In his
over-eagerness he seems to have put a
strained interpretation upon the amendment
to the no-confidence motion carried last week.
That amendment, moved by Wade, declared
that the time was inopportune to consider
whether the Government had the confidence
of the House or not. His Ex. was not justi-
fied in assuming that that was tantamount
to a declaration of want of confidence. The
House had, indeed, by a smashing majority,
rejected a motion declaring that the Govern-
ment had lost the Assembly’s confidence.
Somebody may want to talk to his Ex. about
Holman while he is busy fabricating a
new piece of mechanism in his workshop;
but it does not follow that his Ex. has
expressed any opinion upon Holman one
way or the other if he says that he is too.
busy or too happily occupied to be worried
about politicians just then. And the case
would be all the stronger if, over his
shoulder, his Ex. barked that at any rate
he refused to say he didn’t have confidence in
Holman. So the 12-hour crisis ended as it
ought to have done—in an explanation, no
doubt, by Wade, who leads the most numerous
party in the House, that he did not mean his
amendment to express no-confidence in his
new friend Holman ; and in his Ex. with-
drawing his refusal to do any more business
with the Holman Ministry—and waiting.
Hughes and the Referendum.
Prime Minister Hughes puts it this way:
The refusal on the part of a free people to make
sacrifices to defend their freedom will be used as a
proof of the unwisdom of submitting great national
issues directly to the people.
And Hughes says that this is “certain.”
Much as The Bulletin regrets that the
vote was “No,” it is equally certain that
Hughes is wrong, if he means that among
the democratic peoples of the world and the
electors of Australia the plebiscite of Octo-
ber 28 will become a historic warning against
direct appeals to the popular vote on ques-
tions of public policy. No doubt there will
always be people ready to use the result of
the vote as an argument against an exten-
sion of the elector’s share in government.
There have been individuals prepared to take
their oath after every general election that
the results proved the folly of extending
the franchise. But that has only shown
that they were feeling sore, and the
franchise hasn’t been withdrawn. So, too,
the mere fact that Hughes was stung by
what he monkeyed with is not going to make
the electors of to-day and to-morrow surren-
der their claim to sting some more. They
are not going to see in a vote which some
of them consider wrong on one thing a
reason for being permanently deprived of the
right to vote in the way they believe to be
wise about something else. Incidentally, it
would do Hughes no harm to knock off
remarking that the Australian people have
refused to make sacrifices to defend their
freedom. It is not true. For though they
might have done more there is no getting
away from this —they have done a lot, and
the thing they declined to do is one that
Hughes himself told them not long ago he
was opposed to their doing.
A Temporarily-forgotten Menace.
When war and drought bumped Australia
about the same time attention was diverted
from the American Beef Trust. Prior to
the drought its operations in the Common-
wealth aroused alarm and suspicion; since
then it has been almost forgotten that the
Trust is still here and steadily throwing its
tentacles around the live-stock business. In
Australia the drought swept away millions
of head of stock, disorganised the industry
for a period and temporarily checked the
plans of this merciless exploiter of pro-
ducer and consumer alike. But in Maori-
land, where there has been no drought and
things have been booming in the dead-meat
line, alarm has been caused at the hold the
Trust is securing, and an endeavor is being
made to rouse the farmers to a sense of
what they are threatened with. M. A.
Eliott, of Palmerston North, is an active
party in the movement in the Dominion, and
is being strongly supported by the Auck-
land Farmers’ Freezing Co. According to
this authority the Trust owns or controls at
least four of the freezing works in the
Dominion, and is endeavoring to secure others,
while it is generally believed that one of the
biggest works recently erected was built with
money supplied by the Trust. It also seems
that the Trust has manipulated the lamb
export trade, despite the British Govern-
ment commandeering all supplies, and that
it exploited the London market most artistic-
ally last season. It is practically controlling
the chilled meat trade in Great Britain; it is
absolute boss in the United States and the
Argentine. It has secured huge tracts of
country in Northern Australia, is alleged to
have purchased some of the old-established
meatworks, and has erected some of the
finest works in the Commonwealth. Before
the drought deranged things its activities
were noticeable throughout Queensland and
N. S. Wales, despite elaborate steps to secure
secrecy. Beyond doubt it is out to control
the world’s meat supply and is likely to do
so unless the countries concerned wake up.
Its rapacious methods in the past are well
known and admitted, but the suicidal notion
prevails in many quarters that those days
are past and that the Trust will be a friend
to the stockowner by giving good prices and
encouraging production. It is the confidence
of the lamb in the tiger. How little it is
warranted is shown in a statement published
recently in a Maoriland paper:—
A farmer from Maoriland bought land in the
State of Connecticut, U.S.A., at what appeared to
be a cheap price according to M.L. standards, with
the intention of raising cattle. He said: “I found
it impossible for me to raise cattle, for the local
markets were closed against me ; the Meat Trust had
full control and they would only buy stock at their
own price. No butcher could buys direct from us,
for if he did he would be denied supplies by the
Trust. A neighbor wanted to sell two bullocks to
a local butcher, but this butcher replied that he
could not buy without permission of the represen-
tatives of the packers. The community was com-
pletely under the thumb of the Meat Trust. The
price paid by the consumers was high, while the
price paid to the producers was low. The farmer
had to sell to the Trust if he sold at all and accept
the price paid to him-; the retailer had to get his
supplies from the Trust, at its price, and under
its conditions regarding the conduct of his business! ”
This is the concern that is now sitting back
waiting for Australia to stock up again and
become worth plucking, and some stock-
owners are simple enough to regard it as a
benevolent friend to be cherished. Even
some who view its advent into this country
with alarm deprecate interference because
it is held that the stockowners—these autho-
rities are not worrying about the mere con-
sumer-—will be protected by the fact that
the railways in this country are State-owned.
It is the rankest fallacy. This being a
meat-exporting country the Trust is not
concerned greatly with the meat until it
reaches the seaboard, and the refrigerating
space is not State-owned. Australia and
Maoriland are facing a common menace
which is always busy, and should not be
overlooked, war or no war. Joint action is
required between the two countries to pro-
tect the Australasian meat export trade
upon which the pastoral and farming indus-
tries now depend largely for their existence.
The best protective steps would be Common-
wealth and Dominion control of an overseas
steamship line and stands in the London
meat market, backed up by co-operative meat
works controlled by stockowners. And such
concerns would require to have a clause
compelling members to supply their stock
to the co-operative works because there is
always a selfish, shortsighted section which
will bite greedily at the inflated price offered
by the Trust with ulterior purpose, and not
give a thought to the squeeze which is com-
ing later when competition is crushed out.
Before it is too late stockowners should
decide whether they are going to stock up
again for, the benefit of a foreign-owned
Beef Trust, or for the benefit of themselves
and Australia.

Stopping Evolution with an Axe.
Evolution gets alonv by a process of selec-
tion, and can’t be hurried up much even with
gunpowder. The expert gardener, using the
natural process to arrive at a new rose or an
improved cauliflower, knows this well enough.
All sorts of trial plants will turn out mere
weeds, but when the desirable thing comes
along it is an easy matter to root up the
weeds and save the precious seedling that is
to nropagate a new race of vegetables. The
eternal thing that is the key of all evolution
is growth. Stop growth and nothing can be
evolved. The same is true in the evolution
of the State. First and before all things a
State is a society of people banded together
to work for the general advantage. If' they
don’t want to work, which means that they
don’t want to eat or be clothed or have
houses or see picture shows, or do any
darned thing, there is no l-eason why there
should be a State; but while there is a State
its central principle is work, exactly as
growth is the central principle of cabbage-
raising and rose-culture. Without work evo-
lution in society can’t achieve anything at
all; with little work evolution can’t achieve
anything much. This is why the strike
as a labor weapon is so futile. Labor,
by the steady process of evolution, can
achieve anything it really wants. It
can’t achieve it all in one bound, because it
doesn’t know exactly what it does want, and
can’t know until the crop of probable wants
reaches something like maturity. But the
selective process can’t fail if the princinle of
work is maintained, and lnbor is absolutely
bound to win. The strike is simply an
attempt to cut off sun and rain from the
whole garden because there is a weed in it
that the gardener doesn’t like.
“Mujik” arrives in Sydney and has a night
out: —
As the pubs sealed up at six there was nothing
for it but to hustle around and look for mental
refreshment somewhere else. Someone suggested the
theatres. Good! Nothing like dramatic art. One
gets away from the atmosphere of booze, sees a
mirror held up to nature, is sharpened by wit, stimu-
lated by refinement and exalted by poetic illusion.
So we fell in somewhere for a start —and sampled
a vaudeville turn that yelled for chloride of lime
and was received with loud giggles. It was as blue
as the empyrean and smelt like a garbage tin. End
of mirror No. 1. Next place: Pictures. Ah! The
mummer muzzled! But it was as hot as devilled
mustard all the same. The theme was eminently
respectable and suitable for pictorial treatment —
merely about the consequences of Sin and getting
rid of them by paying a friendly call to the local
abortionist. Only a depraved taste could see any-
thing in this picture to justify its exhibition, and
only people who are frankly trading upon nastiness
would take the money at the door. Of course the
place was crowded, and outside was suspended a
notice, “House full.’’ There are some few half-civil-
ised states in Yankeeland where films of this kind
are passed for exhibition, and N. S. Wales can be
bracketed with them for distinction in (1) per-
mitting the wretched things to be shown and (2)'
having a public that will take its boots off in the
rush to get in early. Finding no stimulation in this
pruriency, we dashed for the home of the drama
and sat down to hear about —syphilis. “Damaged
Goods” may be worth reading, but to suggest that
it has any merit as a drama is preposterous. The
only “pull” attached to it is that it deals with an
unpleasant subject. That syphilis, or leprosy, or
smallpox is a subject that can be dealt with on
the stage with profit to anyone except the box--
office proprietor is not seriously believed by any'
sober person. If they can be, why not have the*
stage turned into an operating theatre with the*
hero-surgeon arriving in an aeroplane to perform the’
job incidental to ovariotomy or at the critical
moment making a Caesarian section to save the life
of the comedian’s aunt ? Next, as they were opening
a sewer in the next street, we went round and
enjoyed the aroma for half an hour. We got that
free. It was a mistake not to have gone there first.

The allegation is made that in paying
£12,000 for 97 acres of Blue Mountain land,
a wood and iron residence known as Bocling-
ton, and certain equipment and furniture,
the N. S. Wales Division of the Red Cross
Society got rid of £5OOO or £6OOO more than
the property was worth. The statement to
this effect is supported by evidence that has
not been controverted. It runs this way:—
(1) That the buildings on the property stand
valued in the building register of the Shire Council
at £3530.
(2) That six years ago the land was valued by the
Council’s valuator at £450, that against this the-
owner appealed on the ground that the figure was
excessive, and that the valuation was reduced to
£3OO.
(3) That an adjoining 50 acres were purchased at
£5 per acre by the owner of Bodinrton immediately
after he had sold his own property to the Red Cross
at over £3O an acre.
There are further assertions that Bodington
was hawked about Sydney for sale at
£5000; that there is an area of Crown
land close handy which the Red Cross could
have got for nothing by merely opening its
mouth; that a free gift of land was actually
under offer to the society for the purposes
of establishing a home ; that to pay £550 a
room for a sanatorium is on the face of it
open to grave suspicion of being an act of
wild extravagance. Nothing which _ the
society has produced gets past the admitted
facts/ That there was any corruption about
the business need not be suspected for a
moment.' But what appears to be clearly
indicated is that the money was paid away
so carelessly that the responsible persons
are deserving of censure. That they are
honorary officers merely makes the duty of
the critic unpleasant. It does not in any
way relieve them of the Obligation to get
20 shillings’ worth for every pound of the
society’s money wffiich they spend.
When the N. S. Wales Progressive Party
re.alised that the Libs, and Labs, had leaped
out of the pantry window with the bacon, it
pursued the vanishing commodity with the
loud, plaintive howls that were raised by
Robinson Cktjsoe when he was dropped on
a deserted island. Whereupon the hearts of
the escaping raiders were softened, and they
waited until the Progressive Party caught
up in the chase, and let it share the
loot. As the Progressive Party numbered
fully one the adjustment was easy, and
George Beeby went to sleep in the comfort
that is due to a man who makes himself a
party and converts it into a portfolio or
something.

“G.D.M.” on the rights of man: —
In these stirring times of war, the most extra-
ordinary phenomenon to a philosopher of the smoking-
room is the dazzling triumph of wowserism and
the splendid victories of the wowser. The wowsers,
numbering one-tenth of the community, march as an
all-conquering army over our most cherished customs,
practices and habits, be those good or vicious, harm-
ful or innocuous. This band of idol-smashers and
habit-breakers has upset the one very simple principle
which ought to govern the dealings of society with
the individual, viz., that the only reason for con-
trolling or compelling any citizen against his will
is to prevent harm to others. That a citizen’s own
good, whether moral or physical, is in danger does
not and should not justify society in interfering
with him. He cannot rightly be compelled to do or
not do things merely because it will make him
better or happier or richer. Herein lies the whole
history of the struggle between liberty and authority
from immemorial time. Yet in Australia to-day
we behold the spectacle of a noisy minority of
women and old people, organised by cranks, local
preachers, temperance fanatics, aspiring politicians
and religious maniacs, in full charge of the machinery
of government, stopping this custom because it does
not harmonise with their notions of morality, abolish-
ing one habit because they have no taste for it,
and passing laws against another human practice
because it offends their own idea of propriety. And
the strange feature of this widespread irruption of
Puritanism, modernised as wowserism, is that the
majority of the thinking, sensible people submit to
it and try to placate it instead of combining to
uproot the noisome pestilence. Why do we not join
together and smite wowserism in its vitals and
utterly destroy it and wipe it out entirely and for
ever ? For this new crusade is not on behalf of religion
or of church or creed. It is a fearsome assault on
joy, on brightness, on pleasure, on amusement. For
illustration, lam a lover of clubs and clubmen. For
some reason entirely without righteousness, I cannot
drink my usual pint of claret with my dinner. Who
forbids Sammy Mauger, John Vale, Rev.
Worrall, Rev. Woo.dfull, the Victorian Allian<>
the W.C.T.U., Y.W.C.A., Y.M.C.A. and all that
class of people whom I don’t know, don’t like and
never meet. Who endowed them with the right to
stop me going to a picture-show on Sunday? Why-
have they stopped me getting supper, and lager
beer with it, at 11 o’clock at night? What entitles
them to insist that my club guest must not stop
in my club, which is an integral part of my daily-
life, after 9.30 p.m. ? Who gave the wowsers the
right to stop me publishing a newspaper on Sunday?
Nobody. They simply took it, as they have taken
numberless rights and privileges from me and
other law-abiding citizens like me who do not harm
their neighbors. Everywhere we are faced with
rules, by-laws, regulations and restrictions designed
for my good by people I regard as a collective pesti-
lential nuisance and as my inferiors as men and
women. An Anti-Wowser Association on anti-killjoy
lines would have my active support.
The Night We Left Old Anzac.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the haze of the full moon the men filed off
from the trenches to the beaches, and passed away
from Gallipoli, from the unhelped attempt which they
had given their bodies and their blood to make. They
had lost no honor. They were not to blame that
they were creeping off in the dark, like thieves in
the night... .They had failed to take Gallipoli....
but they had fought a battle such as has never been
seen upon this earth. What they had done will
become a glory for ever, wherever the deeds of heroic
unhelped men are honored and pitied and understood.
They went up at the call of duty, with a bright
banner of a battle-cry, against an impregnable fort.
Without guns, without munitions, without help, and
without drink, they climbed the scarp, and held it
by their own glorious manhood, quickened by a word
from their chief. Now they were giving back the
scarp, and going to new adventures, wherever the war
might turn. — John Masefield, in “Gallipoli.”
That night we left old Anzac,
The dim moon overhead
Saw us in silence creeping—
And, close behind us keeping,
The rear-guard of our dead!
For we were leaving Anzac:
They could not understand.
In anguish and in wonder
They broke their graves asunder,
And swift the trenches manned.
Our murdered mates of Anzac
Stared at us in surprise;
We saw beside us standing
Men missing since The Landing,
With mateship in their eyes.
We’d thought them dead in Anzac;
We’d left them to their fate.
Our souls were strangely shaken,
Our hands by cold hands taken;
We heard a whispered “Mate!”
That night in awful Anzac
To me my dead mate spoke:
“We took the hill; they shelled it;
But, blimey, ain’t we held it?
Bill, wot’s the blanky joke ?
“We lie in pride at Anzac ;
We stormed with you this shore!
There’s nothing now may hurt us
But this —that you desert us
Who were your mates, and more!’’
I answered him of Anzac :
.. “We’ve done all that we could.
Not for our orders blame us,
Nor with reproaches shame us.”
My old mate understood.
And sobbing down from Anzac
Came men that once were strong.
How stop the tears from falling
When far we heard them calling
Their sorrowful “So-long!”?
As we stole down from Anzac—
Bleak graveyard choked with Youth—
Why we marched safe we wondered.
How could the Turk have blundered?
Too late we learnt the truth.
For our dead mates of Anzac
Had one more duty yet.
They saw the trench deserted,
And danger swift averted :
They lined the parapet!
And, looking back on Anzac,
We calmed our wild alarms.
Our mates were there, and ready;
With trigger fingers steady
Once more they stood to arms!
Who smote the Turks at Anzac?
What made them halt and blench?
Had Allah sent an omen?
Look ! Grey and grinning foemen
Still held the emptied trench !
From those grim ghosts of Anzac
The foe shrank back in dread.
Not Allah gave them daring
To charge against the staring
Cold eyes of men long dead!
And so for aye at Anzac,
W’hile nations rise and fall,
They stand till God relieves them,
And as His guard receives them
At His last trumpet call!
N.s.w.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
