From somewhere near the inside-
The sudden intrusion of the Governor into the
Holman-Wabe laboratory was something which neither
of the conspirators reckoned on having to encounter
so early in the plot. And, of course, there was much
solemn talk about “constitutional procedure” and
“Ministers refusing to resign” and so on —all of
which was mere rubbish. Nobody was concerned
about the Constitution. Sir Gerald Strickland has
very pronounced ideas about the project for
depriving the people of the franchise, and
when he found the Assembly declining to
express confidence in Ministers, he reckoned it was up
to him to expedite the negotiations for giving the
Crown a Ministry on whose advice it would be proper
for him to act. He had not forgotten that a few
months ago his Ministry resigned for four hours and
forgot to mention it to him until it had rushed back
to its job. And Strickland did expedite things;
but the alleged crisis was smoothed over not by
Holman but by Wade. The sombre Gregory took
Holman by the arm and led him before his Ex. and
explained that he (Wade) was behind the Ministry
and that the Assembly would support the Ministry
which was being hatched. And Strickland, by this
time conscious that he had made a mistake in con-
veying his displeasure to the newspapers (a step
which the bigwigs in London would view with great
disfavor), and having an assurance from the leader
of the principal group in the House that all was
well, smiled on Holman again. Then Wade and
Holman salaamed to a relieved and chastened
Cocked Hat, returned to town and . proceeded
to give further consideration to the question
of billets, while the anxious Libs., whose tongues
have been hanging out, sang hymns of hope,
and disgruntled Labs, hied forth to address the demo-
cracy in the Domain. Parliament’s life will be
prolonged all right, because the people are without
an axe, there are many juicy jobs to be handed
round, and the Crown cannot object to a democratic
institution showing its rottenness if it wants to.
Are Hughes and Pearce engaged in a
bungling competition? First of all, they
heavily handicapped the case submitted to
the jury on October 2S by a general call-
up under the Defence Act. Having seri-
ously damaged the compulsion cause by this
tactical error, they are proceeding now to
make enemies for our home defence system.
Incidentally they are using up a million of
money. For what purpose? The men were
called up to be turned into soldiers in the
event of the Government being granted cer-
tain powers. Those powers have been re-
fused, and the scheme has been abandoned.
Yet the Exemption Tribunals are sitting,
and men are being dragged from their har-
vesting to attend them. The Supreme Courts,
moreover, are hearing appeals, and medical
men are pawing recruits over. Those
who are passed are dragged away
from work to be turned into what
Pearce calls “something like soldiers.”
Everyone else knows that they are being
merely converted into individuals with a
grievance and a grudge against our home-
defence system. The whole business is gro-
tesque, and a revelation of official stupidity
which would be almost beyond belief if such
things had not become familiar during the
past two years of hideous waste.
♦
Australia is at war. So, too, is France.
A Frenchman who was not working for
France to-day would consider himself dis-
graced. A coal-miner who worked to-day for
Australia would consider himself an outcast.
This paper takes the French view, and de-
clares that the stoppage of Australia’s coal
supplies is a disgrace which must be shared,
not only by owners and miners, but by the
Federal and State Governments for their
past betrayal of the arbitration system. It
is a disgrace to Australia as a nation and
a reflection upon her institutions. The
public is getting a long way less than a
fair deal. People who are no parties to
this dispute are being dragged into it and
made to suffer loss and embarrassment. And,
apparently, worse still is to follow. Tnis
page goes to press while a circumstantial
story is in circulation about an intention to
declare coal black —to tie up Australian
industry, that is, from end to end. If put
into effect this will mean that Australian
Labor Unions have gone back to where they
started from and decided to rely upon the
power of the worker to take the community
by the throat and choke out of it whatever
can be obtained. It is possible that the imme-
diate object will be achieved by this means,
but it is going to have very serious after-
results for Labor both in politics and out of
it.
According to G. W. Russell, a member of
the Maoriland Ministry, the characteristics
of the Australian people are due to “the
convict taint.” We might shrug our shoul-
ders at this if it came from Berlin. In the
mouth of an office-hunting politician playing
to the gallery it would perhaps, in normal
times, be entitled merely to contempt. But
as Australian and Maoriland soldiers have
fought and died together on Gallipoli, and
are now brigaded alongside each other in
France, The Bulletin puts it to Jimmy
Allen, the Acting Premier of Maoriland,
whether it is not up to him, as a decent man,
to disown the author of this grossly ignorant
and abominably mischievous commentary
upon the citizens of Australia. If Allen
shirks this obvious duty Australia’s only
conclusion can be that the Government of
Maoriland associates itself with the pot-
house gibes at the Commonwealth’s army
which decorate the German newspapers.
*
According to Garvin, of the London
Observer, who has some very lucid moments,
the Germans cannot be throttled before 1918.
It looks a fairly conservative estimate,
though six months ago its author would have
been prosecuted for doing something “to the
prejud.ee of recruiting” if he had mentioned
it in Australia. He attributes the delay in
the proceedings to muddle in the Balkans.
He may be right, but it is probable that the
main reason for the German not throwing
up the sponge is that he is working as though
he meant business. Meanwhile the inflexi-
ble determination of Australia to get even
with the enemy at the finish is expressed by
a resolute intention to: —
(1) Reinforce the Australian army any old way.
(2) Tie up the Australian transports.
(3) Interfere with the production of tucker.
.(4) Borrow the last shilling to make a super-
fluous monkey-cage with.
All of which shows that when Germany
learns the worst she will be ready to capitu-
late on any terms and admit that she had
misjudged the splendid devotion of Australia
to the cause of social organisation and
national freedom.
The health of N. S. Wales Liberal leader
Wade has been so gravely undermined by the
labor of undergrounu-engineering with Hol-
man that he felt obliged to mention it lately
every time he met a reporter. Wade’s trouble
is of an obscure character which it is thought
by his friends may poss.bly yield under the
influence of a change of climate. The Agency-
General being vacant, Wade’s indisposition
will—unless something goes wrong at the
last moment—be cured bv giving him that
job. This (quite incidentally, of course)
will get rid of what might have proved a
discomfort to the new political crew—the
presence of two skippers, who don’t love one
another much, on the bridge at the same
time.
When politicians fall out you get the
strength of things. The split between Hol-
man and some of his old followers has let
light into the Norton Griffiths infamy. The
Opposition, almost without exception, de-
nounced and voted against the agreement;
and now McGirr, of the Labor party, using
the most violent language about the disgrace-
ful thing, declares that Holman simply flogged
it through the Caucus, and threatened any
Labor member w T ho opposed it with political
extinction. So we have another beautiful
result of machine government: this most
disgraceful agreement foisted for years upon
the people by a handful of men in one party.
This handful carried the party; the party
carried the House. So less than 20
men, who for one reason or another were in
favor of it, were able to carry it in the face
of 50 who were opposed to it. And that is
democratic rule and government by the
majority!
Felt the pulse of his last patient, Dr.
W. G. Maddox, aged 73. Born in Launces-
ton (Tas.), son of a former Dr. Maddox,
for 50 years he has helped the young Laun-
cestonians into the world, and kept the old
ones out of the other as long as possible. He
died at Plymouth (Eng!) while on a visit
to a daughter, wife of Commander Luxmore,
R.N. Just an old-fashioned famly doctor,
fond of fishing and cricket, and a good
friend to all who could not pay big bills.
“Gallery Hand’s” send-off: —-
There are a mort of men in Maoriland’s no-license
crowd that I would sooner have seen dead than Frank
Whitmore Isitt, who passed in his marble at Dunedin
this week. For many years he was one of the big
wheels of the M.L. water-cart, and he ran without the
squeal that characterised the rest of the machinery.
Evidence of this was to be obtained from the fact
that in the early 1900’s there was always a welcome
for him in the Parliament Press Gallery from men
who had little time for narrow-mindedness. I re-
member the first occasion Isitt was on the roster
to buy afternoon tea—a privilege permitted few out-
siders. In deference to his principles, all hands
ordered tea—real tea —but the smiling old tactician
shook his head. “No, no, gentlemen,” he said, “make
it the usual, please.” Frank Isitt arrived in M.L.
from England in 1870, and was in active service on
behalf of John Wesley’s policy for a pile of years ;
but he was best known as vice-president of the anti-
grog Alliance, and as its general secretary, a job
from which he retired a few years ago. He was 70
when he went to where the worm (of the still)
corruptcth not.
The Westralian Liberal Government is
jettisoning its predecessor’s State enterprises
one by one. The sawmills,. which were para-
lysed by the war; the implement works,
which furnished farming tools at below cost
and so closed up competing firms; the
brickworks, which were closed down in de-
spair by the Scaddan Ministry six months
ago—all are now on the market, and nego-
tiations are in progress for disposing of the
State ferries. The meat stalls will probably
be the next to go.

Another musical ghost is just finishing a
brief stroll at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. 1 his
time it is “The Girl in the Train, with
dainty Dorothy Brunton as tne pretty net*
ress who borrows the good young husband s
sleeping berth, and Marie Eaton as the
jealous wife who makes all the trouble. Ine
part gives this ragtime specialist a chance to
sit still and show that she can act, and she
does it quite impressively. The musical
numbers are not numerous enougn to inter-
fere seriously with the story: there aie
only about 14 noisy interruptions and one
solo dance —with a 20-minute drought be-
tween the two acts. Alfred Frith, whose
grin is his fortune, gets 90 per cent, out
of the part of Cornelius Scrop, the sleeping-
car conductor. Fred Maguire has a life-
and-death struggle with his first-act music
and semaphores his emotion. C. H. \\ ork-
man (President Van Eyck) makes up to
look like Parson Frank Harvey and does
a wild burlesque of “Romance” with Field
Fisher (Lucas Van Tromp), hurriedly
wigged and gowned as a scarecrow Cavallini
with singlet and braces showing above his
low-cut gown. Connie Ediss is amusing ■in
the witness-box as 'a bothered Dutch ser-
vant, Madge Elliott soars gracefully through
a dance, and a final word of praise is due
to the whimsical court crier. “So Long,
Letty,” is to start a fortnight’s revival on
Saturday-—if the coal strike doesn’t condemn
the city to darkness.
In the revival of “The Parish Priest”
on Saturday night Allen Doone gave one
of his best pieces of character work, and
the packed audience at Sydney Palace
let him know it thought so, too. The
play is the story of true love saved
from taking the wrong turning by the
sympathy and understanding of Father Allen
Doone. Edna Iveeley made a convincing
little heroine in spite of the flapper-like hair
arrangement; and if she could persuade
Uncle not to give the show away —as to
her age being the same as that of her bril-
liant doctor-lover —all would be well. Tom
Buckley’s Michael Sullivan was as excellent
as Doone’s Father Whalen; and Ethel
Grist shouted the lines of Kathleen Corrigan
while Ethel Bashford did the same for Agnes

“Damaged Goods” will be removed from
Sydney Criterion to-morrow night, though a
short season will follow at the Royal. On
Saturday, the Criterion will see the new
foreign actress, Florence Rockwell, in
another U.S. play, “Common Clay.”
The Follies of Pleasure still hang out
the sign of the Gross at Sydney Opera
House. The audience is pleased with the
well-fed performers, the management with
the audience, and the well-fed performers
with their reception; so everybody’s happy.
Warning to Sydney theatre-goers: Brans-
combe’s Red Dandies, at the Garden, just
over the hump of the William-street hill,
is a clean show.

One more revival of “Silver King’’
seemed appropriate to Cup Night at Mel-
bourne Royal, because the trouble begins
with the downfall of a reckless punter who
drowns his losses iu derrink. The topical
moral lesson drew a full house, and the
performance pleased the crowd. _ All the
same, the cast of the present revival, when
averaged, falls considerably below par. In
the first place, Julius Knight is anything
but a convincing Wilfred Denver, drunk or
sober. His robust, romantic style is out of
sympathy with the character of the intem-
perate husband and father who has “done
it in” on the gees and flies for consolation to
the bottle. It wouldn’t matter so much that
he dresses the part in up-to-date garments
when he returns from the Derby, although
the Wheatsheaf suburban pub, with skittle-
alley attached, was already an old-fashioned
notion when the play was written, and the
tall hat of Daniel Jaikes belongs to a bygone
age. The anachronism would pass if Julius
were the right type of supposititious punter
for the job. However, he steadily improves. He
gets nearer to the truth after he has sobered-
up at the sight of Geoffrey Ware’s corpse,
and as the penitent millionaire back from
America he is quite satisfactory. Miss
Lizette Parkes is mostly unequal to the
trials and tribulations of Mrs. Denver. Her
diction is unsuitably affected, and in the
cottage scene she hardly suggests the suffer-
ing mother who is trying to look pleasant in
the last round of a long contest with bitter
poverty. Athohvood, on the other hand, is
an admirable Jaikes in nearly all respects,
neither too senile nor too servile in his
conception of the angelic old retainer of
the busted house of Denver. Boyd Irwin is
a hard, unpleasant Spider, who couldn’t pos-
sibly have got into a social position to dis-
cover the whereabouts of Lady Blanche
Winter’s diamonds. The largest of the
other good acting characters in “Silver
King” are played effectively or passably by
Leigh, Lloyd. Huntley, Hughes and Eileen
Sparks; and Sweeney makes a worthy attempt
to naturalise the contemptible spitefulness
of G. Ware. The staging is in accordance
with tradition save for one glaring absurd-
ity. Old Jaikes and pretty little Cissie
Denver are each required to speak of “the
only chair” in the cottage where Mrs.
Denver is doing her starving. They empha-
sise the singularity of the chair in the fore-
ground. They feature the piece of furniture.
But on Saturday there was a second chair
obtruding itself on public notice, and each
time the other one was said to be lonely ybu
could hear the audience smile. Perhaps the
stage manager had put the other chair in
for comic relief.

Bright drab patches of naturalism in
•‘While the Billy Boils” will make that
good-tempered Australian fellowdrama fairly
popular at Melbourne King’s. Its sins of
construction are atoned for by its temper,
which is in happy contrast to the irascibility
and peevishness that prevail in the broad
comedy scenes of “Our Selection.” There’s
a lot of human kindness in some of the
episodes that happen outside Mrs. Stiltners
pub. The birth of Joe Wilson’s first-born,
while Joseph waits around and upbraids
himself, is one of the most realistic domestic
events that ever transpired off-stage. And the
main supports of Mrs. Stiffner’s business in
the township of Bedclay are convincing. They
carry conviction with them. After they
have grouped themselves in attitudes of
casual attention to the Salv. Array Band
one feels further persuaded that Australian
drama thus far is very much a matter of
People and very little a matter of Plot.
“The Taming of the Shrew” at Melbourne
Princess’s was doubtless doomed to com-
parative neglect by the fact that Oscar Asche
played it for all it was worth. and knocked
the stuffing of public interest out of it.
Moreover, he played it with the Induction,
which Allan Wilkie omits, lienee the present
revival is discussed as a moderate-sized
goose, somewhat overdone and minus the
apple sauce. But “Hamlet” will be the
immortal bill on Saturday.
At Melbourne Majesty’s they are continu-
ing “The Cinema Star” for yet another week.
This journal has one thing more to say. It
is concerning Clutterbuck’s alleged address
to a meeting at the Albert Hall before the
curtain rises. Clutterbuek complains that
the meeting was composed of kiddies who
laughed joyously when he addressed them as
Fathers and Mothers of England. Subse-
quently it is stated that, he fought like a
lion with an infuriated mob of picture-show
barrackers at the same meeting, and was
undismayed when Minnie Love threw an
onion at him. These conflicting references
to Clutterbuck’s meeting leave a critic per-
plexed ; but, anyhow, he reckons that an
onion flung by Miss Love would come with
all the fragrance of a rose now that she
has straightened her shoulders.

Expectation of a violent change in Mel-
bourne Tivoli programme must remain un-
realised for a few more nights. The Togos,
Cardo and Noll, Mennetti and Sidelli, and
other individual acts previously mentioned in
this journal are still enjoying the freedom
of the Tivoli stage; but the Strollers are
leisurely papking up, and may put out the
camp-fire and shift their sylvan scenery at
any moment. On Saturday the Strollers’
gaiety was exposed in a fresh bill, whereof
one of the features was a pathetic inquiry
from Sidney James’s partner, Billy, concern-
ing the whereabouts of a moke called
Quinologist.
Wirths’ Circps is about to finish its month
in Melbourne. Last Saturday was a great
night in the arena, owing to the recent
occurrence of Melbourne Cup. The winning
jockey-lad faced the music whilst George
Wirth gave him a gold-mounted whip. The
presentation took place with the assistance
of Sasanof Stead and Melba’s brother
Charlie, both of whom offered a few remarks
on the altar of publicity.

The rain is still calling frequently on the
St. Kilda (Vic.) Dandies without damping
the enthusiasm of the little party. Joseph
Brennan continues to be responsible for the
brightest jokes, and receives forcible assist-
ance from Harry de Robeck, who in moments
of tense frivolity finds the aspirate a great
encumbrance. Also, there is a presentable
new comedian, John Campbell, who affects a
monocle like a bad habit, and sounds as if he
could recite Burns in the original. Among
the others, baritone Lawrence is now voicing
the martial sentiments oi' a local composer,
while the flapperish Pearlie McKenzie is
making amorous appeals to blokes in the
audience without being specially bewitching
in her impertinence. The allurement of the
other sex is a fine art in which Pearlie is not
yet knowing enough to take full honors.
Adorers of Theda Bara can ask no more of
their goddess than an hour of her in the
film representation of “East Lynne” at
Hoyt’s, Melbourne, on Saturday. Theda is
a success as Lady Isabel in the first few
chapters, wherein she manages to suppress
her natural tendency to suggest the serpent.
After she bolts with Francis Levison, how-
ever, the snake asserts itself, and she looks

like the usual siren of shady resorts and
supper-crushes. Hoyt’s other long pictures,
“The Weakness of Strength” and “By Whose
Hand,” pale into unimportance beside this
latest impression of Mrs. Carlyle, and even
the first sensational spasm of a new serial,
“The Mysteries of Myra,” is not strong

With the Boyal still on films, and the
King’s closed, the Tivoli has a monopoly of
audible drama in Adelaide. Maggie Moore
continues to reinforce the Anderson co.,
and is open to play just anything, from a
street waif to the mother of a family; and
she is welcome in all she does. The athletic
Chinese have left the Majestic, but are
replaced by a mysterious Wong Toy Sun,
with an outfit of magic. Mplinari sings
finely at this house, and the Panlastos are
funny. It is difficult to pay more than
half a crown at any Adelaide house just
now—barring odd coppers (2Jd. in some
instances), which have to be fished out for
the new amusement tax. John Dempster’s
big Women’s Choir concert lias been fol-
lowed by Winsloe Hall’s fine Adelaide
Orchestra performance. The weather for
outdoor shows is rotten.

Before the Westralian Assembly reassem-
bled after the Referendum poll, men in the
street were predicting that Scaddan would
not get the Opposition leadership. Acting-
leader W. D. Johnson and ex-Mines Minis-
ter Collier were alleged to be after the job.
As the leader of the Other Side in West-
ralia gets £5OO a year for pointing out where
the Government has made a hash of things
it was not altogether improbable that Scad-
dan would get a run for the money; but
whatever happened in Caucus, tolerably-
Happy Jack was announced on the meeting
of the House as the unanimous choice of the
party. The late Labor Premier disappointed
his admirers by his feeble twisting in the
Referendum campaign, presenting himself
as a sort of pro-and con scriptionist; never-
theless he is undoubtedly the strongest
debater of his crowd.
AT POVERTY POINT.
J. J. Ennis, actor, has just died in Mel-
bourne Hospital. For many years he was
a member of the Taylor-Carrington Dramatic
Co., and travelled every part of Australia.
For some time he stage-managed for Maggie
Moore, and at one period was a member of
William Anderson’s team. Later, he played
character and old men’s parts with George
Marlow. Plow old he was may be guessed
from the fact that he got his theatrical tuition
from Hoskins.

with the cleverness shown by the adaptor of
his stories for stage purposes. Well, Henry
is an amiable poet, and wonderfully easily
pleased. He reminds me of the man who,
having been knocked off St. Kilda pier with
a blow in the face from a stale skate, ex-
pressed his appreciation of the action of liis
assailant in helping him to a pleasant bath.
Moreover, much of the so-called criticism of
“While the Billy Boils” is written in the
same generous spirit. But the point at which
forbearance ceases to be a virtue is passed
a mile, and I assert that the treatment
meted out to Lawson’s characters is of the
n , a^.u^ a criminal libel, and should call
shrieks of protest from every man who has
a ha porth of regard for Henry’s people.
lan Maclaren s Bob Brothers may pass, but
the rest, without exception, are gross carica-
tures of bush-bred men and women. I
am moved to write with some heat because I
clearly see that unless there is a marked
improvement in the dramatic handling of our
literary material the mucli-longed-for Aus-
tralian play is going to effect nothing beyond
the utter degradation of Australian fiction.

O.K. : Old Melbourne memories of the
Williamson. Garner and Musgrove days are
revived by a Town Hall concert appearance
of Ida Osborne. She has been kindly-enough
treated by woman’s bitterest foe. The voice
is “on the go” as regards quality and range,
hut the enunciation is clear and incisive as
ever. Ida Osborne belongs to the old school
of Australian stage warblers who left none
of their words to the imagination.. When she
utters a ballad j t ou Know what it is all about,
The intonation waits unon the diction,
whereas a singer of the Ethel Cadman type
seems to trill her lay with her mind fixed on
the musical effect. Ida Osborne’s tone is
nasal as compared with, say, Florence
Young’s direct sinking, but the phrasing is
enually honest in both cases. Artists of their
style mark the difference between singing and
“vocalism ol ”

T. 0.1. : Hied, at So, Agnes Kelly Robert-
son, widow of Dion Boucicault. This great
actress was born in Edinburgh on Christmas
Hay, 1833, a daughter of Thomas Robert-
son, art publisher. She first appeared as a
child singer at concerts in Aberdeen..Atlo
she made her debut in that town in ‘ Spoiled
Child.” Next she went to Manchester, where
she divided child-parts with Marie V> iit°n
(Lady Bancroft now), and also played with
Fanny Kemble and W. C. Macready. At

Edward Glover at Glasgow. She met
Bion Boucicault ill 1850, while sin vas vith
the; Keans, and soon afterwards married him.
It was to see Boucicault and Agnes Robert
son nluy m The 5 umpire that Queen V IC-
toria went three times in a fortnight .and
gave a commission to Corbould to paint the
picture of the pair athey appeared in the
piece. Later, she joined i<u amt , s '
and Charles Mathews at the Lyceum. In
1853 she went to the United States; her

years. With her husband she returned to
England in 1809, and for several yeais
appeared in Ills most popular play's. Alter a
long absence from the stage she re-appeared
at tile Opera Comique in 1889 for the
benefit of J. A. Cave. Pier last important
appearance was as Mrs. Cregan in “The Col-
leen Bawn” (1896), since when she has
lived in retirement. Dion (Dot), Nina and
Aubrey are her children ; all have had good
stage careers. In 1885 the Dions, father and
son, came to Australia; at the end of the
tour “Dot” decided to remain, and entered
into partnership with Robert, Brough. This
association lasted 10 years.

MAUD'S ART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She used to scrub the hash-house stairs,
ti, ,ii, . „
And clean ones bluchers for a “tray.
Despite the strain of kitchen cares
Her little heart was always gay;
01 , . , ,
She sang her songs the livelong day.
Her hair was ever in her eyes,
And poor and shabby were her clo’es;
And frankly and without disguise
She sported freckles on her nose,
And holes, too, in her cotton hose.
They paid twelve shillings every week
To Maud. It was a handsome wage
For one considered half a freak,
Till Rillson said: “She’d be the rage
With just that Stunt upon the stage.”
They then inveigled Maud away.
I saw her but a week ago
In her accustomed quaint array,
And singing her old songs just so—
Now as a “feature” in a show.
She polished boots in make-believe,
She scrubbed, but now made nothing clean,
She trailed her nose upon her sleeve,
And sang and chattered in between.
Of comedy we call her Queen.
Now forty pounds a week she’s paid,
And Maud when off is voted smart,

She eall.d it work, she calls it art.

GOOD NEWS FOR STOUT PEOPLE.
RAPID FAT REDUCTION WITHOUT
EXERCISES OR STARVATION DIETS.
“One great objection to exercises for fat
reduction.” says a noted Health Scientist,
“is the after exhaustion and discomfort they
generally cause those who adopt this method
of becoming slender. Yet superfluous fat
can be rapidly and surprisingly reduced in a
more simple way. Get a dram of quassia
chips and three ounces of cirola-bark extract
from any chemist. Put the quassia chips in
a pan and pour a cupful of boiling water
over them; then, after they have stood for a
minute, strain the liquid through a cloth
and add the cirola-bark extract. Rub this
lotion into the fatty parts of the body, hips,
abdomen, chin or wherever excessive fat
exists, for about ten minutes night and
morning by a circular movement of the
hand. As the preparation is absorbed by
the skin it dissolves the fat tissues under-
neath, at the same time tightening the
loosened skin, and leaving it beautifully
smooth and firm.” — Advt.

SOCIETY
“ J.G.”: The just-dead Alisou Smith may
have produced one or two fiue types of
engines for the Vic. Railways, but there
were some howling failures to his account
as well. He designed the only real buck-
jumping loco. I ever helped to handle. The
free circus turn was given in Armstrong-
street, Ballarat, on the bit of line leading
from the Phoenix Foundry to the railway
station, about half a mile away. It was a
new type of engine which had caused a lot
of discussion amongst mechanical engineers,
so there was a big crowd outside the
foundry to see the juggernaut make a start.
It was coaxed into the street successfully,
but the fun started when steam was turned
on to get to the station. The brute ad-
vanced about a yard, then gave a terrific
buck, and ploughed up a few yards of the
street with its snout. Road metal and sand
flew in all directions, and so did the on-
lookers. The shaken driver shut off power
and the loco, righted itself again. After a
pause steam was turned on once more, and
again the buckjumper snorted and tore up
some more of the street. A third try gave
the same result, and the steam peril was
coaxed back into the foundry and the doors
banged defiantly at the crowd. The engine
was balanced so near the front that the
pressure of steam when it went into the
cylinder was just sufficient to make the beast
stand on its head.
A letter from brother Billjim of the Light
Horse:—
The Bedouin women have many charms and graces,
but truth remains at the bottom of their deepest
wells. After a search party, out for Turks or
arms, has done its duty by a village, a long pro-
cession of women, headed by the oldest and ugliest,
and tailed by the young and lovely, makes its way
to the Chief Officer’s quarters, where to his embar-
rassed subordinates a tragic list of offences is charged
against the searchers. Mostly the complaints concern
the loss of heirlooms of priceless worth, jewels which
would make the splendors of the Arabian Night 3
tales seem like a rush candle against the moon.
The Billjirns are rounded up to hear the charges, and
the interpreter is kept busy with denials and counter
charges for hours. In the end some young thing,
after much adroit cross-questioning, will suddenly
remember that she knows where the missing treasures
are. These turn out to be brass trinkets—rings,
anklets, armlets—or cheap combs and strings of
glass beads. Occasionally the collection will in-
clude some silver bangles, in which case the owner
gives herself the airs of (kesar’s old pal Cleopatra.

“Wowser Willy” from Salisbury Plain: —
We overheard one English Tommy talking, to
another at a picture-show last week. We were just
behind them and heard the one telling the other,
who was evidently a new chum, all about the place.
The newchum asked about the Australians. “Oh,
they’re up on top of the hill,” the old head said.
■‘You can’t miss them. They all wear wristlet
watches and gold teeth, and, instead of calling
each other ‘chum’ like we do, they call each other” —
well, say deacons.

From a Billjim’s letter to his lady :—•
Last week I went to Buckingham Palace for my
Military Cross. First I got a telegram ordering
me to be in attendance at 10.15 on Tuesday morning,
so I turned up resplendent with polished buttons,
brilliant long-boots, spurs and a borrowed sword—-
the last causing me a deal of anxiety. I took a
taxi to the Palace. As soon as I arrived, to my
great relief, the sword was taken from me and I
was put into a large room with about a dozen
others who were also to receive decorations. We
were lined up, and as I was the second senior I
was given the second place. Then a colonel came
and told us exactly what we were to do. He was
followed by a man who stuck a sort of meatliook
into each man’s coat just over the left-hand top
pocket. After the usual delay a door was opened
in the end of the room and we had to go in one at
a time. The King was standing, dressed in field-
marshal’s uniform, in the middle of a second room
and rather to the left. I had been told to walk
straight ahead into this room until I was level with
the King, then turn to my left, bow and advance
two paces towards him. I did it all quite success-
fully. Then the King stepped forward and, taking
the medal from a cushion on which it was handed
to him, placed it on the hook on my chest. He
said, “I congratulate you,” and shook hands with
me. After that I had to take two paces to the rear,
bow again and leave the room. As soon as I was
outside a flunkey removed the medal and the hook,
put the medal in a case and handed it to me.
Then came the part which I had funked most —leav-
ing the Palace and fighting through a crowd of
people and press photographers. Outside the Palace
itself there is a large courtyard into which the
“WHERE ARE MY CHILDREN?”
people are not allowed. Luckily as I came from
the Palace entrance I saw a littie side-gate out of
this courtyard with no crowd round it. Through
this I bolted and had the luck to find a taxi just
outside. I hopped in and fled.

“J.F.H.” (London) : Some patriotic Aus-
tralian ought really to move in the High
Court for an injunction to restrain the
Bishop of Kalgoorlie from persistently bring-
ing the Commonwealth into contempt and
derision. He has issued yet another
“appeal” here for the
coppers of Cockneys.
“Our financial posi-
tion has become in-
creasingly critical,” he
proclaims to the wide
world. He talks of the
“fast diminishing pop-
ulation of Westralia,”
deplores the “dying
townships” in his vast
diocese of 300,000
square miles, and pro-
ceeds to give the de-
tails of his episcopal
bankruptcy. When
Kalgoorlie separated
from Perth and went
into the business of
bishing on its ' own
account it got £9493 to
start with. That en-
dowment fund yields
an annual income of
£5OO, which is all the
diocese of Kalgoorlie
Can rely upon. But the
minimum figure on
which it can be run is
£l5OO a year. So, in
order to maintain an
unwanted bishopric,
the poor of London
are badgered by
female collectors
called the Kalgoorlie
Diocesan Association.
“The stipends -paid to
the clergy on the
Goldfields are in seve-
ral cases not a living
wage,” he adds, “and
a further difficulty is
that half of the 31
churches in the dio
cese are in need of
urgent repairs.” What
a proclamation for an
Australian prelate to
make in the world’s metropolis!

The old Treasury Hotel, Queen-street,
Melbourne, is going the way of all de-
licensed houses at the end of the year and
taking its retiring editor, Tom Flanagan,
with it. Tom’s father,
Michael, who reigned
there before him, is
remembered as a pro-
found patriot of the
Ould Sod. Every 17tli
of March he kept a
tray of nice fresh
shamrocks on the
counter for the bhoys.
Early one Patrick’s
Day, Michael, was dis-
mayed, on re-entering
the bar after a brief
absence, to find the
tray cleared of the
precious leaves. Among
the few callers at the
time was a big Ger-
man customer, and on
the calamity being in-
vestigated it was
learned that Otto was
the perpetrator of the
atrocity. The vora-
cious Hun blandly
confessed to the furi-
ous patriot that he
had mistaken the
sacred grass for watercress.
* * * *
Melbourne, not to mention other business
centres in Victoria, gazed at its regulation
Cup Day holiday with a glum, inquiring
stare. The voice of the people asked, under
its breath: Why is this thus? All other
annual public holidays carry their meaning
on the face of them. Good Friday, Christ-
mas Day, January 1, King’s Birthday—they
commemorate actual facts. They are fixed
items in the to keep holy or
get drunk upon. But the Government
Gazette which declared November 7 to be
Melbourne Cup Day was an obvious liar, and
the pomilace wore the hangdog expression of
one who has been forced to take part in a
notorious fraud. Everything that happened
was occurring under false pretences. Hum-
bug was rampant in the land. Theatre
matinees, Sunday-school picnics—every sort
of wild festivity arranged for Cup Day was
duly carried out except the races at Flem-
ington. The entire excuse for the public
holiday had vanished. The dismal merry-
makers in the streets felt as though they
wei’e attending a funeral in the unavoidable
absence of the corpse.

“Hamer”: Corking the public bottle at
sundown has not made the drunk a mere
memory in Sydney. The police-court statistics
have gone joyfully on their way, 61 persons
being prosecuted in one court on a single day
recently. The sly-groggeries are doing a
brisk business, and, although the police secure
many convictions, they rarely capture
the principals. The real work is done by
runners, men and women, who seek out the
thirsty ones and volunteer to secure the stuff;
but they cancel the contract if the customer
attempts to follow them to the place where
the bottled treasure lies. So, when a runner
is caught, the sly-groggery is not disrupted.
Cold Tea, where is thy victory 1
* * * • *
One of the most tender blossoms in the
public service, in regard to criticism, is
Frank Tate, Director of Education. Every
now and again sombeody declares that the
State schools are understaffed, or ill-kept, or
that they do the thing better somewhere else.
Forthwith the Director shows signs of acute
discomfort, protesting that his schools are
not understaffed, that £47 9s. 2d. was spent
on repairs last year, and that Victoria’s is
absolutely the best system in Australia.. Yet
an earnest critic is hardly to be satisfied
with such simple statements. One of the
latest strictures to annoy the Director was to
the effect that “frills and fads” and an
excess of subjects load the time-table so that
it sags down behind. The mere denial of
such an allegation gets the Department
nowhere. What the injured Tate should do
is to explain exactly why he authorises the
pursuit of various studies which have been
added to the curriculum in recent years,
while an immense proportion of State
scholars continue to go through their com-
pulsory term without learning to write and
speak plain English.

“Murkah”: I agree with “Condenser”
(B. 28/9/Y6) that for wireless telegraph
detectors galena is the daddy of all crystal
receivers. But the Yankee has lately intro-
duced an improvement on a British idea
that makes galena, with its 4000-mile per-
formance, look like a deaf and dumb asylum.
Signals that are inaudible on the best crystal
are amplified so as to be easily read by the
senso-audion. Remarks from stations a
mere 4000 miles away come in with a roar
like a fast train. Its best performance to
date is about 9000 miles.
THE WAY OF THE STARS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It has been suggested that, in view of the need
of energetic repopulating after the war, not. only
should bachelors be taxed, but there should be given
a direct incentive to the acquirement of progeny by
making a man’s advancement depend on the number
of his children.
The law of Providence is just:
Oh, Heaven forbid that I should doubt!
I can but bow and take on trust
The way my lot is portioned out.
My brother Algernon and I
In adolescent years designed
The world’s temptations to defy
And in good works our solace find.
We kept our theologic terms
At the same university,
And lived the lives of bookish worms
Through sunshine and . adversity.
We were ordained the sdlf-same day;
Our pass percentages were level:
It looked odds on that we would play
An equal havoc with the Devil.
And for some years we surely did :
Our score of salvaged souls all square,
We prospered evenly amid
The smiles and favors of the fair.
At swarries and at parish teas
We practised affability
Precisely in the same degrees;
We balanced in humility.
Our incomes coinciding, too,
Each drew a like amount per year.
Deciding the same night to woo,
Each whispered to a chosen ear.
At the same altar we were wed ;
One organ played our wedding march;
Contemporaneous prayers were said
With equiponderating starch.
On coextensive honeymoons,
Coequally felicitous,
We basked, and in the afternoons
Like people came to visit us.
And then —for who shall say what sins?—
His way and mine no more commingle,
For Mrs. Algernon had twins;
My wife could but achieve a single.
Persisted as the years ensued
This curious disparity—
With Algernon a growing brood,
With me increase a rarity.
His ran to twelve; mine stopped at four;
Pray as I might, no blessings came;
On him they showered more and more
And his became an honored name.
I to my vicarage adhered,
Not much by those in power accounted,
While he, increasingly revered,
The hierophantic ladder mounted.
The law of Providence is right
And still for worth and merit caters;
My vicarage must hold me tight—
I envy Alg. his bishop’s gaiters.
N.S:W.

Governor Stanley has taken it all back and
apologised. Some time ago he suggested that
perhaps the wattle wasn’t the decorative
vegetable this country might wish its best-
advertised native flower to be. Ever since
then a multitude of Arts-and-Orafters have
been busy working the blossom up into fire-
screens and candlesticks and ceilings and
chinaware and kettle holders: and the other
day they showed his Ex. the accumulated
results of their labors, whereupon Sir
Arthur gave three hasty cheers and ex-
pressed his penitence. Still, the wattle is a
difficult flower to deal with in most
mediums, and it seems likely enough that the
0.C., Government House, got his original
impressions thereof from the plaques, hand-
painted china and one-dollar water-colors
seen at the bazaar-openings of his early Vic-
torian career.
* ‘ * *
“Pink Tape’s” guffaw :
N. S. Wales Public Service has been enjoying a
rumpus which commenced in the Auditor-General’s
office and ended with Gregory Wade in the soup, as
usual. It appears that the A.-G. made some remarks
about the Irrigation Commission which came to the
ears of Wade. He ’ jumped at an opportunity to
slang the Government, and the astute Holman agreed
to appoint a Commission to ascertain if the Trust
was monkeying with public tenders. Then Wade
made some inquiries and- found out that he was
the victim of a misunderstanding. Whereupon he
faded away and refused to attend the Commission, or
have anything to do with it, or even pay for the
unnecessary expense he had caused. The Commis-
sioner’s finding was a complete exoneration of the
Irrigation Commission, and he sounded the loud
timbrel of praise for the staff and its methods. He
did not adversely comment on the Auditor-General,
his staff and their methods, but his finding must
carry a hint to that effect. There is unholy rejoicing
in many of the departments in consequence, as the
Audit office succeeds in getting on the nerves of all
hands—and perhaps wouldn’t be much of an Audit
office if it didn’t.
* * * *
There was a queer tangle in Maoriland the
other day. At Blenheim last June a ease
was to have been heard in which It. W.
Young claimed £lOOO from H. A. Munro for
assault. Before it came on Munro paid
£175 into court, and denied liability. On
the day of the hearing the defendant com-
mitted suicide; and then plaintiff notified
defendant’s solicitor that he agreed to
accept the amount paid into court in
settlement. But the question arose
whether it wasn’t then too late for
him to do it. There was profound
argument, of course, ended by the
Chief Justice holding that the de-
fendant’s death abated the action. So
the defendant’s widow gets the cash
back.

“Lantana”: Is it brotherly love or just
insanitary carelessness? During Referendum
week I attended a meeting at Bondi Junc-
tion (Sydney). There was only one tum-
bler (to drink out of), and six of the eight
speakers, after finishing their turns, drank
from it without as much as swilling it out!
The chief offender was the chairman—an
M.D. He actually poured out the liquid for
the orators.
.** * *
Hobart Gaol is still the same casual, easy
old place to get away from. George Hudson,
sentenced to death in 1908 for murder (the
sentence was commuted into a “lifer” at the
last moment), calmly walked out the other
day. Good conduct had earned him con-
siderable freedom, so nobedy was surprised
when he wandered into the Deputy-Gover-
nor’s quarters, borrowed a civilian suit, and
strolled into the street. He is still strolling.
A little while ago two other prisoners
got out in a similar fashion. Down in
Hobart only panicky fools advertise their
attempts by scaling walls.
The Parliamentary perk had an unlooked
fo’’ advt, during- the V.It.C. Spring meeting.
Alfred Billson began it with a few remarks
in the Assembly as to V.R.C. admission-fees,
which drew forth a reminder from chairman
L. K. S. Mackinnon that every Parliamen-
tarian was sent a pass for himself and his
wife, etc. That, apparently, set the Com-
mittee inquiring; and presently it announced
the withdrawal of members’ tickets and
the adoption of a new procedure—the
admission of members by the exhibition of
their gold railway passes. Perks are perks;
but apparently the Parliamentarian has his
own ideas as to the disposal of the trifles,
such as passing along the concession to a
pal when he can’t use it himself, with con-
sequent damage to the patriotic funds, which
get the benefit of money taken at the gates.
The new edict ought to put a damper on
that performance.

attractive than the one the authorities have
designed. They want to arrest attention, if
nothing else.
A poor strategist who
had never competed in an
egg-and-spoon race or
served his time as a waiter
walked into a Sydney shop
last week, dived at a 201 b.
dish of sausages and fled
with the lot. Anybody
who has ever tried to
hurry with a laden dish
will feel as sorry for him
as he probably did for him-
self when he got fairly
started. However, there
was no going back and
starting afresh, so he con-
tinued to spurn the pave-
ment with his feet and
keep his eyes fixed on the
far horizou. A zealous and
fleet footed assistant with
the unusual name of
Dewberry started in pur-
suit and the Hun in front
began to gallop. Sausages
began to leap from the
shaking dish like new
Spring grasshoppers, till
the blindest bloodhound
could have followed his
trail. Meanwhile the venge-
ful Dewberry was storming
along gallantly like Beatty
in the Lion. The despe-
rate thief in front then
cast the accursed dish and
the remainder of the
sausages from ' him and
escaped down a lane which
may be called Kiel Canal.
Next time he will finesse a
bit and get the sausages compactly wrapped
before he flies with them.

“Binnacle”: Well into the third year of
the war, after a series of explosions and fires
on shipboard and a few founderings, some
Kip Van Winkle has suddenly awakened, and
now it is an offence for a master or owner to
employ a person of*enemy origin on a vessel.
Naturalisation is no excuse. It may seem
incredible that up till recently (and maybe
now) three steamers trading out of Sydney
were in command of full-blooded German-
born skippers. The trouble will be to find
out who are aliens. Fifty per cent, of the
coastal men are “Dutchmen,” but while
before the war a large number were ad-
mittedly Germans, now they are all Danes,
Swedes, Finns or Norwegians. None have
birth certificates, and if you ask one of them
to get a copy (cost, 25.) he will take it as
a personal affront. A lot of experience has
taught me that if the foreign seamen on the
coast are to be required to prove their birth-
place there will be much fun for somebody.

“St. Jimmy”: Billjim, hungry in France,
wanted scrambled eggs at the coffee-stall, but
h ; s French wps not equal to it, so he and
the bright-eyed waitress put their heads
together over his pocket-dictionary. Pre-
sently he made a shot at it by indicating
“Avancer avec l’aide des pieds et des mains”
(to go forward with the help of the hands
and feet—the nearest he could get to scram-
ble). The girl for some reason concluded
that a chick in the shell was what was re-
quired, and politely regretted that the dread-
ful article was not in stock. However, Bill-
gim was a resourceful and determined Aus-
tralian, so he called for a pot and asked for
the eggs (for which he had the French) and
did the scrambling himself.
“Lyndal Wood”: Miss Corabelle Toorak
entered the common tram-car clad in a real
fur coat and wedged herself between me and
Artillery Bill, who was at the mellow stage.
Just then the conductor came along and
intoned “Faresspleess!” Corabelle started to
explore all the gilded ironmongery she had—
gold bag, cigarette-case, lip-salve and vanity
box, but did not seem to be able to locate the
coppers she was searching for. Then Bill
thought he sized up the trouble, and handed
over three coppers unnoticed to the con-
ductor with a careless jerk of the thumb
towards Corabelle. The conductor took the
fare, punched a trip-slip and walked down
the car. “Oh, here is my fare!” called
Corabelle as she held up a ticket she had
discovered in her matchbox. “You have been
paid for,” said the conductor. “By whom?"
she asked in her best Government House
dinner voice. “7 paid for yer,” said Bill in
a big-brotherly way. “I know wot you
wimmen are, leavin’ yer purses be’ind yer, on
the copper, or the sink, or the mangle after
yer been payin’ the Chow or the grocer
or” By this time Corabelle was dull
purple in the face and her eyes were flashing
danger signals. “Take my fare!” she com-
manded in a staccato vibrato. “We only
charge one fare for one person,” said the
conductor brightly. Then the suburban
princess jerked the bell-strap and brought
the car to such a sudden stop we all roiled
sideways on the seats. As she flounced out
she flung the ticket at Bill. But it fluttered
out of the door on to the road, and Bill re-
marked scornfully, “I might er saved me
thruppence!”

IDYLL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We could not frolic ’mid the heather,
Because there was no heather there;
Instead, we lolled in golden weather
Among the scrub, and you were fair.
I loved you at first sight, and you
Loved me straightway. What could we do,
Since joy was keen and fate was kind
And we had left the world behind?
We fibbed and kissed (do you remember?),
And then we kissed and fibbed again.
You’d be eighteen the next September,
And lived with Ma and sister Jane
At Darling Point. I said that I
Was twenty-six; that’s ten years shy.
And well you knew (you rascal Kate!)
I knew you’d done with twenty-eight.
You didn’t, and you guessed I’d guessed it,
Live down at Darling Point with Ma
And Jane, you rogue! As for the rest, it
Mattered but little. Better far
To kiss away the afternoon
Than swap hard facts that stale so soon.
The man's at best a graceless lout
Who spies a lady’s secrets out.
And thus at set of sun we parted,
To meet again we knew not where.
We didn’t whimper broken-hearted,
Or sob aloud, or tear our hair.
We’d had, let pain come late or soon,
A very happy afternoon.
Maybe we missed a lot of pain
Because we never met again.
N.S.W.
ELLERSLIE GREY.

the lower animals only through accidents
of evolution.— Von Gumboozle.

—and hold musical evenings
in trees.

for dinner because he
stopped to bark at a
sparrow.
And Percy and Mabel driving the neighbors mad at 3 a.m.
This might have been a common sight
Think of father get-
ting up in the night to
hay at the moon.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Some people may call this a drab old
world, but it’s been all Hughes lately. Fad-
ing Hughes, mostly.
Ormond (Melb.) and Trinity (Cam-
bridge) can gild the laurels they put against
the name of Lieut.-Professor S. Bruce
McLaren, who was mortally wounded in
France on July 26, whilst making an
attempt with two others to put out a fire
that threatened to explode a pitful of bombs.
The first attempt proving unsuccessful, he
tried again single-handed, was hit once more
and had to be taken away on a stretcher
towards the dressing station. On the way
the bearers put down the stretcher for a
rest, whereupon McLaren struggled to his
feet saying that he was too heavy and
would walk. But, game as he was, he
couldn’t manage it. He died in hospital on
August 13 of the original head wound. The
fine brain that a German bullet stopped had
been engaged on “recasting the whole of
the physics of matter and ether.” The
hideous waste of war !
ASKING TOO MUCH.
WAITER : “Alas, Monsieur, the tear”
BILLJIM : “Yairs, I know all about the war;
but, ’struth, you don’t expect me to pay
the Whole bloomin’ indemnity, do yer?”
Still another Bulletin art-contributor has
gone to the Front, so the list now includes
(Julies, Ilartt, Brown, Sommers, Mattinson,
IP-oth, Townshend and Elmes- —the last the
latest to go. Incidentally, The Bulletin
last week made its one and only application
to hi'v • a man exempted from military ser-
vice for the present. David Low is the solitary
unmarried man on The Bulletin’s literary
"i’d artistic staff who is not at the Front;
■ d because of the large number of married
men who have also gone, it did not seem nos-
sible to suddenly fill the gap that Low’s de-
parture would make, nor did it seem from
the public point of view desirable that his
recruiting pen should be lost. Dwyer, P.M.,
of Melbourne, thought so too.
The German professor, Alfred Manes, who
whooped last week in the Lokal Anzeiger
over the Referendum, was in Australia in
15)09 and published his opinion of us in
the following year. His book, “Ins Land
der Sozialen Wunder,” should have been
worth translating, but the British have never
bothered about foreign criticism. Manes
met Deakin and Fisher: “both fine types,”
he said, “without the stiffness of English
politicians.” He thought Australia and
Maoriland were paradises for the working
man, who ran the Government and there-
fore worked at a slower rate, ate more meat
and had fewer children than their fellows in
Europe. “Nowhere has the citizen more
extensive rights and fewer obligations....
Australasians are admirers of their own
countries, yet lack true patriotism, for patri-
otism must be judged by the sacrifice one is
prepared to make for the Fatherland.” Our
national enthusiasm was manifested in sport,
and he deplored our lack of interest in science
or art. Above all, we were a mere handful
of people in a continent which could hold
20 millions, surrounded by perils, without a
past and without a future! The learned
pessimist must have been surprised when the
meat-eaters from Australasia showed up so
well, at Gallipoli and Pozieres. But the
million-odd who voted “No” show that he
was right to that extent about Australian
short-sightedness.
Died last week, at Broken Hill, Dr. L. L.
Seabrook, who had been resident-surgeon at
the local hospital for 15 years, and his father
was there before him. Dr. T.E.F., the father,
was the first lecturer at the Adelaide Medi-
cal School. He knew his job thoroughly—all
except about sending in the bill. So he found
himself picked up by friends and sent off
into the wilderness to be the mine doctor
when the Broken Hill Prop. Co. was born;
and father and son —the former died a few
years ago—have been mending the splin-
tered miner in all the 30 years since.
Tommy Jessep, the banana-dealer who
persuaded poor old Parkes that he really
was politically defunct, died in Sydney last
week. After the “new Liberals” had defi-
nitely arrived, and the Labor party had
turned him down, Parkes ruled off the ledger
and wrote his autobiography. But he
couldn’t keep out of politics, and in ’95, on
the death of Angus Cameron, he asked
Waverley to give him another life. The
cemetery suburb, however, knew more about
burials than resurrections, and Tommy
Jessep came in a winner on the tale of the
Yellow Pup. He wasn’t much of a poli-
tician, but he made a great success of the
Fruit Exchange, with which he was con-
nected till he died. He had 12 years in
Parliament.
Mrs. William Crick, who died at Rand-
wick last week, aged 84, was mother of
Meagher’s old partner, W.P., the brainiest
and worst-ballasted politician who ever
sailed into the N. S. Wales Lands Depart-
ment —and the Minister’s room there' has
been the refuge of brainy and badly-bal-
lasted men for 40 years past.
Engineering circles in Sydney lost an old
identity through the passing out of James
Blackwood, aged S 6. He came from Glas-
gow 53 years ago with Captain Gilmour, in
the steamer Alexandria, representing the
builders, Blackwood and Gordon, of Port
Glasgow. The vessel was sold to the old
A.S.N. Co., whose service Blackwood joined.
In the next 20 years he superintended the
building of 16 steamers, including the
Wotonga, Ranelagh, Cintra, Quairaing,
Rockton and Cahors. Some time in the
’eighties he retired from his A.S.N. job and
went into business, where he stuck.
Johnston, M.L.A., of Westralia, now an
Independent but once a follower of Happy
Jack Scaddan, described his ex-chief last
week as “a political gastropod,” explaining
carefully that a gastropod was an animal
without brains or backbone that crawled on
its stomach. Scaddan at once rose and
(quite unnecessarily) urged him to “say it
outside.” Of course it will be said outside.
It is one of those carefully-barbed “im-
promptus” that are repeated outside until
everybody is sick to death of hearing them.
When the claims of the Federated Clerks’
Union come before the Court of Industrial
Appeal the Union will be represented in Mel-
bourne by a woman, Miss Rose Smith. She
is a brainy girl who was a State school
teacher before taking up quill-driving, and
she can be relied upon to put up a good fight
for equal pay for equal work.
“Te Whare”: John Williams, an erst-
while Crown Solicitor of N. S. Wales, joined
to a caustic wit a marked preference for
comfortable old garments. To him once
said Julian Salomons, who at a very early
stage of his brilliant career earned bread
and butter in the shop of a pawnbroker:
“Really, Williams, the Bar will have to
subscribe and get you a new suit.”
“Thanks.” replied the astute “Crown” —
“much obliged to you. But. Salomons, what
an eye you have for old clothes !”
“Fitzwilliam”: 1 hate to give the Hun
anv credit just now, but Torrens was not
the originator of his system of land regis-
tration, and never claimed to be. He got it
from Germany, where in Bavaria it had
been used for 600 years and in Prussia for
60. My authority is Torrens himself —in a
statement made before a British Parlia-
mentary Commission that was considering
the introduction of the system some 50 years
ago. Torrens afterwards explained his views
and the history of the system in an essay
published in one of the English reviews.
There’s a copy in Sydney Public Library,
where I read it many years ago.
HE WOULD RISK THE WORST.
“What! Want to marry my daughter?
You’d he doing better if you went to the
Front.”
“Do you think so. sir? I’m — er —willing
to take the risk, sir.”
Apparently it was only in peaceful S. Aus.
that the leader of the No Conscription party
argued the matter with his fists. The row
was between him and Goode, local Minister
for Lands. Goode describes the bout this
way:— ■
I turned to Lionel Hill and said: Privately you
have expressed yourself in favor of conscription, but
you have sold your soul for a seat. He thereupon
called me a liar. I shook my fist in his face and
cautioned him not to repeat the charge. Without
any warning he struck at me.
To which Hill naturally replies that a
gentleman who shakes his fist in another
gentleman’s face must be pretty unsophisti-
cated if he expects to be warned that he is
liable to have his own nose punched.
Hardworking Judge Russell, who looks after
Insolvency, Taxation and everything just
below Supreme Court importance in S. Aus.,
has been in bed since July, with neuritis.
It is the first real holiday he has taken for
10 years.
Alfred Searcy, Auelaide’s authority on old-
time conditions in the Northern Territory,
has just heard from Russia that the transla-
tion of his gossipy book, “By Flood and
Field,” is to be published at Christmas. The
translating was done before the war, but
Petrograd has not been able to find the
requisite paper till now.
Will Dyson has received an honorary
appointment from the Australian Govern-
ment to make battle drawings at the Front,
more particularly of the Anzacs. These
drawings are to be constructed with a view
to historical interest, and will be the prop-
erty of the nation. The permission of the
British War Office is necessary to complete
the arrangement, and High Commissioner
Fisher was negotiating for that when last
mail left.
Albert Dorrington’s little daughter “Billy”
recently wrote to Kipling asking him to auto-
graph a photo of himself, which the late
Frank Bullen had given her. The picture
was returned, signed, with a note: —
Dear Miss Billy, — Because I do not always answer
letters personally X am considered a very husky fellow
at times. Between you and myself there is a grain
of truth in this report, and I am often very terrible
when the post comes in. I am glad you knew and
loved Frank Bullen ; he was also my dear friend, and
had a fine regard for you Splendid Australians. I
had a boy who came near writing stories about kan-
garoos and Queensland pearl-fishers. But another
call came, and Johnny went to France, where the
stars are still shining, although Johnny’s drum has
ceased to beat. —Very sincerely, Rudyard Kipling.
As most people are sorry to know, “Johnny”
Kipling was killed in France some time in
March last.
“Stenog” : Gilbert White, Bishop of Wil-
lochra. lately lectured some of the women of
Melbourne on the wickedness of putting re-
strictions on maternity. While he had them
there he might also have referred to the
dodgers of paternity. But perhaps lie didn’t
like to. Take Petersburg (S.A.), the head-
quarters of this talker’s diocese. White him-
self, his rector, curate and diocesan clerk are
all single men. White is either very rash
or else he can’t see that he resides in a
glass palace.
Australia’s old friend Arthur Bentley
Worthington lately came to the surface
again in a brief cable from New York which
stated that a Western Presbyterian Church
tribunal, after an investigation of charges
of false pretences, had deposed him from
the ministry. Years ago Worthington worked
a religious business in gorgeous fashion in
Christchurch (M.L.), mostly on the money
of adoring women. His “Temple of Culture”
included magnificent bathrooms—
things that are not often associated
with the old religions. When the
Temple became mostly mortgages and
debts, he engaged in soul-uplifting
operations in Sydney, and his Greek-
god head proved an attraction to
fluffy saints. But a lady who had
been induced to spend money on the
strength of vague remarks about the
rising of the Nile and the commands
of Osiris and Isis finally revolted,
and the Law decided that Isis was
a fraud and Osiris hadn’t a leg to
stand upon. Apparently Worthington
has ’verted to Presbyterianism for a
rest from these religious fancy-goods.
Melbourne councillor Charles Atkins, win
went to England and insisted on being made
a useful war-worker, is now a minor officer
of the Defence Corps: His alternate job is
to look after stores and boss the police-
guard, more or less, at a prisoners’ camp
where 600 Germans are having a nice quiet
time. When he goes the prison rounds he
carries a loaded revolver and is preceded by
a corporal armed with a loaded rifle, although
two men and a machine-gun would seem a
better precautionary measure. The incar-
cerated Huns give concerts, to which the
officer-in-charge is invited on condition that
he comes unarmed. Councillor Lieut. Atkins
went unarmed to a recent musical evening,
and sent a programme (printed in German,
of course) to his Melbourne friends as evi-
dence of good faith. There was a lot of
musical talent in an orchestra of 18 strings,
two reeds and four brasses. The first violin
was a member of the Kaiser’s Imperial
Orchestra, and a baritone soloist had b:-
longed to the Royal German Opera Co. The
superabundance of strings is accounted for,
seemingly, by the fact that fiddlers are feeble
combatants who surrender easily. They don’-
fight and die with the enthusiasm of clarion-
ettes and brasses.
Some men prefer the horse to motor-cars
and railway trains even in these days of
hustle. The other day David Barber, who
some years ago moved from Stawell (Vic.)
into N. S. Wales, took a trip to his old
home. He harnessed up his _ horse and
drove all the way, about 800 miles, putting
in just about a month on the trip. Mrs.
Barber did the first 500 miles or so with
him. Then she gave it up and caught a
train.

The Thomson family, which has its head-

has seven sons out of eight in khaki. The
eighth happens to be a parson ; but he woukl
also have gone to tight the helmeted Devil
abroad if a bishop had released his reverend
coat-tails.
THE DEAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Others returning; from the fields of fame
Will tell their tales of hazard and of stress,
And o’er and o’er to eager ears repeat
Their glimpse of battle, vivid, incomplete;
Blurred recollections, and no two the same ;
Broken remembrance patched with thought and guess.
But we who know the whole shall silent, be,
Holding our tongues to all eternity.
VICTORIA.

’Exclusive of Replacement and Depreciation Fund.
Poor old thing! It is having a dreadful
time. Eighteen months or so back the busi-
ness was cut in halves, and the C.S.R. (Fiji
and N.Z.) Ltd. was formed to take over the
assets in the Pacific. That left this old
C.S.R. Co. with the Queensland and a few
N.S.W. North Coast mills and farm proper-
ties and the Australian refinery business.
Queensland is, of course, the main source
of supplies; hence a disturbance of the
industry in Queensland has a larger effect
upon this co. now than it would have had
in the days before the subdivision of assets.
And there has been a disturbance in Queens-
land all right. The price of sugar was first
fixed by one authority and then the wages
in the industry w r ere fixed by another autho-
rity. The result was, naturally, chaos. A
Government can fix prices or it can fix
wages without any very serious conse-
quences. If the prices are fixed too low,
then wages have to accommodate them-
selves. If wages are fixed too high, then
prices have to accommodate themselves. But
when both prices and wages are fixed, then
the irresistible meets the immovable, and
there are likely to he neither prices nor
wages. In this case the farmers, after pro-
testing that they couldn’t go on, buckled to;
whereupon there were yells that the pre-
vious kicking was all bluff. But nothing of
the sort was proved. That the growers made
the best of it showed, not that they were
being fairly dealt with, but that they
couldn’t afford to lose everything. That
the chaos in the industry may do a lot of
damage to this co. is nothing to be cheerful
about. That it should seriously prejudice
one of the greatest of our semi-tropical
industries is little short of a national dis-
aster.
If there is any starving to be done and
anybody else can do it, it is fairly safe to
bet that it won’t be done, by the
C.S.R. Even in the midst of the present
Queensland trouble, it shows this silver
lining to the cloud: ’‘So far as the refining
business is concerned, we have again agreed
with the Commonwealth to handle this year’s
Australian crop and the sugar imported to
supply the community.” The table shows
how well the co. has done through all the
tribulations of the industry. Nominally it now
pays only 65 p.c. But the C.S.R. share-
holders got share for share in the Fiji co.,
and the latter pays a ridiculously easy 6 p.c.
So the actual return is 12| p.c. on
capital, which has been very largely pro-
vided out of earnings. Besides owning the
assets of the old co. in Australia this original
C.S.R. holds 250,000 ordinary £1 shares in
the Fiji co.; and these shares are entitled
to all the profits after the prefs. have had
0 p.c. On past experience this holding may
be enormously valuable by and bye. But
past experience may not be a fair guide to
the future. The Indian. has been of great
service in making sugar profits in Fiji; and
now the Indian Government, is discouraging
coolie emigration. Hence Fiji doesn’t know
for the moment where to turn ; for the local
population won’t work —hard enough, any-
way—-and the islanders are frankly afraid
to introduce Chinese lest the dragon should
swallow them presently.

That doesn’t look very cheerful —Sept., 1914,
£99,901 ; 1915, £82,022 ; 1916, £60,151. But
there is something to be said in extenuation:
out of the past half-year’s net profits £2OOO
has been put by for the benefit of employees
who have enlisted and £15,000 has been
snicked out to provide for additional State
and Federal taxes. Perhaps this better
shows what things would have been like but
for abnormal calls
Profits on Management, Balance
Brewing's, Wages, of
Sept., etc. Excise, etc. Profit.
1912. . £233,070 £132,768 £100,302
1913.. 268,548 172,935 95,613
1914.. 293.003 186,101 106,902
1915.. 270,499 174,478 96,021
1916.. 272,370 184,219 88,151
If we exclude 1914, when the circumstances
were exceptional, the gross profits on brew-
ings and trade have been well maintained.
On the other hand, not only have what may
be called the ordinary outgoings been
steadily increasing, but extra handfuls must
now be taken out of the profits. Between
1913 and 1916, to earn an extra £3822 called
for an extra expenditure of £11,284, and on
top of the latter figure falls the extra taxa-
tion and the vote for the benefit of men who
have enlisted.
So far as one can judge there has been
no decrease of trade on account of the early
closing of pubs. Anyway, the gross earn-
ings have been maintained. But this posi-
tion has only been reached by a larger out-
lay, some of it no doubt defensive; and this
and other brewing cos. probably always will
have to spend a good deal of money in fight-
ing. Apart from that it looks as though
there will be less sparkle in the beer very
soon now. The end of the N. S. Wales State
borrowing boom seems to have definitely
arrived. It will be kept going awhile, no
doubt, in a modified way, by the wicked
Norton Griffiths agreement; but the little
jobs with which the Government has been
feeding and bribing its supporters are stop-
ping one after the other. We are near the
same painful awakening that we had in the
early ’9o’s. Previously Tooths had been
gaily paying 10 p.c. In 1894 they were
down to 7 p.c. and were not too comfortable
even then. llow long the depression will
last this paper doesn’t know. Perhaps not
very long. It depends on the seasons and
what we can get for our wool and wheat
and metals. After that all experience leads
us to expect another such fling as this co.
has enjoyed during the last 10 years. Look
at this: —

and reserves combined) have shown only a
fraction over 6 p.c. for the past year; and
in a business of this sort it isn’t enough.
* * * *
Public Trust Office of Maoriland. —
Nobody in Maoriland now thinks of this
ollice as a cub of the Socialistic tiger. It
has the goodwill of practically everybody;
and it is growing every year. The principal
executive officer is the Public Trustee, who
is subject to the directions of a Board con-
sisting of the Minister for Finance, the
Public Trustee himself, the Government
Insurance Commissioner, the Valuer-Gen-
eral, the Superintendent of State Advances
and two persons outside the Public Service
appointed by the Government. The func-
tions of the office are numerous: besides
acting as trustee in the ordinary way, on
appointment, it automatically takes control
of intestate estates and of various public
sinking funds, etc. And it may act for pri-
vate trustees, carrying out their written
instructions.
In relation to ordinary trusts it does one
or two things which the private trustee
doesn’t attempt. Thus it will pay a fixed
rate of interest on trust funds if that be
desired and will guarantee the principal—in
which case, of course, the Public Trustee
selects the investment. That really is no
more than life insurance cos. do. And this
no doubt has suggested a new* feature —the
granting of bonuses, after the life-insurance
manner. Section 61 of the M.L. Finance
Act of this year says :
Where the net profits of the Public Trust Office in
any year exceed the sum of £20,000, then the
Governor-in-Council may direct that the surplus profits
or such part thereof as he names shall be credited
pro rata as a bonus to each estate or account (other
than investment agencies) that received interest from
the common fund during that year and have not been
finally closed and distributed before the end of that
year.
For the past year the bonus, distributed as
'an accretion to the account of each person
who receives interest from the common
fund, amounts to 2s. in the £. That is to
say, if the Dominion has guaranteed a bene-
ficiary a rate of interest which gives him or
her £2OO, his or her account will be credited
with a further £2O.
The profit for the past year was £52,156.
made up as follows: Net profit as per P.
and L. account, £46,109; earnings of Assur-
ance and Reserve Fund, £5589; ditto of
Investment Fluctuation Fund, £458. The
total reserves, including these profits for the
year, were £302,369 ; but from that £20,000
or so will have to be deducted, being the
amount of the bonus referred to above. The
profit is all derived from the surplus earn-
ings of the trust funds whose principal and
interest are guaranteed. The revenue from
that source last year was £65,448; so that
actually on the ordinary operations of the
office there was a loss. Which is another
way of saying that the commission charges
for ordinary trustee business are under the
cost of doing the work. The reserved funds
are substantial; but, as before said, they
have not only to cover the usual risks which
a trustee takes, but also to guarantee the
return of invested principal where that pro-
vision of the law has been taken advantage
of by the testator. The value of estates in
hand is £13,598,744 (as against £7,358,947
in 1910) and the amount of the “capital
funds”—that is, the trust funds whose in-
tegrity and interest are guaranteed—is
£7,082,888 (as against £3,414,168 in 1910).
Port Swettenham Rubber Co., office in
Melbourne, plantations in the Federated
Malay States: —
Charged to Deficit.

1914.. 1,047 Nil 641 6775
Law costs written off. £3455 .. 3320
1915.. 1,105 Nil 754 4425
1916.. 250 Nil 1441 4675
But those reserves are quite nominal, and
the profit for the past year may be realised
or it may not. If the whole truth were told
in the table —that is to say, if the accounts
gave the data for a complete story-— some*
where or other there would appear an item
“Loss by storm, £10,000,” or it may be more.
And even that wouldn’t reduce the figures
sufficiently. The co. got in at about the top
of the rubber boom, in 1910, and sold its first
year’s crop for 4s. 9d. per pound. Within four
years the market had crawled down the lad-
der about half-way; and the harvest, instead
of mounting higher, climbed down too. Then
came an adverse verdict .in the courts; and
after that a storm which left about 11,000
trees and destroyed 13,700. The co.’s local
advisers thereupon recommended the co. “to
buy another property if it. can raise the
money and if a good offer is made to sell
Swettenham estate.” This recommendation
was carried into effect during the past year
so far as the purchase of another plantation
is concerned. The new estate, known as the
Australasian, is 70 miles from the old one,
consists of 290 acres of four- and five-year-
old trees, and cost £15,500. The manager
thinks it is worth just under £17,000. Swet-
tenham, by the way, consists of 261 acres
with 11,000 trees in bearing and say 14,000
young stumps to replace those ruined by the
cyclone, and not fit for tapping for four
years. It stands in the books at £53,221.
Compare the two, and you get some idea of
the overvaluation of the Swettenham estate.
This means, of course, that, as things have
turned out, the co.’s share capital of £57,500
is too great. Perhaps £40.000 might be a
fair thing, including the £lO,OOO which is at
present borrowed money, raised to help pay
for the new estate.
Apart from that debt the co. owes £lBBl,
and it had cash and more or less liquid
assets totalling £1538 on June 30. However,
on working account a profit of £l7lO is
expected from the new estate this current
year and thereafter a steady improvement to
£6207 in 1920-1; and on the old estate there
should be £lOOO to the good this year, with a
small increase till 1920, and thereafter some-
thing substantial. This, of course, assumes
that a fair price will be maintained for rub-
ber. The estimate for the new estate is
based on two-and-fourpenny rubber this
year, with an allowance for shrinkage to
Is. lid. in 1920-1. Costs also are an im-
portant factor. Chinese and Tamil coolies
are employed, and it is assumed that they
are not going to change much. From these
figures deductions have to be made for man-
agement exes, and interest. Even so, how-
ever, and notwithstanding that so much of
the capital is blown away or has otherwise
disappeared, prospects are a lot better than
they were a year ago. Many of the people
who are interested in the Melbourne-owned
and successful Bukit Kubu are interested
also in this; and Bukit Kubu is giving the
new estate a hand.

The accounts allege that the net profit for
the year was £3714. But, apart from that.
£2287 has been added to what was formerly
called the Machinery Depreciation Reserve.
Whether any provision for wear and tear
is made otherwise is not very clear, though
there are some signs of it. In any case, if
the true net profit was but the £3714 it is a
remarkably satisfactory performance, remem-
bering that the capital is only £24,068. To
start with, £5OOO of the capital was repre-
sented by goodwill. Now, after six years’
trading, and notwithstanding the payment
of an annual dividend of 8 p.c. or upwards,
the entire capital is represented by tangible
assets, and there is a very substantial sum
over. Further, the shareholders are risking
very little borrowed money : total liabilities,
including trade debts, are £13,490. And the
profit is all the more imposing because the
co. has over £15,000 in city property, exclu-
sive of the plant and machinery which that
property houses. The other assets are book
debts and stock. Altogether a very gratify
ing balance-sheet; and the success is all
the more cheerful because the co. is engaged
in manufacture. In addition to the ordi-
nary cardboard box business, the co. a
couple of years ago went in for the pro-
duction of corrugated paper sheets and fibre-
board ; and the directors report that this
department has justified itself. In view of
the scarcity of light timber, for which the
corrugated sheet is a substitute, it seems
likely to go on doing it.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per' cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d„ making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd.— a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.— Advt. [2
