It is possible to put it another way. The
directors, knowing that the end of this
Broken Hill mine is not very remote, have
been looking around for other properties, and
a couple of years ago got on to a show at
Misima, in the Louisade Archipelago, which
forms part of Papua, though it is about 120
miles east of Samarai. General-manager O.
B. Ward has been probing this ground, and
incidentally smashing his thigh and making
himself about the most popular man in New
Guinea parts; and to June 8, when it was
decided to exercise the option, he had spent
£11,200 on his explorations. This amount
is formally written off the half-year’s profit;
and with that done the total is reduced to
£2654. But previous figures relate to the
working of the Broken Hill mine; and the
intrusion of figures referring to the cost of
developing some other property would spoil
the comparison.
On the outbreak of the war Block 10
lost its market for concentrates, and the
mill was shut down on August 19,
1914. The co., being no longer of
first-rate importance, took no part in
the subsequent purchase of the Port Pirie
plant from the Broken Hill Prop, and
the formation of the Associated Smelters.
When, however, the metal markets moved
along, the directors, feeling that they couldn’t
afford to go on missing the ’bus, made a con-
tract with Associated Smelters; and the
mill resumed its job on May 8 this year.
Since then 34,914 tons of ore have been
treated. In view of (1) the high price of
lead and (2) the facility with which ore of
somewhat better than average grade was
taken out, in consequence of work done after
the mill was shut down, the profit is dis-
appointingly small. However, a bit more
may come in from surpluses on final settle-
ment. Credit appears to be taken for that
10 p.c. of the value which is held back by
the smelters when the first payment is made ;
but that first payment is made on the basis
of £25 lead and 2s. silver, and the metals
may realise more than that.
Looking to the future, big profits can only
be expected when there are booming markets.
The co. can never make big profits through
low costs. Ore extraction can never again
be cheap, because it is largely a question of
picking up small or comparatively small and
overlooked shoots and bodies; and that may
mean working through broken ground. No
estimate of ore reserves is now made. The
latest figure was 229,000 tons. But it is
wonderful how long life can be kept in these
big old mines by going back with the rake.
The directors think well enough of the future
to put up a flotation plant in conjunction
with Block 14, which is in the same class.
This will deal with current output of tail-
ings : Block 10’s old dumps went long ago.
One way and another, things will have to be
bad in the future if the co. can’t realise
profits averaging Bs. per ton of ore. That
will allow for a high mining charge. And
things will be equally disappointing if
300.000 tons of ore are not hauled from the
mine. That would mean £120,000 of divi-
dends.
Misima is to be thrown into a co. with
its neighbors. The latter properties are
owned by the St. Aignan’s and Misima Gold
Mines cos. The co. will be in 150.000 issued
shares, of which Block 10 will get 69,666
shares in return for the money it spent
(£11,200) and £30,000 to be put to working
capital. O. B. Ward reckons he has 102.000
tons of 395. ore blocked out already in the
old options, and can see “a profit of at least
£50,000 above legitimate working and de-
velopment costs.” Block 10 has plenty of
strength to do the financing. Its surplus of
liquid assets on Sept. 30 was £63,240.
* * * *
“Drummoyne’s” query :—-
Re Tooth’s. The ordinary dividend this half-year
(as last half) is reduced from 10 to 9 per cent. At
the same time a “reserve for equalisation of divi-
dends” amounting to £40,000 is shown. Last half-
year I fancy an appropriation was actually made to
this reserve. What is a “reserve for equalisation of
dividends” for, if not to maintain a dividend at a
certain figure when net profits in themselves might
not justify the payment ? Of course, the argument may
be that it stands against some very big decline in
profits; but the policy, on the face of it, looks
inconsistent.
Theoretically, at any rate, what Tooth’s
have done could be justified on the ground
that some specific thing has happened which
permanently reduces the profits by one-tenth
at least, that the 9 p.c. cliv. now puts the
same strain upon the co. as formerly 10 p.c.
did, and that an equalisation reserve is now
required, not to lift the dividend up to 10
p.c., but to maintain it at 9 p.c.

Fi’oin a man in the wolfram business:—
The British Ministry of Munitions, acting through
the Federal authorities, has commandeered the entire
wolfram output of Australian mines. The price fixed
in September, 1915, was 555. per unit. The cables
of a few days ago stated that 00s. per unit was the
price paid by the Ministry in England. The price
paid by the Government agents in Sydney is 48s. Id.
per unit. The poor agent’s margin, then, is about £42
per ton to cover freight, commission, etc. Although
the price in New York is now 80s. per unit, up till
a few weeks ago the market had been operating for
many months around 2405. per unit. Even at the
present rates the Australian producer is being paid
about £l4O per ton less than the Americans. Our
mines have been operating for the past 12 months
at 48s. Id. per unit, or Is. 6d. per lb. of wolfram.
It was only last week that the cables announced that
the British Ministry of Munitions had fixed the price
of tungsten steel at ss. fid. per lb. of tungsten
content. That’s not a bad margin for the steel
people; and what a harvest they must have had
during the past 12 months! The companies operat-
ing on this Deepwater field produce about 120 tons
per annum, and 200 tons would be a safe estimate
if all the mines were working; but the 120 tons is
in danger of becoming zero if an equitable adjust-
ment is not soon forthcoming. At present, the
arrangement made by the Government simply
amounts to fleecing the working miner.

“Waiting” : In case the wildest cat of the
jungle has not come under The Bulletin’s
notice, please let me say that yoif may buy
a description of it from the Government
Printer for ss. This description answers to
the name of “The Auditor-General’s report
on the Queensland State Government
finances to June, 1916.” No one has been
arrested yet, so comment will not be sub
judice.
* * * *
Mount Royal copper, Tottenham (N.S.W.),
has been half a lifetime waiting for the rail-
way to reduce smelting costs. With cheaper
coke and fluxes large quantities of ore can
now be treated ; it is reckoned—optimistically
perhaps—that normally 3 p.c. ore will pay.
During the past eight months something like
8000 tons have been smelted to produce 260
tons of copper, which spells an average of
3.25 p.c. The metal is sent to Port Kembla
in the form of matte, which, by the way, is
very useful for fluxing high grade silicious
ore. The management proposes to chew up
reserves by producing matte containing 70
tons of copper per month.
* * * *
History is repeating itself in the South
Broken Hill Board room. Years back P. C.
Howard was appointed to the Board to
represent the Adelaide shareholding- —a job
he still holds. With Molesworth Greene
dead, another vacancy has had to be filled.
The newcomer is Donald Fraser, friend of
the Collins House crowd and a pal of W. S.
Robinson. It isn’t everyone these days who
is dropped into a £5OO directorship like that
of the Associated Smelters and picks up
another almost as fat within the year.
“Edo” calls for a reform: —
Because numerous State batteries in Westralia have
been the means of producing a lot of gold, and in
some instances of making prospectors even rich, it
must not be concluded that the system is perfect.
The pioneer prospector who discovers more or less
rich ore very rarely puts up a mill for himself while
a public crushing plant is handy; and because he
has to pay fairly well for ore treatment, and because
he wants to boom his property, he almost invariably
takes out the rich patches only. Of course, the hope
is that a buyer will' appear. But the buyer is mostly
too stingy and the vendor too greedy, and no sale
eventuates. Meantime the rich patch-hunting goes
on, and the usual result is a rabbit warren which in
the end has to be abandoned, because the good stuff is
all extracted and the ground is in too dangerous a
condition to permit explorers to hunt for more rich
patches, while the poor rock left will not pay, there
being no crusher on the mine itself. From Southern
Cross up to Laverton and Wiluna and beyond much
good auriferous ground is so riddled and badly worked
as to be useless. In most cases abandoned leases would
require too much cash to straighten out workings or
they are in such a dangerous state that no sane man
would risk his life in inspecting theni. This bad
state of affairs could easily be Let the
Mines Department make.the charges low only so long
as a property is being properly opened up ; and let
the charges b,e increased when a property is. capable
of keeping its own plant going. -*£-
* *
Nullagine (W.A.) failed to get enough
water to start sluicing, and down came the
market with a heavy thud. The report of a
2in. shower momentarily broke the fall, but
the apparent helplessness of even this down-
pour to relieve the situation afterwards made
the drop swifter than before. Nothing, it is
stated, can now be done until more rain
falls, but if the ground is as dry as some old
hands represent, a flood will be needed to
keep the co. alive. There is no doubt about
the gold being there, but its profitable extrac-
tion is another matter.
Paynes Find Co. (W.A.) battles along.
Last half-year’s receipts were, as usual, out-
distanced by expenditure, and the debit of
Profit and loss now stands at £4542. At the
outset The Bulletin regretfully remarked
that it was hopeless to expect the show to
earn money for shareholders. The co.’s in-
debtedness to sundry creditors has now in-
creased to over £6OOO, and is still mounting.
Cash assets total £164, and machinery, plant,
etc., £2220; but in an outlandish place like
Paynes Find the auction value of plant is
barely more than that of old iron. The
co. should have given up the struggle long
ago.
Westralian Government has joined Morgan,
Lyell and other Eastern producers in a
copper deal and sold the whole of the State’s
electrolytic copper production for 1917 to
the British Munitions Department. The
quantity involved is about 60 tons a month,
and the price is fixed at £ll6 13s. 4d. per
ton.
*••***
For some time owners of low-grade shows
have been allowed special crushing rates at
the Westralian State mills, but they will
now have to send more ore along if the
privilege is to continue. Total weekly treat-
ment capacity of these batteries is between
4000 and 5000 tons, but not one-fiftli of this
is utilised, and for the most part of the year
the stamps are idle. Only 3700 tons of low-
grade ore, which forms the bulk of the stone '
treated, were put through the Government
mills during the first eight months of 1016,
and Minister for Mines Robinson says that
unless there is a big improvement the present
concession will not be retained.
* * * *
Edna Consolidated, Westonia (W.A.), is
straightening up its corkscrewed main shaft.
It is also dragging in calls to pay for a
battery. This plant is going up because
N. S. Stuckey and J. M. Aldridge, who
jointly run a show that could easily be
handled by one man,, reckon the co. has
15,000 tons of ore in sight. Most of this
dirt lies in the shallow ground, and though
it is reckoned payable, there isn’t the shadow
of a doubt that it’s patchy stuff. The main
prop of the show is still the new make cut
at 200 ft. in a south-west prospecting drive
shoved out from the west crosscut off the
main shaft. Officially the shoot is reckoned
the “most important and satisfactory dis-
covery yet made on the lease.” This has a
sweet sound, but is not half so alluring as
the statement that the reef “stands nearly
vertical, with a slight underlay to the south-
west.” The implication is that the formation
dips that way. It may be so, but the green-
stone is cutting across the lease almost as
flat as a pancake. With such an underlie the
Edna Central to the north looks like having
the call of the shoot if it opens up as any-
thing more than a bulge in a pretty badly
fractured bit of country. The cold truth is
that, although the Consolidated has put
£16.500 into its patch of gneissic schist,
nothing except big expectations has devel-
oped so far.
* * * *
When Jim Harris took over the New
Golden Fleece, Bendigo, he spent a month
checking values before saying whether the
mine had a chance. The claim is almost
out in No Man’s Land, and it has stagnated
since the racketty days of its youth. Quartz
is in plenty, especially at 400 ft., where there
is one make of 30ft.; but it shows how much
road metal and how little gold can go to
the cubic yard. Still, nice carbonaceous
slates are associated with the spurs near
the fault, so there is something to cling to
in that quarter. There is a run of spurs
at 600 ft. that has provided enough gold to
justify further hunting in the locality at
750 ft.; and the Comet reef, if provided with
nothing much else, has more than the aver-
age of blende and galena. In these circum-
stances Harris has hope, but declines to talk
about a prosperous future. It is a long shot
—no more.
* * * *
Moline’s No. 9 run at Cock’s Pioneer,
Eldorado (Vic.), gave only 7d, a yard. Now
he is back to Is. sd. dirt with No. 10. The
last three runs panned out thus: —
No. 8. No. 9. No. 10.
Cub. yds. sluiced .. 74,000 120,000 90,000
Gold—oz 566 570 1,215
Tin Oxide —tons .. 8 9] 15
Taking gold at £4 per oz. and tin oxide at
£lOO per ton, the gross value of No. 10 run
is £6360; and if costs were kept down at
8d (though the floods may have something
to say on that point) a net profit of over
£3OOO would remain. The improvement
shows what can happen at Cock’s Pioneer.
* * * *
Star of the West, Kevington (Vic.), is
small potatoes compared with what it was
when James Mackay and Marriott first fast-
ened on to it. Between 40,0000 z. and 50,0000 z.
gold were got out of the Star shoot before
obscurity came for the last year or two. No
amount of tootling has charmed the necessary
cash from shareholders to sink into more
settled country. Still Jim Mackay isn’t
throwing up the sponge, and as the country
hasn’t been more than scratched he may get
the old claim on to its legs again, although
at present it’s about as groggy as the general
run of Victorian mountain shows.
* * * *
New Minnie Moxham (N.Q.) has had its
dam replenished by a fall of rain, and the
mill is to be given another run. No. 2 level
(210 ft.) has been driven to 152 ft., and for
the last few feet there has been a strong reef
2ft. wide carrying visible gold. So far there
have been no deep gold-mines in N. Queens-
land ; even the Tyrconnel, on the Hodgkin-
son, enriched its first owners from shallow
levels above 300 ft. vertical. Copper and tin
mines, too, after a dazzling record from
shallow levels, have become very uncertain
propositions as depth increased. Even the
old Vulcan, which got payable tin below
1000 ft., isn’t a good exception, for it is
making a call.
Ba.vley’s Reward, Coolgardie, is not done
yet. Recently two old prospectors struck ore
in what is believed to be a continuation of
the shoot of gold from which the late Arthur
Bayley and his mate got rich returns. So
far, 23 tons have been put through the State
mill for nearly 4500 z.
* * *
Hans Irvine has made a start on his Trip-
licate option at Tuckabianna, near Cue
(W.A.). Some Italians found the lode, which
is big and said to be rich, though a 70-ton
crushing treated by the vendors before hand-
ing over to Irvine yielded only 40oz. over
the plates.
♦ * * *
Tasmanian Government Geologist Twelve-
trees has submitted a preliminary report on
the asbestos deposits at Anderson’s Creek.
The serpentine belt in which the asbestos
occurs is about two to three'miles west of
Beaconsfield, and extends from one and a
quarter to two miles in length by about a
mile wide. The Department has been asked
to assist prospecting, and if satisfactory to
subsidise the erection of a fibering plant -uni
also offer a bonus on the initial output. At
present,” says Twelvetrees, “the prospecting
work appears to be just on the border
between payable and unpayable results; it
would require only a slightly greater amount
of success to tip the balance.”

HOW I SAVED MY HAIR.
“My hair was thin, short, dry and brittle.
Every time I combed or brushed it large
quantities came out, and I had begun to
fear that soon I should be entirely bald. A
specialist whom I consulted said mine was
a particularly bad case of dandruff —so bad
that he knew of only one thing which he
could hope would overcome the trouble and
restore my scant tresses to their former
luxuriance and beauty. He told me to get
my chemist to prepare a lotion composed of
three ounces of bay rum, one ounce lavona
de composee and quarter-dram menthol
crystals, and to rub this into the scalp twice
a day with the finger-tips. I was sceptical,
but did as I was told. To my delighted
astonishment I soon noticed that my hair
had stopped falling out, and in a few weeks
my head was covered with a new growth of
downy hair. I continued the use of the
lotion until I possessed a head of hair, all
my own, that is the wonder of all my
friends. My specialist says he has known
of many cases similar to mine, and in every
instance the results have been truly mar-
vellous. He says this is the greatest hair-
growing formula ever known. It has done
so. much for me that I hope you will publish
this for the benefit of those who want to
save their hair.”—R.C.E.— Advt.

Sydney. November 13, 1916.
My Dear Art.ni. —
“On a visit to Melbourne” is the social
legend which politely hints that something
or other is going on in the Peacock’s garden.
Potts Point and Darling Point are bearing
u.p against the exodus to the overflowing
Cup. A great peace broods everywhere ex-
cept over coal and politics, and the G.-G.
took a week’s extension of his refuge here
from the odds in Melbourne.
The G.-G. Fairguson caught the express to
Melbourne on Sabbath night, but broke the
journey at Albury. Military and other
dongs there were to hold him till the follow-
ing morn.
Mrs. Wade is wondering how she’ll like
London. For it's likely to come off. If
father’s health or the fact that two into one
won’t go makes the trip necessary, we shall
soon hear more about the splendor of the
twins. Mother is a well-meaning little
woman, and the duchesses might be more im-
pressed if her manners were a trifle more
amiable.
There’s a virile Scotchman over here from
British East Africa —one M. A. McGregor,
head of a big experimental farm. He’s a
buyer with a bag of gold for good stud cattle
and beasts of burden. The McGregor put in
a few days at Hill View with the Stricklands,
and is glowing with the tale of life near
the jungle. He says the climate is good, and
before the war money was plentiful. British
East Africa used to be the place where mil-
lionaires —in fashionable attire —stalk< d
lions, tigers and big game, and lady hunt-
resses collected elephants’ teeth and pan-
thers’ claws for their dressing-tables. Now
there’s big-game shooting in France, and the
elephants take their morning bath unmo-
lested.
After all, his Ex. did rush up to Newcastle
to open the Charities Carnival. Then he
hurried home from the coal dust to keep his
eye on the Cabinet and finally supply the
newspapers with new headlines.
The Women’s Sketch Club wisely refrained
from a plunge in frames, remembering that
this is war-time and art patrons are slow
to bite. Purchasers took their wares away
in brown paper, and saw to the glazing them-
selves. The show is in the Education gal-
leries. Calendars, good, bad and worse,
make a lug bid for the casual half-crown.
In the more ambitious realm of unframed
impressions in water-colors, Laura Booth and
Mrs. F. W. Parsons and Mrs. Hedley Par-
sons dominate the landscape department.
The two Parsons ladies are unrelated, though
they are both facile brushwomen. Betty
Solomons, a promising art student, con-
tributes some virile portrait sketches—nota-
bly a youthful siren with red hair flaming
down her back.
When the Arabia followed two other
P. and O. steamers to the bottom, battalion
dames began to hurriedly look up dates.
Most of the Christmas stuff for our soldiers
went, round the Cape—thanks be! But
there’s an appalling lot of Christmas packets
and letters lying at the bottom of the sea in
mailbags. When will the enemy get all he
is looking for? On the Arabia went the
widow of the late James Graham, M.D., a
former legislator here. Mrs. James was a
sister at Prince Alfred Hospital when the
Scotchman proposed. If there were any
hysterics when the torpedo came this cap-
able woman would promptly take charge- of
the fuss.
Ashfield is very pleased with itself. Mrs.
Hammond, its mayoress, has just handed over
to the Edith Cavell Memorial Committee
nearly £6OO. The plum fell from a recent
fete in the suburban town hall. Somewhere
about £3OO is likely to be counted to the good
from a concert organised since, in Sydney, by
Mrs. Verbrugghen. So there’s going to be
corn in more places than Egypt.
.Mrs. George Black is on her wav back
to Australia, after a health trip to the
other side.
Brigadier-General Jack Antill sends a
coo-ee from France, where he has a com-
mand. Looking back on the August fighting
in Egypt—the only chance the Light Horse
had as mounted troopers—he says :
The Turks attacked with 18,000 men, and it’s
hard to see what they were after. The tough fight
was on the night of August 4 —the second anniversary
of the war —when the Ist and 2nd Light Horse bri-
gades, assisted by the M.L. Mounted Rifles, drove off
Johnny Turk. Von Cress, the German Commander in
Chief, then bolted with his staff, leaving Hanoi Bey
to get out as best he could. Then the Brd Light
Horse and the Maorilanders gave chase, and Johnny-
got a dusting in three battles on the edge of the
desert. The Fez fought well, as he always does.
Colonel Dudley White is being transferred
from the old Barracks at Paddington to
France. Mrs. White, who goes also to do
some war work thereabouts, was before mar-
riage one of the handsome Yaldwyns of
Queensland. Her two sisters. Mrs. Fred and
Mrs. Bucldand Taylor, have been in the Fog
for years.
Before Mrs. White left the usual Pekoe
brew was handed round by Mrs. Stuart
Thompson at the Australia. Maybe some-
where in France her husband-colonel will
meet a brother of his who has a big com-
mand at the Front.
One of the frocks that make the flapper
want to put her hair up in a bun and
qualify for the matrimonial stakes went to
old St. James’s altar last week. The girl
inside this loveliness —diaphanous white
georgette under a cloud-like veil of honiton —
was Myee Garland. The lucky man was
M. J. Steel, of Aslifield. Diamonds, his
token of devotion, glistened in the lacy
corsage.
The Queen Victoria Club, in the Chamber
of Commerce Building, is going to sell
onions and parsnips and fat cakes of butter
on November 20. The cash goes to the
Voluntary Workers’ Fund.
The gallant Andre
de Beauvivier, now
a prisoner of war
i n Germany, i s
looking at you in
this picture. Be-
fore the war he
was one of the most
popular of the
French woolbuyers
who make Sydney
their headquarters.
Here he represented
the Rheims firm of
Dreyfus and Modi,
and lived at Turra-
murra when the
wool season was
hibernating. In the
act of returning to
Australia via the
Cape when the Hun sprang at the French
throat, Beauvivier dropped off at a neutral
port and rushed back to offer his services to
his country. For two years he has been in
the firing-line. Enlisting in 1914, he won a
commission on the battlefield, was mentioned
in despatches, and later decorated with the
Croix de Guerre. In the recent struggle at
Verdun he was one of a handful of French-
men taken prisoners when the rest of their
company had been annihilated. Captain de
Beauvivier has been recommended for the
Legion of Honor —and will no doubt get it if
the Dachshund lets his prisoners live to see
the Dove flap its wings.
That tireless streak of energy, Mrs. Boult,
is rounding up recruits to give a cheer-o to
the kiddies of soldiers. Of course it’s for
the Christmas stocking. Last year, if you
remember, the organiser’s “Joan of Arc”
was the matinee bait, and a swarm of
youngsters got away, after the performance,
with an armful of toys. This year Mrs.
Boult, who throws playlets together as
lightly as Alphonse tosses an omelette, gives
“The Buckle of his Shoe” for the cause at a
matinee on December 16.
The northern suburbs, with hard-pressed
hospitals to help, went on a carnival spree
last week. The chief bosses were big A. C.
Cocks, M.L.A., Tom Carlton (who organised
the show for love), and Alderman Cowdroy.
North Sydney and Mater Miserieordim hos-
pitals are patiently waiting for the cash to
be counted. Saturday’s finale was a proces-
sion trimmed with trade advts. and presided
over by a queen (Mrs. Lincoln Jones), who
out-gemmed her Majesty of Sheba.
One of Sydney’s beautiful homes, on the
green hat of Point Piper, had a big garden
fete on Saturday afternoon —by the grace of
Mr. and Mrs. Richard Binnie. The idea was
to put new household linen in the School of
Industry cupboards. This survival of old
Sydney now tends the small orphan girl at
Petersham. Years ago it used to be at
Darlinghurst, where the Hospice for the
Dying now stands.
The old School of Industry was started
by Mrs. Darling. She was the wife of that
early N. S. Wales Governor to whom Sydney
gave such an enthusiastic farewell, citizens
cutting steaks from an ox roasted in the
Wentworth grounds to show their joy at
his departure. Mrs. Darling’s scheme worked
fairly well in the primitive days when young
girls could be trained as domestic servants.
They wore lugubrious uniforms of the drab
winsey which society then considered helpful
to the morals of the young person. The
other afternoon bright-faced youngsters in
Australian blue frocks grinned cheerfully
under wide sailor hats as a sport named
Kendall gave them the word “go” down a
slithering wooden slide on the Binnio lawn.
These are the School of Industry kiddies
modern style. General Finn and his lady
joined the party—which was mostly young
and hilarious —and daughter Kathleen pre-
sided over icecreams. Mrs. Clibborn, Mrs.
Scott and Mrs. Garland were amongst the
stallholders. A hundred pounds or more
goes into the lockers as a result.
Here is a remarkable instance of feminine
sagacity as displayed-by the new girl. There
was a birthday pending, and unusual efforts
were put forth to celebrate the occasion. An
artist assisted the ladies of the home circle
in decorating the'dining-room with charming
paper-flowers. The result might have put
Solomon in all his glory into the shade. The
morning of the great day arrived, and the
new girl was up bright and early to push
things along. Then the family arose, and
sudden lamentation filled the house. The
decorations hung in grotesque dribbles of
mush. The new girl, desiring to keep every-
thing fresh and dewy, had sprinkled the
paper-flowers, with a copious spray.
The international rumpus has shaken up
the Sydneysiders’ desire to converse in
foreign tongues. Hitherto we have allowed
the inevitable German clerk to slip into any
well-paid billet which had a big Continental
correspondence. Hence the present enthusi-
asm for a blend of French conversation. For
some months A. C. Aucher, 8.A., lias been
chatting to our girls on French literature.
At a recent meeting he took the chair, while
others talked of the right way to extend this
education. Asa result delegates will give a
profound bow to the Alliance Frangaise and
see what they can suggest.
Captain J. F. de Meyrick, formerly of
Moss Vale and now in khaki, has put the
usual question to Constance, a. daughter of
George Broughton, of Carey Bay, Toronto.
Philip Wilson, the elegant young man who
is on the teaching staff of the Conserva-
torium, gave a highbrow song recital in the
early spring of last week. A top-dressing
of our Best People, supplemented by the
Hairbows Department, straggled in to sit at
the tall tenor's feet. Philip has an aca-
demic style, too cold for common use, and
pours out his honeyed tones with more re-
straint than the multitude approves. Still,
he pleased in groups of old Italian, French
and new Australian songs. The first were
dug out of the cupboards of the long past—-
the quaint “Amarilli” had her first birthday
in the end of the sixteenth century. Fiddler
W. J. Coad and pianist Frank Hutchens
assisted.
On Saturday, December 2, there’s to be
a kiddies’ party at Beulah, Beaconsfield-
parade, Lindfield, in aid of the Army Service
Corps’ Comforts Fund. A Christmas tree
is the star turn.
It is not all as unpleasant in France as
the Coldfoot Guards thought it might be.
One of The Bulletin’s kittens, writing
from headquarters of an artillery brigade,
describes their new billet. A two-storey
house; used to be a chateau. Big. airy
rooms; fireplaces; windows; furniture (you
can see his eyes getting bigger and bigger) :
hot water; and (here he bursts into printed
capitals) padded satin walls!
Talking about kittens, another artillery
boy, who was among the field-guns for 22
continuous hours in a recent push, tells me
of a little companion they had. A kitten,
taken possession of by the animal-loving
Billjims at a farmhouse-billet, was made at
home in the gunpits, and soon settled down
to its new life. It isn’t alarmed by the
firing, and actually crawled about among the
guns during the Push, when each throat
coughed a couple of shots a minute for hours
at a stretch, and all the furies were raging
above and around.
When the war
broke on Belgium
singers, artists and
musicians of every
tonnage rushed to
the defence of their
little country.
Amongst the sing-
ers was Albert
Goossens, now on
the staff of Sydney
C o nservatorium.
Discharged honor-
ably after their
little .country had
been trodden* flat,
the operatic star
and others of his
kind came this way
with a band of
high-class orches-
tral performers. Then Verbrugghen took
charge of the Conservatorium and pounced
on Goossens as a teacher of singing. One of
the most amiable and unselfish members of
the profession, the Belgian, whose photo I
send you, has been always eager to do his bit
at patriotic shows. Within an hour of get-
ting' news of his brother’s death, in action,
he sang the “Marseillaise” in our Town Hall
rather than upset the programme. Goossens,
who used to warble in grand opera in
Brussels, holds a sheaf of prizes. Bruges
Conservatorium handed him its highest
honor. In Brussels he won four. This week-
end he gives a concert in the Conservatorium.
with Yvonne Charvin at the piano.
If you want to see a pathetic little pro-
cession, soldier-wives with the youngsters
clutching mother’s skirts, go to 2(j Carring-
ton-street, Wynyard Square. Don’t knock.
Go through the passage and push the back
door open. Shelves with a swiftly-lessening
stock of fruit and vegetables, eggs, jam, etc.,
as the day goes on supply the dependents of
men in the firing-line with some of the little
luxuries of life. This is R. M. Pontey’s
scheme, which is now getting into low water
for lack of funds. Only the Casino branch
of the Red Cross has stuck to this effort.
The Absent-Minded Beggar has left so many
things behind him that 700 families and over
2000 youngsters are on the depot’s books
for heip. Yet, if the public doesn’t come to
the rescue, 12 months’ good deeds must end.
Sister Florence Spalding, the petite and
capable Nightingale who did line work
amongst our wounded, is now Mrs. Leslie
Fidler, of Gordon. The wedding-breakfast
was at the Pacific, Manly, and the knot was
tied in the local kirk. The Royal Red Cross
decoration and “Mentioned in despatches”
are items in the trousseau Sister Flo takes
to her new home.
From Maoriland comes news of the
engagement of Kitty Sinclair Thompson to
Major White, of somewhere in England.
Both Miss Thompson’s brothers enlisted
from S. Canterbury and both were killed in
the awful strife. Banker Sinclair Thompson
and family then migrated to the Fog. Miss
Kitty excels as a chauffeur and at other
times in a hockey hustle. Her mother was
one of the gilded Goulds of Christchurch.
The headmistress is beginning to sit up.
and Miss Badham is president of a new
association. She is the daughter of a former
Sydney ’Varsity don, and drank deep
draughts of Greek at her learned father’s
knee. Only sclioolmarms outside the Govern-
ment area will be eligible for membership.
At the back of this new growth there’s a
wish to make headmistresses 'of important
girls’ schools more articulate. For instance,
many of the more experienced dames see
danger in the large masses of instruction
which the State is pressing down the throats
of juveniles who have scarcely a bowing
acquaintance with English.
One of the old Windsor family of Prim-

The Kangaroo has never felt any bitterness
against the usually fair-fighting Turk. An
Australian soldier, writing from Alexandria,
says he got permission from the Commis-
sioner, Sir Henry McMahon, to take a trip
to “the Cage,” where the prisoners are:—
I took Hamil for an outing, and also Nouri Bey,
an Arab commander. The former, I found, is a
cultivated Turkish gentleman and a very smart man.
He had been educated in Germany, and had besides
spent four years there before the war—-while the plot
was thickening.... He expressed the greatest surprise
and admiration for the mobility of the Australian
Light Horse.
Young Rex Buckland, of N. S. Wales, and
Urquhart and Robertson, of Victoria, were
killed in the fight with Hamil.
State-school women teachers meet together
here and there. On Saturday evening they
clattered into the new Education Gallery,
where Miss Soubeiran dilated on her tw r o pet
themes, Frenchwomen in war time and the
doings of the French League in Macdonell
House. Miss Gurney knitted vigorously while
her colleague discoursed on the wonderful
spirit of modern France. No one doubts it.
In profound admiration, and by favor of
the British navy, Australian school kiddies
have sent about 30,000 garments to the
orphans of Jean and Pierre.
It looks as if the war has crushed the
crinoline. It was very nearly, but not quite,
ready (like the rest of us) about two years
ago. The New Yorkers are wearing nar-
rower skirts in their present wintry weather.
And the imitation mummy is now the lay
figure _on which the man-dressmaker is
swathing draperies like bandages. An Aus-
tralian abroad writes of a black satin gown
encrusted with heavy embroidery that would
have suited Mrs. Pharaoh I. if that mystic
was as willow wand as we imagine. So
there’s no saying whether that old tube
tailor-built skirt of yours won’t be the
latest cut in autumn, 1917.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

(whose address is to the undermentioned trustees
unknown, but who is supposed to have come to
Queensland some time before 1870), or his child or

sentatives or creditors is or are hereby required pur-
suant to an order of the Supreme Court of New
Zealand made on the 30th day of August, 1916, to
send in his, her or their claims forthwith' in connec-

Malcolmson in the Estate and under the will of the
abovenamed John Malcolmson deceased to JOHN
McRITCHIE MARTIN and DAVID BRADFORD, care
of Raymond Raymond & Campbell, Solicitors, Timaru,
New Zealand. If no claim is made within three
months of the last miblication of this advertisement
the said trustees will distribute the said estate as it
the said James Malcolmson had died unmarried betoie
the said John Malcolmson.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, November 13, 1916.
My Dear Yaurie, —
Cup Day arrived, smiling but steamy, on
Saturday. The sun peered through the
heavy moisture like an overdue cook, and the
lawn felt like a hothouse full of new blooms.
The dresses did come to light, too. Mrs.
Waters, from Sydney, looked like an ice-
cream in white ninon and pink tucks. The
Mt. Gambier Clai'kes floated in chiffon. One
was champagne-colored and tucked crosswise.
Roma Clarke bid for publicity with dappled
shoes and generous glimpses of fawn-silk
legs. Squatter Lewis’s two daughters from
Adelaide, clad in sporty linen duds, got every
ounce of enjoyment out of the day and played
about with Miss O’Loughlin, daughter of a
South Australian M.P. Sam McGullagh, just
returned from Red Crossing, looked pale and
bored. Mrs. Trautwein, whose husband owns
Quinologist, explained that the animal must
win the Cup, as they had a fortune on its
back. She was a lai’ge and striking episode
in bright pink silk. Mrs. Mills walked the
lawn after Sasanof’s win with her black
Spanish veil hanging dejectedly from a black
hatpin and a doleful expression on her coun-
tenance. One gets into such a habit of
winning, you know. Her friend Mrs. Widdis
accompanied her. She was moi'e cheerful
and was comfortably frocked in white.
General Sellheim was the guest of the
stewards. He wore khaki and an abstracted
aii% and his wife white crepe de chine and a
lovely hat of black, white and gold. General
Williams was inconspicuous in mufti with a
blazing lilac tie, and Mrs. Williams Wore
shot gold-brown silk.
Sam Hordern wore a broader smile even
than usual when his blue-and-gold colors
came home aboard Pleasant Moments in the
November Nursei’y. The Bungowanah Power
chief took his win cheerfully. He looked
bluff and well in short-cropped grey hair and
blue serge. Lesley Madden terraced her
black cloth gown with rows and rows of
coster buttons. No Governors decorated the
vice-regal pen, but it was not left tenant-
less. Lesley sat there with three fi’iends.
Mrs. Davis, in a fluffy mixture of blue and
filmy black, went out with her poet-com-
poser brotlier, Boanas. The Maoriland
Coopers wei’e in evidence and rejoiced patri-
otically over Sasanof’s win.
Mrs. Connolly wore the trickiest gown of
navy all smocked round her slender waist
and hitched together with little blue buckles
and ribbons. Mrs. Barry Thompson wore
the loveliest champagne stockings and other
things to match. Tom Whyte, of Deniliquin
and Cliveden Flats, motored out with his
little wife —a dainty vision in buff color and
brown. Mrs. Russell Gabb mourned the
defeat of Londonderry in navy.
The rain clouds seemed to be massing for
an attack just as the Cup was to be
run; but they restrained their fury and
held off until the last i*ace. As the
last winner passed the post the rain came
down like a grey curtain on the dressed and
the undi’essed, ruining chiffons and frail
hats; and pelting on the devoted heads of
men herding their chattering women towards
the motor paddock. Then there was a
rush! Ci’owds shoved and clamored at the
cloakrooms and the motor paddock looked
like the flight from Antwerp. At this
dreary stage Mrs. Leslie Stuart revelled in
the smartness of a serviceable flannel suit
with coon stripes of blue and white. A
Cromwellian round white felt hat sat securely
on her head. Mrs. Geoff. Syme in white
chiffon was under a tiny parasol like a rare
china saucer. Mrs. Campbell, her race com-
panion, seemed happy inside a big shower-
proof race coat.
As any sport will tell you, Thursday and
Friday were good “drying days.” Dry-
ing days always interest washerladies, but
in uncertain weather a racehorse-owner or
a cricketer can be just as fussy as any old
hired dame with a basket of clothes. Eaimest
backers spent their time in studying the
flanks of prospective Cup horses. They
reckoned that the winner would have to
possess a heavy back understanding to lift
itself out of the mud and throw itself for-
wards towards victory.
Hennessy, our chronic Mayor, gave the
usual yearly dinnei’, and his lady asked the
customary feminine bunch to coffee and
claret cup upstairs till it was time to listen
to the speeches from the balconies. Hughes
was to speak, and as everyone hopes to
hear him say something thrilling some day,
invitations were rushed. Women who came
early were shooed away from the balcony
doors when the lions were taking soup. The
Mayoress and Mrs. Hughes received the
guests in the blue-and-gold boudoir suite
that was fitted up for the Hennessy dame
ever so long ago. No other Mayoress has
yet had a chance to queen it there. Mrs.
Hughes was in lettuce green satin, cut very
low. She covered her shoulders with a
green ninon coat until she reached the bal-
cony. There the plush balcony-edge gave
the usual bath-tub effect. Mrs. Pearce was
muffled almost to the ears in black tulle.
Lesley Madden, l-epx-esenting mamma, who
is still a.n invalid, came along in a rising-
sun of jet wrapped tightly l-ound her plump
figure and a flare of glacd skirts below. She
bound her iron-grey locks in a chain of
plum-sized pieces of jet. and remarked that
she “came to hear Hughes and dad speak.”
Dad was below in the banqueting hall,
resplendent in gold lace, black broadcloth
and decorations. The Best dame wore a
deliciously cut gown of black. Alfred
Deakin’s sister and Mrs. Ivy Deakin-
Brookes were together. State Commandant
Williams’s wife was in black, and her hus-
band wore khaki and spectacles. Mrs.
Mclnerney appeared in a debutante sort of
dress made of white tulle and blue ribbons.
Mrs. Henry Stead favored the pale blue of
flapper blossoms. Alderman Jeffries’s
daughter paraded the balcony in Mechlin
lace-flounces and gold shoes. Mrs. Fred
Eggleston, in a purple sash, gazed admiringly
at her barrister-husband dining below in a
private’s khaki. The High Court Isaacs
family sat in lovely clothes.
There were heaps of women, and they
crowded the large Council Rooms, where food
was spread and the flowing claret-cup bowl
went round. At a signal from downstairs
they rose like a flock of parrots and flew.
At the doorway there was a sudden hold-up,
and the massed circus started to fret
and plead not to be pushed over the balus-
trades. “Mrs. Pearce! Where is Mrs.
Pearce?” wailed the halted advance guax-d.
The holy oi’der of precedence forbade the
mob to move until the very small lady was
extracted from the avalanche of femininity.
When they were all in their places the
gallant male ci’eatures drank a toast to them
and bowed with courtly grace. Then the
Gaud Mayor rose in his might and his
l’abbit-skin and made a flowery speech. Pre-
mier Peacock, who has turned snow-white in
a single session, bellowed out another speech,
and Alderman Crichton boomed from the
centre of the room. Then at last little
Hughes took the floor when by rights he
should have taken the table. A canopy I’ose
above him to throw his voice to the four
corners of the gi-eat room and give a semi-
regal air. First we had Hughes speaking
with such eixergy that his three diamond
shii't-buttons flashed lightning; next, Hughes
received with handkerchief waving and
applause; then Hughes mumbling into his
empty glass that the Government had decided
to send the called-ups, “after a month’s train-
ing, back to their homes.” Not a sound came
from the whole room in response to that
remark.
I forgot to tell you that pi’oceedings opened
with an organ storm. The Hennessy dame
loves that thunderous giant down to the last
little reed that twitters happily when the
great siege pipes have gone to sleep.
Thei’e is a romantic mystery suri’ounding
the foot-trouble of Governor Stanley. It
may be the after-effects of a desperate
attempt at leg-pulling, or Capital may have
trampled on it while leaving the country.
The confidential secretary can only breathe
the happy fact that his Ex. is getting on “as
well as can be expected”—the ancient, sick-
room retort that makes the sympathetic in-
quirer who has expected nothing feel stupid.
The potentate has had a slight operation on
his foot, and may be “about” again in a few
days.
The Best People are wailing and gnash-
ing their choicest factory-made teeth, because
Governor Stanley refuses to allow them to
play two-up, fan-tan or thimble and-pea
when they come to the French Oi’phans’
Fete at Stouington. He has been coaxed,
cajoled and thi’eatened in a respectful
manner; but to vei’y little pui’pose. The
only concession he has made is with regard
to raffles, which he will allow on the ground
that nobody buys a ticket in a selfish
gambling spirit. Lesley Madden had a
beautiful spinning-jenny ready, which was
relied on to make so much money that you
have to blink when the sum is mentioned.
Sassiety counted so much on getting a haul
when the fete was booked for the vice-i’egal
home; but this official set against gambling
makes it about as alluring as a Methodist
tea-meeting.
At the Stonington fete, > to be held on
Saturday, there is to be a big dancing scene
in which 60 children will take part. Jennie
Brenan has composed certain contortions
and labelled them “Titania’s Court.” The
Stanleys’ eldest daughter will be Titania.
The Geoffrey Syme beauty-children are also
involved; also June Baillieu, Betty Fair-
bairn and Baby Wilma, delight of the Mount
Schank Clarkes.
Adelaide’s epistle:—
Saturday, the day of the children’s fete at Austral
Gardens in aid of the League of Loyal Women and
the prisoners of war in Germany, showed a cheerful
balance-sheet, in spite of rain and a slight fall of
snow. In the circumstances a Noah’s Ark and
menagerie of animals were the strongest magnet.
Mrs. A. A. Lendon and Miss Peacock were the
organisers of the show, and a whirling time they
had when the downpours necessitated a transference
of the entertainments from the garden to the veian-
dahs and the spare kitchens. The plunder amounted
to nearly £4OO, the expenses to a modest £2l.
On the same date there was an Australia Day fes-
tivity at Stirling, and -patriots paddled about in
the rain looking for the sports and the procession
and colliding with drenched damsels hawking sod-
den collection-boxes. There was a Queen competi-
tion, of course. It took sedate S. Aus. some time
to adopt the idea, but now there is barely a suburb
that is not playing up the ruse.
At the annual meeting of the Children’s Hospital
the news was broken that the expenditure for the
year had exceeded the income, which made it neces-
sary to lift some of the invested funds. The presi-
dent’s job is shouldered by C. H. Angas, in suc-
cession to the late Chief Justice Way. Like his
father, J. H. Angas, C.H. has always taken a keen
interest in this hospital.
Dr. H. S. Newland (lieut.-colonel by rank) has
been appointed to the command of a general hospi-
tal in England. Since he got busy he has toiled in
Egypt, Lemnos, Gallipoli and France. His wife
steamed for England some weeks ago, leaving her
small family in charge of her mother, Mrs. Lindon.
We are pluming ourselves on the Servian Order
of the White Eagle that has come to one of
Admiral Creswell’s sons. His mother was a Stow.
The Adelaide Women’s Choir gave its first concert
at the Town Hall on Saturday evening. Governor
Galway was there, likewise the Mayor and Mrs.
Isaacs, but mere citizens were turned away by
baker’s dozens.
One of Josiah Symon’s daughters was aboard the
Arabia when the steamer was torpedoed. The Symon
girl was on her way to join a sister and take
up V.A.D. work in England. Like all the other
passengers, she has been landed safe and well.
The recreation hall added to the Home for Incui -
ables at Fullarton, thanks to a bequest of £6OOO
from the late M. McNally, gave the Governor another
chance to turn a key. Lady Galway, who returned
from Melbourne on Wednesday, went round with the
hat.
Copies of Lady Galway’s Belgian Book have been
sent to King George and King Albert. Of the 2000
copies printed nearly 1000 have been sold.
Lady Helen has promised to present
trophies for a knitting tournament at some
date not yet fixed. All' exhibits are to
become the property of the 15th Infantry
Brigade Comforts Fund, and must reach the
depot, Olderfleet, by November 20. Her Ex.
is also going to a gift afternoon for the
Brigade at Scots’ Kirk Hall, on November
23.
Mrs. Gordon Lane, who used to be Kath
Mcllwraith, is about again, after her severe
illness of last year, looking as handsome as
ever. She has taken a swagger flat at St.
Kilda, and leaves hubby to chase motor-
cars and other animals with his old passion
for speed.
Dr. Charlie Lempriere’s one fair daughter,
Clare, is just engaged to an Apple Islander
in khaki, who is now off to the Front.
Sal Druitt, who left the maternal sheep
acres in N. S. Wales this year and tripped to
England, sends woi’d that she is on the way
to the altar with an attractive young man
who is a rubber planter at Singapore in times
of peace. He is now in khaki and bound for
the trenches. Sal is a beauty, and that awful
thing known as “well connected.”
Solicitor Gaden’s wife from Darling Point
i 3 staying with the Ilope-Campbells at
Toorak. She is doing the Race meetings
with them, for though the visitor has had
her share of war losses she refuses to sit
at home and weep.
Mrs. Sheridan, who was a Hordern, you
know —cousin of the Hordern family—has
crossed over from Sydney with her two girl-
ettes to spend the hot months in Melbourne.
She stays with her sister, Mrs. lioystone
Davey, who is something of an invalid these
days.
Reggie Bloomfield, Rupert Clarke’s partner
in rubber planting, had hardly got back with
his wife from a jaunt to Java before he was
off again to Papua to see how the trees were
getting along. Mrs. Reggie preferred to
grasswidow in the city this trip.
A whisper went round Flemington on Cup
Day that Connie Waugh had come quietly
back, and was establishing herself at the old
address in Queen’s-road. She did not put in
an appearance on Cup Day, however. Maybe
the lovely lady is carefully unpacking some-
thing startling for the delayed Oaks and
Steeplechase meetings.
Mrs. Ranclaud, from Sydney, gave a race-
time party at the Oriental last week. Lesley
Madden, in black fui’belows, exchanged
bazaar notes with Mrs. Barry Thompson,
who tried hard to talk seriously about stalls
under cute blue millinery. Mrs. Whyte sup-
ported a duck of a hat over her edge of the
table; it was pink-mauve above and pastel-
blue below, and it carried baby pink roses
flattened out iu blotting-paper style. Only
an I.W.W. could have hated it. The hostess
wore an adorable di’ess of ninon in what the
dress artists call midnight blue, and the
fiuffiness was weighted down with a scented
necklace of pressed cloth-of-gold roses. Have
you seen these new scented necklaces? They
are made of perfumed flowers papier-mached
into strings of miniature blooms. The little
Tewkesbury girl, just back from England,
had one. of buttercups strung round her
sailor collar. Mrs. Russell Gabb, the lovely
Adelaide girl who now belongs to Mel-
bourne, was wrapped in amber and brown
furs and brown ninon. The frocks have
got to blossom, you know, even if festivities
are cut down.
The dresses and hats displayed the wearers
on the comfy leather seats of the Oriental
lounge on Cup Tuesday—the hopeless day
when Flemington was all water and mud.
The women first sipped teas and then got
on to cocktails. At last the orchestra was
so compelling that the whole company, led
by the eager flappers, began to dance.
The Greens, Mrs. William and daughter
Florence, will give their usual annual ball,
despite the war. It happens on December
6, and will include the usual astonishing
display of pocket Gendes and Pavlovas. Of
course the proceeds go to the patriotic funds.
Mrs. Dyer, of Camberwell, got going pro-
perly after her return from a health trip
to the Islands. She is a dyed-in-the-wool
Red Crosser, so when outsiders saw a big
party in progress at Torryburn they knew
what it was for. Mrs. Robin helped to sell
things, and her bride-daughter from Donald
made a set of silk underwear that sold for
£4O. The pale hostess, in pale blue and
white, had engaged a heap of entertainers.
Kirby sang and brought a gifted girl pupil,
Nita Delaney. She has a fine soprano voice.
Norah Delaney (no relation) was also there,
coaxing the piano. May Griffiths, in fawn
fixings, played and sang prettily. Girls
sold flowers and sweets to the loyal Haw-
thorners, who rolled up in great numbers and
dripped lots of money into the fancy stalls.
A pretty little stage lady of uncommon
talents has just felt obliged to refuse the
plump hand and considerable income of a
popular singer well known in these parts.
The swain has long been regarded as a
callous bachelor with a preference for laying
up treasure on earth. But Miss Footlights
wrought a rapid change in the artist, and
when her fluffy charm had soaked into his
system he was a hot and happy stranger to
his former self. Heat begat no heat in this
case, however.
The Louis Essons, who are leaving for
the Neutral States on the 22nd, were fare-
welled by an inky and artistic crowd at the
Cafd Denat on Friday. Vance Palmer pre-
sided and px-oposed the health of the depart-
ing pair. Louis nervously read a reply at
breakneck speed, and his handsome wife (she
is a B.A. with a yearning for the footlights)
made a very presentable speech. Dave Dow.
Ted Dyson and Hal Gye also made them-
selves heard, and some neat Australian
parodies wei'e read by Maisie Maxwell and
the chairman.
The fine Maoriland kinemacolor pictures
have struck silver at the Auditorium, and a
“limited season” has been joyfully extended
to enable the rest of Melbourne to roll along
and do homage. Glaciers, snow peaks, water-
falls and blue and green torrential rivers
are thrown on the screen, and the Maori
dances and bathes and does his or her hot-
spring cooking as if you were an expensive
tourist. Then you see wheatfields with
busy white Maorilanders working in them,
and after that more scenery. By way of
contrast you are next shown the delicate
evenness of English scenery: and then the
King and Queen, in all their royal pomp and
circumstance, visiting the placid, sweet town
of Stratford-on-Avon.
Miss H. liwine, a sister of the Iceberg, is
showing her paintings in the Upper Hall of
the Athenaeum. Mrs. Walter Withers is
superintending the exhibition, so it will be
well marfaged. Miss Irvine studied painting
in Paris, and has many sketches made in
France and Ireland. Justice aßeckett is
particularly interested in the show, which he
opened on Monday. It \yill continue until
November 23, and 25 per cent, of the profits
go to the French orphans.
The Arts and Crafts are having a great
show—the finest they’ve yet held in Mel-
bourne. All crafts are well represented. Lady
Stanley is exhibiting a fine piece of enamelling
—a crucifix made by Alexander Fisher, a big
London craftsman. This specialist taught
the industrious Lady Carmichael. Victor
Hood, who has a pretty taste in art values,
bought a piece of bronze as a Christmas pre-
sent for Carmichael himself. The precious
thing was made by an Australian, and is a
kingfisher with the colors cunningly fused.
Mrs. George Chirnside isn’t coming back to
domesticity and Werribee just yet, after all.
George Chirnside got a cable telling him that
Audrey was being useful in London —-she has
a job at the Admiralty. Other Victorian
maidens are useful there, too. Athol Levy
has a billet in the same place; so has Wynne
Laidley, of Sydney. By the way, her engage-
ment is being whispered about.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Win”: “Konini” asks for information re
the fire-walking by Fijians and other South Sea
Islanders (B. 12/10/’l6). The ceremony in Fiji is
much the same as that recently gone through at
Cook Island, but only one tribe, the natives of Bega
(pronounced Bengga) can do the trick, and they will
perform only on rare occasions. A hollow in the
earth, about 20ft. in diameter, is floored with stones,
arid on these stones a monster fire is kept burning for
several hours, sometimes days, till the bed is at
a white heat. The coals are then raked off, and,
after throwing in some hibiscus leaves, the fire-
walkers, barefooted and arrayed in gala dress, step
calmly into the oven and walk around two or three
times without being scorched in any way. The ordeal
over, a great feast is prepared, and pigs and sundries
are cooked on the stones. English spectators have
examined the firewalkers’ feet before and after the
furnace act, but the how and the why remain a
mystery. I have been told that trusting whites have
ventured on the fire-walk holding the hands of the
natives, and have suffered no ill effects. According to
the Fijian legend one Galita, whilst digging in a hole
wherein abode a monstrous eel, unearthed a dwarf in
human form. He decided to serve up his captive as
an offering to the storyteller of the village, but the
dwarf begged to be set free, and in return for his
liberty gave Galita and his followers the power to
found a famous practice. Forthwith they prepared
an oven, and on that day the mystery was taught.
SOMETHING TO BE FEARED.

“Old ■Recruiter” • rpu. _ ~ .
O d Recruitei . The narrative in a
recent Buiaetin revives other memories of the
schooner Peri. In the early ’7o’s a cutter named the
Miva was making a trip from Levuka to Taviuni, a
distance of 90 mile% carrying Solomon Island
laborers and several Europeans, mostly Ballarat or
Jldbou™ people, amongst them beibg Wa,barton,
Kington, Robinson and Williams. When they were
well out in the Koro Sea the savages, being all on
deck and having their tomahawks with them, rushed
the Europeans and axed them and the Fijian
crew. The master, old Bill Banks, and the steers-
man, Marika, were either knocked overboard or they
jumped over, and some of the Solomon Islanders
jumped after them. A fearful fight in the water
ended in the pursuers being- killed. In the meantime
the cutter had sailed away and was lost sight of.
The nearest land was the island of Nairai, distant
about 10 or 15 miles, and side by side the white man
and the native started to swim for it. All that night
and the following day they swam, and some time in
the afternoon the native reached the shore. But old
Bill went down' somewhere in the passage through the
reef. As soon as the news of the massacre reached
Levuka parties went out in all directions searching
for the Miva. One of the vessels was the Xariffa, the
one-time crack yacht of Port Jackson. She was sailed
by an old Sydney yachtsman named Carrington
Croft. Meanwhile the murderers on the Miva, sailing
westward, got stranded on the Island of Biva, in the
Yas-awa Group. They all got on shore and took to
the bush. That’s where the Peri came in. Her men,
with the assistance of the native inhabitants, captured
most of the runaways and took them to Levuka. That
was the schooner’s first introduction to tragedy. The
second and last phase occurred shortly afterwards,
when she was chartered to bring in another load of
recruits. Captain Bergen was put in charge of her,
and he impressed me as a sort of Sunday-school
teacher. He spoke rather strongly against keeping
black brethren below, and said he did not think it
necessary to have an armed sentry on deck. I
warned him and his mate, Bill Orr, of the class of
passengers they were likely to have, and advised
them, as soon as the men came on board, not only to
put them below, but to keep on the hatch-gratings,
and not to allow more than two savages on deck at
a time. I don’t know whether the advice was taken,
but at one of the outer islands a load of 70 canni-
bals was shipped, and the Peri put to sea, bound
for Levuka and never reached there. Some two years
afterwards I heard casually that a vessel answering
her description had been picked up off the coast of
Australia with a few living natives and the remains
of many dead on board.
“Hambidge” : From between the sheets on
the verandah this is the little comedy I saw played
by Australian artists. Around the corner of the house
came the cat with a proud curl in his whiskers and
a mouse in his teeth. Horace, the jackass, stood on
the edge of the path apparently fast asleep. Tom
deposited his load
on the gravel and
kept turning his
head and yawning
as if he were too
tired to interest
himself further in
the matter. Then
the mouse would
make a rush for the
hedge; but the
suddenly-alert cat
was too smart for
him. Several times
the prisoner tried
to escape, on each
occasion edging
nearer to Horace.
Finally Tom started
to wash his face in
a leisurely sort of
way, and the mouse
got right to the
feathered party.
With a single
movement of his
head Horace seized
the titbit in his
beak and had
almost swallowed
it when the cat
looked round and
missed his pro-
perty. Tom was
absolutely bewil-
dered for a
moment, then he
saw the last half-
inch of tail vanish
down Jack’s throat.
That hard case
never moved an
inch except to immediately close his eyes again, and
do everything in the sleep-line except snore. Puss
considered things for a moment and then walked
calmly over to Horace, spat in his face and retreated
dejectedly around the house. Even that insult
didn’t move Horace. He didn’t open his eyes for
over half an hour.

“F.C.S.”: Re shearing tallies. My memory
leads me to think that the best performance by any
one man in a shed extending over 16 weeks and
averaging over 1000 per week for the full period
was put up by one Denny Noonan. He was alleged
to have shorn 16,400. That was about 1888. Denny
learned the art from Alf Wagner, of Maoriland. I’d
like .to have the figures verified, and I don’t mind
offering my second-best motor-car as a reward.

“Papuan Settler”: I have travelled over
most of the South Seas, and done a great deal of
swimming therein, with the not-to-be-avoided con-
sequence of a cut or two from the reefs. It is
quite true that coral cuts are exceedingly dangerous.
Small wonder, as they are usually filled with decay-
ing animal matter from the coral insect (if it is
an insect; it used to be, but fashions change). I
have heard of limbs being lost from the effects of
a coral cut, and seen a good many people going about
tied up for much longer than was convenient or
agreeable to them. But I have never had an hour’s
trouble myself, because I know the remedy. I have
torn one leg nearly open on a mid-Pacifie reef, when
dragged over it by a sudden swell, and it healed up
(the leg, not the reef) as cleanly as a cut made
with a surgeon’s sterilised knife. The reason: When
I got home I poured oil of turpentine over it. It
made things lively for 10 minutes, but there was
no trouble. Turpentine, or the oil thereof, will
cure any coral cut before it has time to begin
troubling. It will also cure the sting of most
stinging fishes, if the stung part is slightly opened
with a knife-point and the turpentine dropped in.
* * * *
“Cochabamba”: Concerning the outbreak
of Irish-Australians in Bolivia, mentioned by “Ver-
dad” (B. 12/10/’l6). In Bolivia you can sometimes
take liberties with the police, but with the military,
no! The yarn about five or six amateur “bad men”
running out a detachment of soldiers is bosh. The
man who set fire to the Indian village (an Irish-
Australian) cleared as hard as horses could take him
to Cochabamba when he got sober enough to travel.
He afterwards made a poor attempt to hold up the
railroad camp at Aguas Calcentes. After emptying
his two guns at my timekeeper and hitting him only
twice, he found the keys of the safe (containing
65,000 dollars) were with us out on the line. There
was • dynamite in the camp, but I guess he was too
scared to use it;- so he and his pal (a Yank) took
a mule and two horses from the corral and beat it
to the hills, full of whisky, and loaded up with
enough ammunition to kill all Bolivia. They
appeared back at the camp within 24 hours, mounted
on two burros, with their legs shackled underneath,
and escorted by a sub-lieutenant of police and half
a dozen Indians. There are “bad men” in S. America,
but they are of different clay to the men “Verdad”
writes about. When they shoot, which is seldom,
they don’t waste ammunition, and they never drink
too much whisky when on business.

“Pick”: Hares forsake rabbit-infested
lands simply because among the sweetmeats of the
fields there isn’t room for two prolific colonies, and
the fellow with the best punch remains in possession.
A buck hare, when there is a lady in the case, will
fight furiously, but though much the larger animal
his name is mud when the tough rabbo comes along.
Notwithstanding the scoffing of “A. Borig” (B:

BONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Eden, so the Scriptures say,
Old Adam lived alone a day.
It is agreed by learned men
That Eden was an Eden then.
To furnish Eve, the first of crones,
She had the pick of Adam’s bones.
The sex is perfectly content
With that unhappy precedent,
And woman with exceeding skill
The bones of man is picking still!

motor touring are yours in fullest measure
in a car whose satisfying completeness
delights you at every point —the Mitchell Six.
You will find in the Mitchell the realisation of
your ideal of what a car should be.

Mitchell is the easiest-riding car known. You
will be pleasantly surprised by the wonderful
smoothness with which it passes over rough
roads without your feeling the roughness in the
least.
You will never require to use a shock absorber
on the Mitchell. Its exclusive Bate cantilever
springs make it so remarkably easy riding.
Your first glance will tell you that the Mitchell
is built on lines of surpassing beauty.
Actual experience will bring home to you the
absolute reliability of the Mitchell engine.
Fitted with magneto ignition, it is unfailing in
the most severe conditions of hard service.
You have every convenience in this car, includ-
ing self-starter and engine-driven tyre-pump.
The long wheel base gives you ample roominess
and comfort; the matchless endurance and
strength of the Mitchell mean full security.
The great efficiency of the Bate-built engine
makes the Mitchell very economical in upkeep —
it goes twenty-five miles and over to the gallon
of benzine.
The price here is lower than the price of any car
of equal cost in America.
Call in during the week and ride the-Mitchell
yourself, or write for full information.

ACID STOMACHS ARE DANGEROUS.
NINE-TENTHS OF ALL STOMACH
TROUBLES SAID TO DUE TO
ACIDITY.
A PHYSICIAN’S ADVICE ON CAUSE
AND CURE.
A famous physician, whose successful re-
searches into the cause and cure of stomach
and intestinal diseases have earned for him
an international reputation, said in the
course of a recent lecture that nearly all in-
testinal troubles, as well as many diseases of
the vital organs, were directly traceable to a
deranged condition of the stomach, which in
turn was due nine times out of 10 to excessive
acidity, which not only irritated and inflamed
the delicate lining of the stomach, but also
set up , gastritis and stomach ulcers. It is
interesting to note that he and his colleagues
have secured remarkable results by the use of
ordinary bisurated magnesia, which, by
neutralising the acidity of the food, removes
the source of the trouble. He contends that
it is as foolish to treat the stomach itself
as it would be for a man who stepped on a
tack to rub liniment on the foot without
first removing the tack. Remove the tack
and the foot will heal itself—neutralise
the acid and stomach troubles will dis-
appear. Irritating medicines and medical
treatments are useless, so long as the con-
tents of the stomach remain acid; remove
the acidity, and there will be no need for
medicine —the inflamed lining of the stomach
wilj then heal itself. Sufferers from acidity
should get a small bottle of bisurated mag-
nesia from their chemist, and take half a
teaspoonful in a quarter of a glass of hot or
cold water after meals, repeating in 15
minutes, if necessary, this being the dose
which the doctor has found most efficacious
in all cases. — Advt.
Martin’s Pills are sold by W. H. Soul,
Pattinson & Co. Ladies recommend them.
Be sure you get the genuine with the
signature. Martin, Chemist, Southampton.*

“Buly True”: Who has seen our dusky
brother produce moisture by the manipulation of
“rain-stones” ? Don’t know what part the stones
play, because Binghi keeps it a close secret. I
remember once, in the midst of a severe drought in
Queensland, I had a black for a mate at a job
of pulling out bogged cattle. I hinted to Yarr.y
that it was about up to the local rain doctor to
get busy, because if he didn’t there would soon
be nobody left to get wet. As makeweight I threw
in a remark that I fancied the doc. was getting
too old and couldn’t make rain for nuts. I shall
never forget the look of scorn Yarry gave me as
he answered: “By cripes, boss, you big feller fool
just like it all about white feller. Doctor maket
it rain just as well as ever he can only he bin too
plurry lazy.”
* * * *
“X.Y.” : The Red Cross has had returned
from abroad, as of no practical value, a bundle of
sticks which bears the following label: “From the
Oodnadatta branch Red Cross; ten walking-sticks
made from mulga wood by Gipps Charlie, abo-
rigine.” Perhaps black brother is proof against the
virus carried by this venomous wood, or it may
be the only wood available at Oodnadatta, but
neither the white man nor his domestic animals
enjoy any immunity, and loss and suffering caused
by wounds made by this wood are common features
of life in the West. The vegetable is, on account
of its yield of edible foliage, the most valuable
tree in the dry country; but a puncture by a
splinter is almost as dangerous as the bite of a
venomous snake. Green or dry, the timber is poison-
ous. It has a tendency to break with a jagged
fracture, and the projecting splinters become as
hard as bone, and will pierce a strong boot or
legging. When a horse is staked about the legs
the chances are about five to one that he will be
permanently crippled. Even when the splinter is
a small one and is extracted promptly swelling and
inflammation set in at once, and usually develop
to such a degree that the joint in the vicinity of
the wound becomes lastingly injured. In the mulga
country every holding has a set of probes and
forceps for extracting these splinters. On the
North Coast the weed Stinking Roger affects man
and beast in a similar way, but in its case the
leaves are noxious also, although horses eat the
young shoots with immunity and apparent relish.
A Sydney solicitor, a member of a well-known legal
firm, has a stiff leg at present from a splinter of
mulga with which he came in contact during a
vacation on a West Queensland station. He came
very near losing his life as well as the use of his
knee-joint.
* * * *
“Bill Bedsox” : It is apparently not gener-
ally known that mice will not violate the neutrality
of flour that is packed in bags made of stout white
cotton duck. A couple of years ago, having a
couple of yards remnant from a tent, I converted
it into flour bags for the camp, and though mice,
rats and ’possums have at various times used them
as bunks, there have been no marks of curiosity as
to the flavor of the contents on the part of the
pests. Try it, you bushwhackers; it saves sifting.

Koikoi-las”: “Papua,” in a recent
Bulletin, is wrong in attributing the murders of
Clark (prospector) and Green (magistrate) on the
Mambare River to the influence of old Bushimai.
Bushi, though a chief of consequence on the lower
river, had no say amongst the upper river people
who were responsible for the crimes, and
was, in fact, many miles aw r ay when they
took place. Green, his police and prison-
ers were murdered at the instigation of
one of his own constables, a man named
Dumai, and that native with another
served a few years in gaol for the act.
The other murderers were pardoned by
the influence of sundry meddlers high in
the missionary swindle. As a result,
Dumai and his unpunished accomplices
now walk about their villages inflaming
the young bucks of the rising generation
with blood-curdling stories of the way.
they “wiped out the Guv’mint.” “Papua’s”
yarn that Bushimai ate his fellows after
their escape from the Port Moresby gaol
is an oft-repeated fable. As a matter of
fact, only two of them perished, and those
went under from exhaustion ; the others,
including Bushi’s son, Oia, now a sergeant
of police, subsisted on food they got from
the many native gardens on the way home.
* * * *
“Melos”: “Quondong” (B. 12/10/’l6)
offers a good recipe tor bald knees in horses, but I
believe mine is a better one: onion juice and gin
(the spirit, of course), and put in as much of the
former as you can find patience to extract. (P.S. —
Don’t show this par. to the girl, or she’ll put herself
into quarantine and try it on her own dear head.)
“Te Pana”: Living in a land of cocoa
palms one wonders why it is that half the popula-
tion is not annually exterminated by falling nuts,
yet I’ve never heard of a brown or a white getting
the pass-out check in this way. Planters say that
most of the fruit that falls does so during the
night when the dew has relaxed the fibres of the
stalks—which might be all right but for the trifle
that damp tightens up fibres instead of relaxing
them. The native explains that it is the will of
the Almighty which prevents sudden death, and
that’s about as good as any other solution.
THEY PARTED COMPANY.
BOSS: “What the have you done with your horse?"
JACKEROO: “ Didn't you tell me if I got bushed to let the horse go and he would find
the way? Well, I let him go, but I couldn’t keep up to the beggar."
“ J.Z.R.” : Down on Monaro (N.S.W.)
jumbucks look fat and well, but when they are
driven, a few promptly snuff out. Common idea is
that they have eaten aged watercress. What about
it?
“Dead Finish”: I have seen as many as
60 horses yoked up for a 'pull across the sandy
Black Sam’s Crossing on the Flinders River (N.Q.),
but these were made up from different teams. Here-
abouts the average strong string is 27 or 28. As
for loads, Tommy Wilkinson’s 21 tons 14cwt. of
greasy wool drawn by 30 hornies will take some
beating. This was taken from Tarbrax station to
Talmoe wool scour (N.Q.) last July. The same
bullocky reckons he can pull 30 tons with his mob,
but the shire council has an eye on him.
“Bad Australian” : In the bad old days in
Australia the mainstay of the bullocky was green-
hide and stringy-bark. The Paraguayan bullocky
hasn’t got the stringy, but he worries along fairly well
without it. His dray is home-made, body, wheels
and axle entirely of wood, and the squealing and
skraaking of his greaseless outfit has done more
towards filling the madhouse than any other cir-
cumstance in Paraguay. The wheels are about six
feet high, and the sides of the vehicle are thatched
with sedge or slatted with split bamboo up to
where the arched cover springs away. The cover,
tilt or lid is generally made of greenhide, but some
of the more enterprising persuaders use zinc sheet-
ing. The team consists of a pair of polers and a
like number of leaders. The polers have a long,
single yoke laid with an end on each “bull” just
where the neck joins the head and securely lashed
to the animals’ horns with greenhide thongs. Bows
are not used in this country, as the “bulls,” being-
used to the head pull, will not stand up to the
bow. About midway on the yoke there is a
mortise which corresponds to another in the end
of the pole. A strong greenhide lashing is reeved
through these mortises and the yoke is made fast
to each side of the pole at a distance of about nine
inches, and a bamboo outrigger is fixed to keep
the motive power from “boring in.” The leaders
are rigged in the same way as regards the yoke.
The gear which connects them to the polers con-
sists of a long hardwood bar mortised at each
end. One end is lashed to the leaders’ yoke and
the other to the pole by passing a greenhide rope
or thong through the mortises and making fast as
in the first case. More than four “bulls” are
rarely used, as the greenhide tackle will not bear
a greater strain. These folk don’t understand any-
thing about the Australian bullocky’s tackle, which
has been known to shift the creek in which the
waggon was bogged. The Paraguayan puncher
doesn’t use a whip. He has a bit of wire with a
loop on the end suspended from the front end of
his ridge pole. Through this he passes a long
limber bamboo with a four-inch nail in the small
end, and when he wants to accelerate the time-
table he takes hold of the other end of the pole,
sets it properly in rest, takes aim, and with a
short, sharp jab delivers the ironmongery where he
expects to get the best result. Then he lights
another cigar (every human in Paraguay smokes
cigars and some seem to live on ’em) and lapses
into a torpid state until the next bad stretch of
road rouses him. Working-bullocks in Paraguay
are a small, scabby, tick-infested lot. They get
horrible treatment and are worked until they are
eaten up by the tick or fall to pieces from poverty
and neglected wounds —and a neglected wound in
Paraguay soon means a festering mass of maggots.
HIS QUEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dan Wilks had never seemed the sort
Who’d go away to fight,
Wherefore I murmured: “Well, I’m blessed!”
When Private Wilks, in khaki dressed,
I met the other night.
“You see, it’s like this,” Dan explained;
“Last week I went to ’ear
A bloke ’oo made a speech an’ said
That thousands ’oo ’ad fought an’ bled
’Ad found a glorious bier.
“W’en ’e ’ad done ’is speech, I felt
1 could not stay be’ind.
This early-closin' leaves one dry ;
It's up to me to go an’ try
That boshter beer to find!”
VICTORIA.
