ALL MERCERS
Business, Bobbery, etc.
“Trot”: To illustrate the ways of Red
Tape the Age digs up a case found by R.
McC. Anderson in the Post Office. A coun-
try postmaster wanted a table. The appli-
cation led to 12 departmental reports and
minutes, and the whole tremendous issue
was disposed of in seven weeks. But that
is nothing. The Maoriland railways once
delivered a bag of flour at the wrong
address. It was my flour and worth 4s. Bd.
I wrote about the matter, and in a week
got a reply which meant nothing. I wrote
again. More meaningless reply. I wrote
some more. With dignity and restraint I
consigned the railways to Hades and dashed
off an order for more flour. Eight months
afterwards the general manager sent lift,
of paper and four enclosures. The first
asked if I wanted 4s. Bd. and if so told me
to fill in the documents forwarded. I put
them by and said nothing. A week later
another document arrived telling me that
the general manager was awaiting an
answer. I still kept quiet. Then a tele-
gram came asking if I was dead. Continued
silence. More telegram. More quietness.
After a fortnight I addressed to the G.M. a
polite request to be told if my 4s. Bd. was
available, and if so would he buy me a
pair of fowls with the proceeds. Whereupon
stationery descended on the local post office
in a vast mass, fresh inquiries were started
after missing documents, and 14 months
later a crossed cheque for 4s. Bd, was
delivered and 14 receipts taken. I had to
change the cheque at the pub. The deal
cost 155., because McGinnis and O’Flaherty
were there. I heard afterwards that there
were 22 courts-martial over that flour, 17
dismissals or disratings from the service, and
14 promotions for vigilance. The files relat-
ing to everything connected therewith and
incidental thereto weighed 7i tons. About
the same time Skatsby shot one of my dogs,
and when I squeaked about it he gave me a
couple of quid and yelled that if I wasn’t
satisfied with that I could have a punch on
the nose. That incident closed without fur-
ther talk or notes.
* * * *
“B. & B.”: While “C. Fitzmaggot” (B.
2/1,1/’ 16) was worrying out the question of
margarine v. butter on Salisbury Plain,
Inter-State Commissioners Piddington, Swin-
burne and Lockyer (especially Lockyer)
have been working their machines overtime
on the same subject here in Melbourne. Said
Lockyer the other day: “As traders in butter
we in Australia have always discouraged the
making or use of margarine. But why
should we not have both in our shop win-
dow? The world demands margarine, and as
we have in and handy to Australia an un-
limited supply of the raw materials—tallow,
cocoanut oil, whale oil and other fats and
oils, and milk to mill the margarine with —
why shouldn’t we export the manufactured
article instead of sending away the raw
materials?” Incidentally, the Commission
took the evidence of W. A. Osborne, Pro-
fessor of Physiology at Melbourne Univer-
sity, who said margarine contains just as
much nutriment as butter, but is not as
readily digested, and is not nearly as good
a food for children. “But,” he added, “it
is a lot better than jam.”
* * * *
A plain business letter, showing what
England is doing:—
With regard to delivery generally we hope you will
impress upon your customers the extraordinary diffi-
culties under which we are laboring. Now that prac-
tically every able-bodied man who is fit for Class A
(general service), B 1 (garrison duty abroad), B 2
(labor units of regimental outdoor employment
abroad) or Cl (garrison duty at home) is being
called up for service, you can realise the position,
always bearing in mind that the men of military age
and of good physical capacity are the best workers,
and these are, of course, being drafted into the
army. This morning there is a notification that
all youths as they attain the age of 1.8 years and
seven months must report for duty on and after Octo-
ber 7, and also a further proclamation stating that all
boys born in 1899 may attest voluntarily, which will
bring in even some boys under 17 years of age, so.
that you can gather from this the extent to which the
manhood of the country is being mobilised for service,
and this must considerably interfere with and delay
production of goods.
No wonder Lloyd George begged Australia
not to loaf on the English factory, but to
leave off building monkey-cages for new
zoos and get to work on its serious require-
ments.

They did not all come out of the Ark at
Ararat (Vic.). When they have a political

“Murkah”: Does anyone know a good
method of discouraging spiders—the ones
that weave their homes about telegraph in-
sulators? Spider-webs cause two-thirds of
the trouble and delays on Australian lines,
during wet weather. The tangle of web
gathers the dust, and the whole, when wet,
or even only damp, becomes semi-conducting,
providing a track down which the current
leaks uselessly to earth. On coastal lines I
have seen this leakage so bad that the whole
of the current was lost, the line making
what is known as “dead earth.” One genius
suggested that a submarine cable be laid
along the coast with loops into the various
stations. He was either a humorist or a
damfool. What is wanted is a different
shaped insulator. The one now in use adver-
tises for such boarders as wasps and spiders,
providing shelter and foothold for all. But
if it were shaped like an inverted cone, with
the wire half hitching round a small ball
near the bottom, the house-hunting spider
would only be provided with somdiing
which the first shower of ram would Leep
clean. , *
“Billy T.”: The war profits on ctils
should be something enormous. I hc*e
they come within the scope of the Act. x
caul I mean is a thin skin covering the hea
and shoulders of an occasional newborL
infant. I was one of the occasionals. My
mother preserved that caul as a precious
possession. A certain number of friends,
acquaintances and relations knew °t its
existence. Behold how much ground a little
fact may cover! I have had application
from five States for small portions ot my
caul, all from relations or friends ot men
off to the wars. The popular idea, as you
probably know, is that a caul is the luckiest
thing a man could have had about him when
getting himself born. Originally the notion
was that a caul was an unfailing preventive
against drowning, and a whole caul was
deemed necessary to salvation. _ Now it is
believed the caul is a luek-bringer m all
cases and places, and the smallest scrap is
held to be effective. Some applicants who
can’t get caul say they will be content to
take locks of hair. I have no hair to spare,
and would give a whole bale of .luck-bringing
cauls for a sure cure for neuritis.
* * * *
“Ivoikoi-las” : “Kuku-kuku” (B. 10/8/T6)
apparently has not travelled out of sight
of Samarai and Port Moresby. In the
northern and western ends of Papua, thou-
sands of acres of the best virgin land m
the world, on navigable rivers and water-
ways, where native food is abundant, and
labor is available in unlimited numbers at
6s. per month, may be secured by agricul-
turists. Very little of this land has been
taken up, but the areas under cultivation
which are being developed at a minimum
cost have produced some of the most pro-
mising cocoanuts in the Territory. Before
allowing “Kuku-kuku’s” remarks to dis-
courage them, let intending investors . have
a look at the country on the Kumusi and
Opi rivers in Northern Papua and the
Kikori River in the West.

“S.”: One of my neighbors gave a very
good reason for voting “No,” although all
his sympathies ran the other way. . “If I
vote ‘Yes,’ ” he said, “young Cobble will have
to go. Then what about the 30 bob he owes
me?”
* * * *
“Homo”: Only tried munition-making
once. Was then a country nipper, and
shot was something' like Bd. a pound at the
local store. I had heard something of the
principles of the shot tower and was fully
convinced that, with a high enough drop
for the process, I could make all I wanted
for myself and might supply the other
blokes as well. I selected a tall tree in a
secluded part of the freehold, and labori-
ously established my factory in the branches.
It mostly consisted of a sheet of galvanised
iron and a tub of water placed beneath.
An old piece of sheet-lead furnished ample
raw material, and I soon had a fire up aloft
that started the metal running. The only
drawback was that it fell anywhere but
into the tub. Whilst I was trying to over-
come that difficulty, a sudden gust caught
the fire, and I did a scrambling fall into
the tub of water with the front of my shirt
ablaze. You can’t get me to exult over
Zeppelins burning in mid-air. I’ve been
there myself.
* «• * *
“Fleecer”: A certain hair fertiliser now
being widely advertised was “given a go”
by a bald friend of mine, with the usual
negative result. As he didn’t order a fresh
supply, he was soon bombarded with pathetic
queries from the “follow-up” department,
and finally was forwarded a bundle of blue-
tinged “test papers” and invited to moisten
his scalp with cold water and apply one
of the slips. If the contact turned the
paper pink it would prove that the “scalp
was in an acid condition,” which could be
remedied by using a special preparation.
That done, the hair raiser would produce
so many inches of wool per week. The poor
victim duly made the prescribed test in my
presence, and, of course, found the rosy
tint on the test paper. But his jaw fell
when I dipped another paper in the water
jug and saw it turn as pink as the first.
Why is it that the three-card trick man
promptly gets plain quod, while a fraud of
this sort is allowed to loot the bald till he’s
tired of taking the money?
* * * *
“Tiaki”: The Maoriland Wood Pulp and
Paper Manufacturing Co., Ltd., has obtained
reservations over 30,000 acres of bush on
the West Coast in the vicinity of Otira, and
the Government has set aside a further
30,000 acres, with a water-right for generat-
ing power. Samples of timber were recently
sent to the Cold Country to be pulped, and
as a result Maoriland is beginning to think
that it can make paper just as well as
the foreigner. Soon the erring Maori-
lander will be invited to appear at court
on a home-made summons, tinted blue by
a process said to be in possesion of a local
chemist.
* * * *
“F.N.T.”: The planting of the Pinus in-
signis in various parts of Victoria, now
being carried out by the Department of
Forests with some vigor, is a shortsighted
policy. The insignis grows in 25 years, as
against. 50 years for the more valuable
pines; but it sells cheaply and has no real
value in competition with the class of pines
which formed the bulk of the 87,000,000 ft.
super imported during the year ended
June 30 last. As a State enterprise it looks
like a gamble. The fact that local insignis
logs are to-day being bought by box- and
case-makers counts for little, since these are
times of abnormal freights.
* * * *
“8.T.” : He was at one of the stamp win-
dows of the Melbourne G.P.O. “Give me
ninepenn’orth of penny stamps, Miss,” he
said. The appearance of the girl inside
pleased him. “I say, you’re a bit of all
right,” he said. “What do you do with
yourself these evenings?” The girl dashed
down nine stamps and 3d. change, and was
haughtily silent. But the bad man was not
crushed. He pushed the 3d. back. “You’re
a nice girl,” he said. “I like to help on a
girl like you. Give me threepenn’orth
more!”
TOE LESSER EVIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My philosophic neighbor Fred
Is to a tearing vixen wed.
This afternoon in grief to me
His scratches he exposed. Said he:
“I’d tax you bachelors. Behold,
Here are my reasons manifold!
Since we must bear the nails, you Jacks
Should willingly endure the tax!”

NOTHING BUT HEAVY SWELLS.
THE GOOD GIRL : “ She’s got terribly high
notions, hasn't she?”
THE BAD MAN: “Not the word for it! She
wouldn't live near the sea for fear of
knowing a low tide.”
After the Libs and Labs we get a new
party—the Grabs.
Griffith, on resigning from the Holman
Ministry last week, had an interview with
Ins old tennis opponent, Gov. Strickland, and
explained the position. But for that explana-
tion there might not have been any political
crisis two or three days afterwards.
“Adelaide for Culture.” Join Adelaide
with Sydney, and spell it Kultur.
The president of the Melbourne Trades
Hall Council on the Coal Strike: —
The view of Laborites is that as coal is a public
utility and is indispensable to the carrying on of
industry and the ordinary avocations of life, it
should be nationalised. If that had been done the
present trouble would not have occurred. National
control is especially applicable in time of war,
when it is necessary to conserve to the utmost degree
the resources of the nation.
It is the very ABC of democratic doctrine,
and only few plutocrats and unteachable
Tories no», deny it. But Victoria has a
State coal-mine at Wonthaggi, and the
workers there go on strike oftener than in
almost any other mine or works in Australia.
In this latest trouble, though they had no
quarrel, they promptly struck at the bid-
ding of the Newcastle authorities. Where,
then, is the guarantee of “national safety”
in Government ownership of coal-mines? It
is the spirit of the workers that needs
nationalising first.
HIS MASTER’S VOICE.
THE PUP: “Pm not goin’ to let this machine
dictate to me any longer."
The N.- S. Wales Assembly is quite cynical
about some of its most unpleasant habits.
Once, while Durack was speaking to his cen-
sure motion, the House fairly rocked with
laughter. Looking unusually solemn, the
new Labor leader said that he meant to make
a very grave charge against the Holman
Government. This Government, lie under-
stood, intended to gerrymander the next elec-
tions by shifting large bodies of men to
public works in some electorates and away
from others. It was just there the storm
of laughter broke. For everybody re-
membered that six years ago, when Hol-
man forecasted “a vigorous works policy,”
some jobs were found to be urgently neces-
sary in the very electorate which Durack
was running for. And even then he only
won by a narrow majority.
Another curse has returned to the old fowl-
yard. When the Vic. session opened Pre-
mier Peacock promised among his earliest
measures an Industrial Peace Preservation
Pill, which was to hand out pretty solid
penalties to employees who struck work witk-
out first taking a secret ballot
of those concerned. But the
Trifler was bluffed into the
belief that he couldn’t pass it,
so he . postponed the measure
sine die. Yet it would have
passed all right, and under it
those Wonthaggi miners who
have now helped to put the
State’s industrial undertakings
in a hole at almost a moment’s
notice would have been forced
to. give themselves time to think
things over and to realise the
full effect of what they were
doing. As to what they in-
tended to do when they set out
on their “sympathy” strike,
Heaven only knows: what they
have actually done is to knock
out one of the old arguments in
favor of State enterprises, cause
suffering to their wives and
children, baulk their mates of
financial assistance, help throw
out of a job thousands of other
workers, cause loss to many other disinter-
ested and friendly individuals, and indirectly,
but none the effectively, hqlp those who
would cheerfully slit their throats to-day and
will do them, out of a job to-morrow —the
Huns.
They were merely arguing the matter: —
“Liar and blackmailer.”
“Apostate.”
“You are a dirty beast.”
“Tell'him to go" to Hell.”
“I (aged 70) will pull your nose.”
Gome on and 111 settle vou.” (Proceedings
adjourned to allow him to do it.)
It all happened in the N. S. Wales Assembly
the other night, the question at issue being
whether it was not the duty of the House to
reward itself for its virtues and business-like
habits by hanging on to its job for at least
11 months more than it had been elected for.
As Premier Vaughan (S.A.) holds a seat
which the Labor party knows it can’t win
with a new candidate, he has been beckoned
into the ark with two colleagues. But
Vaughan will not go. “If there is any
drowning to be done,” he says, “I prefer to
drown with my loyal comrades.” Vaughan
is right. And the Labor party is wrong. It
was never so good and honest and useful as
when it had no hope of a majority in the
House. It lost most of its virtues when it
was prepared to do anything and swallow
anybody for the sake of numbers. It never
will be much good again until it spurns not
only the member but also the elector who has
to be bought.
A man named Walsh, an agent of the
A.M.P. Society, was informed during the
recent conscription campaign that he must
leave off taking an active part in it. Walsh
replied that he took part in the controversy
as a citizen and only in his own time, but
was instructed that that didn’t matter: —
The society itself has no politics, and the direc-
tors have consequently always laid it down that no
member of the society’s staff, either indoor or out-
door, shall take active part in any controversy on
matters in respect of which the community is
divided.
It is time, if this is so, that the directors of
the society laid down something else. The
humblest agent of the A.M.P. is no less a
citizen than the chairman of the society ; and
nobody protested or thought of protesting
against Alfred Meeks taking the platform as
an advocate of Conscription. Nor has any-
one the right to protest against general-
manager Teece getting on his feet at a meet-
ing of the Civil Service Co-operative Stores
Co. and leading the opposition to a proposal
which is dividing the three or four thousand
members and causing heated controversy. If
the A.M.P. were 10 times as big as it is it
would have no right to interfere with the
citizenship rights of Meeks or Teece or
Walsh ; and if it insists on doing it, it is an
institution far less worthy of the admiration
of this country than it has been hitherto
believed to be.
Canberra is a fairly horrible example, but
if the trans-continental railway is not worse
quite a lot of people are ready to swallow
a locomotive. The original estimate of the
work was £3,000,000. A million more than
that has been spent already. There is
another two years’ work ahead, and the cer-
tainty of the bill reaching £6,000,000. Either
the “facts” on which the line was author-
ised were grotesquely wrong or there has
been the most stupendous loaf in history.
And the cost of one or the other set of cir-
cumstances or of both is going to hang
round the neck of Australia for all time, like
the dead weight of a thousand alligators.
THE VOICE OF EXPERIENCE.
THE UNMARRIED ONE: “It is always better
to laugh than to cry, you know.”
THE MARRIED ONE: “ Not if you're trying to
manage a husband.”
The 19 “official” Labor members who re-
mained true to the N. S. Wales P.L.L. Ex-
ecutive over the conscription business are
not, to put it mildly, the ablest men in the
crowd. Durack, the leader, was chosen to
put the censure motion through simply
because he anticipated and moved to check-
mate the coalition schemes of the wily Pre-
mier. But when the real leader of the nine-
teeners is being chosen the claims of John
Storey, the four-hour leader of the party
during the recent dust-up with the Labor
conference, will have very strong support.
If the people of N. S. Wales are in such
an agitated state of mind that, they are not
to be trusted to use their votes sensibly, what
about their Savings Bank deposits? If their
votes are to be taken from them for 12 months
lest they misuse them, Parliament ought at
the same time, to be consistent, impound
their deposits for a year, too; There would
be quite as much justification for doing one
as the other.
A HARR DOER.
HE: “Is she so interesting to talk to?”
SHE: “Not to—about.”
Durack, the temporary leader of N. S.
Wales Laborites, still in the thirties, some-
what cuff-and-collary, smooth of skin, of
marked forbearance and urbanity, and nur-
tured in a pure atmosphere of Liberalism, is
about the last man in the world that would
be suspected of being a Labor leader. Dis-
daining to speak in the House for the first
12 months, his party was beginning to sus-
pect him of encouraging cold feet when he
broke ice by moving the Address-in-
Reply. With a view of giving him a lift
in his electorate he was then made a tempo-
rary chairman of committees, and soon
showed capacity. Lord Mayor Speaker
Meagher was a busy man, and Deputy-
Speaker Thrower was often acting. This
made Durack about of commit-
tees.. Presently, in the midst of a busy
session, Thrower w r as taken to hospital, and
Durack as a consequence became about
■f-Spgaker. His speech on the censure
motion was his second in Parliament. This
should be somewhere near a record. After
the way Disraeli took in the tipsters it is
safest to predict nothing. However, this
much might be said. Durack is level-headed,
and this' is saying a whole lot these times
when Laborism generally has straws in its
hair and corks on its hat.
The S. Aus. Assembly has passed the
second reading of a Bill forbidding instruc-
tion in schools to be given in any language
but English. It is aimed at the surprisingly
frequent German institutions in pountry
places, and CoomDe (lately editor of the
Labor daily Herald) was the onlv appar
ent dissentient. He called it “un-British."
which it probably is, “British” standing for
most things that are stupidly “generous” in
this connection.
The report of a committee of the Leg.
Council of S. Aus. on the Glenelg break-
water contract, which was increased from
£32,000 to £llO,OOO without Parliament
being consulted, is one of the most savage
things of its kind on record: —
No urgency had been shown for making a new
contract without the consent of Parliament.... The
contractors, because of their failure to complete the
contract, were permitted, without public or private
tendering, to obtain a new contract at their own
price and for their own design. They are being paid
for the work under the first contract on the basis
of the new contract, namely, £lO 9s. 2d. in lieu
of £6 12s. a cubic yard.... The Harbors Board
failed in its responsibility to the public.... The
statements made in Parliament in explanation of the
action of the Government have been proved by the
evidence to be inaccurate and misleading. . . .The
action of the Government was irregular and unneces-
sary, and will involve the State in a heavy annual
loss.
The thing cannot be left at that. If ever
there was a case for a judicial commission
this is it. The proper course is to ask for
the loan of a Supreme Court Judge of a
sister »Slate. If he should say that this
Parliamentary committee’s report is justified,
there should be afterwards no room in
politics for the Ministers responsible.
Just before the formal split in the N. S.
Wales Parliamentary Labor party last week
Speaker Meagher allotted separate quarters
for the use of the new wing. The occupying
of separate rooms generally means something
in Divorce presently.
AUSTRALIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Land of the lofty mountain-balconies,
Where breezes laugh and loiter to and fro ;
Of leafi' halls and colonnades of trees,
The distant dust-cloud rising from the slow
Column of pattering sheep ; of hills that ring
With swinging axes flashing to the blow ;
Of mines and golden quartz, and richer gold
Of wattle-trees in Spring ;
Of blossoming plains, and painted birds, and old
Luggers that roll to morning-seas a swing!
Land of the singing days and dancing light;
Of young and laughing cities; and of lone
Spaces where silence tip-tocs through the night;
Of noons that drowse to lotus-odors blown,
Lazy from pearly beach and purple bay;
Of galloping monsoon-hooves careering down
The flooded roads o’ the skj r , and deluging
A continent with spray ;
Of stars that sail the seas of night, or swing
In busy harbors of the Milky Way !
Land that was like some goddess, throned in light,
Guarded by screens of ocean-cavalry,
And doubly-guarded by the dazzling-bright
Swords of the sun. so that she seemed to be,
Unto those first weak wooers who ventured near
A menacing, barbaric deity ;
Till on a destined day there came a bold
Hover who knew no fear;
M ho saw no goddess, but a girl to hold ;
A maiden eager for the wedding-year !
And so this last among the lands of earth
Yielded her beauty to the will of man.
And true to her adventurous mate, gave birth
To hero-children, in whose blood there ran
Her sunlit vigor and her lazy grace;
Whose eyes were morning-clear, and keen to scan
And scorn old follies ; and who seemed to stand
Strong and uncommonplace:
A' clean young nation, with an out-stretched hand
Greeting the world, firm-foot, and face to face.

Bulletin, 16/11/’l 6.
Good Will Steadily Spreading
Dodge Brothers have built aud
delivered over thirteen million
pounds worth of cars in the past
year and a half.
The car has now reached the
stage where its sales are almost
automatically increased.
By this we mean that one sale
is almost certain to result in one
or two other sales.
The simple truth about how the
car stands with the public is hard
to put'into words because it sounds
like boasting.
It is a common occurrence with
dealers, everywhere, to have a man
walk in and drive out without
wasting time asking questions.
The fact is that the average
owner is not merely satisfied, but
almost extravagantly enthusiastic.
You must have noticed that the
first thing said about the car. in
ordinary conversation, is almost
always a direct reference to its
quality.
People have arrived at the con-
clusion that the Dodge Brothers
give the manufacture of the car
an unusual degree of personal
attention; and that the car is
riglP, down to the smallest detail.
The’-e is a widespread feeling
that they take an intense personal
pride in perfecting the product
that bears their name.
Both of these conclusions are
correct.
As we have said before, the
Dodge Brothers are the actual,
active superintendents, engineers,
managers and owners of this busi-
ness.
Before they began to build their
own car they had built the vital
parts for more than half a million
other cars.
A 1! of this experience, and all
that followed it —coupled with
intense pride in their own product
and love of the work itself—keeps
up a continuous process of im-
provement.
They have concentrated on one
car. and one car only, in a deter-
mination to make it as good as a
car can be made.
For every part and every process
that enters into it they have per-
sonally established a standard.
The work done and the materials
used in each part, and each pro-
cess, in every car, roust conform to
dm; standard.
The factories now cover over 60
acres of floor space —and are still
growing-—-but there is no depart-
ment so remote that it does not
feel the influence of the Dodge
Brothers* personal direction.
This intense watchfulness ac-
counts. of course, for the remark-
able things you hear about the
performance of the car.
In its first year it has acquired
a wonderful hold on the good
opinion of the public—and it has
not developed a single fault worth
talking about.
That it will steadily grow bet-
ter under the Dodge Brothers’
generalship goes without saying.
The petrol consu tnption is unusually low.
The tyre mileage is unusually high.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Tlie belated Melbourne Cup race was a
big success, after all, notwithstanding the fact that
a one-time favorite in Kilboy had gone amiss during
the four days’ postponement. On a deadened, drying
course the race was run at a pace which precluded
the possibility of the prize being captured by an
undeserving gee. Most of the running was made by
Prince Bardolph, and, with Amata close behind, the
leading colors for the first mile or so were mostly
a study in white and black. To the crowd that
viewed the struggle through glasses from afar off
it seemed that both of the white-and-blacks were
going strong until Stageland’s blue and pink rushed
into prominence. Then the crowd began to talk
loudly of Lucky Escape and Sasanof, but all the time
the followers of Shepherd King were quietly con-
fident. The colors of the King had always been in
the picture, and not until Sasanof’s yellow jacket
gleamed conspicuous at the last turn did the crowd
in general doubt that Bobby Lewis was going to
take the lead presently. Lewis himself stated after
the race that he foresaw the victory of the Maoriland
colt when they had still seven furlongs to go. The
coming result became apparent to all when the
straight was arrived at. The colt was slipping along-
easily near the rails, whilst Shepherd King, laboring
to catch him, was losing ground by inches. The
finish was a procession. The crowd placed the first two.
horses long before the leader passed the judge’s box
with 2-1 lengths to spare. The third horse, away
back, had colors which suggested Bridges, but proved
to be the dark tartan and cerise cap of St. Spasa.
Still further behind came Lingle, who had passed
Wolaroi and Lucky Escape in the run home, but
had possibly got there on sufferance.
Of the strongly-fancied candidates Quinologist,
Wallace Isinglass and Torbane performed very badly
in a field of 28. But where were the despised out-
siders when the final struggle began? With the
exception of St. Spasa the four placed horses had
been first favorites at one time or other, _ and three
of the first four favorites on the day finished first,
second and fifth. Sasanof went out at 12 to 1
because the public was put off him by stories con-
cerning his suspicious-looking- hock, and the books
paid little heed to the fact that the stable thought
the hock didn’t matter for the time being. Punters
who went to look at the Maoriland colt in the bn u-
cage could see but little swelling. The trouble had
been arrested in its development, if there ever was
a trouble worth making a song about. So Sasanot
did the trick and form triumphed.
* * * *
Stead and Luttrel, joint-owners of Sasanof
and Kilboy, and Stead, sole owner of two or three
other nags, will carry back a fair-sized wad to
Maoriland. Thev did well at Randwick in stakes
and bets, and though Kilboy’s performance rubbed
a bit of gilt off the gingerbread the bite taken out of
the V.R.C. and MacShortodds was big _ enough for
anyone. Sasanof, though backed to win at Caul-
field, was steadily supported for the big two miles
and they got in bright, and early. Sasanof was
really a better horse from the owners’ point of
view to win with than Kilboy would have been, for
had the colt won the Derby bis price would have
o-rown horribly short. Even as it was he went to a
very close margin before being downed by Wolaroi,
and this maintained a nice market for Sasanof all
the time. Later on, while the gelding was shortening,
there was money for him ; and after he “went lame,
owing to serious trouble in the hock,” more cash
was wheeled up by someone for the more liberal
odds on offer. Yes; it was a good thing. As this
page has taken occasion to remark on a previous
occasion, Sasanof is hot stuff.
* # * *
Within 15 years 10 horses have been, placed
in tlie Melbourne Cup after getting a “shop’ at Caul-
field: Vanity Fair, Acrasia, Poseidon, Apologue,
Tulkeroo, Apple Pie, Flavian, Uncle Sam, Sasanof
and Shepherd King. Poseidon was the only one
to win both Cups. Acrasia and Apologue scored a
second and a first.
* * * *
Big events administer some curious side-
kicks. The Referendum happened along just when
the tide of Cun half-sovereigns should have been flow-
in. towards Tatt.’s. The result was that instead ot
having two big sweeps full, No. 1 had to close down
with 85.000 subscribers instead of the usual 100,000.
* * * *
“Floater”: Vic. bookies in the paddock
differ in their method of expressing their lacer-
ated feelings at the institution of the new betting
tax. Some show their annoyance by refusing the
five shillings of the humble investor and insisting on
a minimum of half a sovereign for all investments.
Others, depending more on the support of the five-bob
brigade, try to get even by offering disgracefully
short odds, ranging from about 20 to 1 on a rank
outsider to even money on a moderate favorite.
The fact that 2 and 3 to 1 were considered a fair
thing for Shepherd King in a field of 28, whereof
about a dozen were stronglv supported, demonstrates
the deep sense of injury with which Isaac Noses is
meeting the demand to pay the Government a trifle
for the privilege of robbing the public.
* * * *
The books, as usual, said the Cup waS “a
bad race” for them. Bad to pronounce, perhaps
they meant. What price Tliathanof, Thepherd King
and Thaint Thpatha? * #
Ike had a bad time at Victoria Park
(Sydney) on Saturday, and great was his wail ot
anguish. Of the nine races five were appropriated
by actual first favorites and two by solid second
fancies. One turn-up —a 33 to 1 shot-caused the
suit to shine for a moment on the inhabitants ot
Israel—except one man who laid £lOO to £3 to
the owner of Gold Twist. Ike has been copping it
hot on the Sydney pony courses lately, and a number
of mushroom bookmakers have retired to their foimei
avocations. . *
* * * *
Trainer-owner Ryan had almost a field
day at Moorefield (Sydney) on Saturday. Ills colt
Blazing Hot had two runs—first m the Maiden Han-
dicap, six furlongs, in which he was beaten by
Krithia and Kenveine; and second in the Kogarali
Stakes, one mile, which he landed in fine style.
Ryan’s gelding Tarpan secured the Carlton Mile, but
was not backed for much. The most pleasing fea-
ture about these successes is that the sire of the
nao-s was Tartan. As Malt King also claimed a win-
ner, Australian-bred horses broke even with the
Pommy on one day, anyway.
* * *
The Malt King filly Princess Path is
proving a good advertisement for the dad that was
a champion on Australian racecourses in his day.
Already this speedy flapper has landed a couple of
races in a style which suggests that bigger things may
come. Princess Path’s second victory was at Moore-
field on Saturday, when, with 9st., she won on the
bit by three lengths. That this was not unexpected
is shown by the starting price—6 to 4 on. A fort-
night previously Princess Path landed a two-year-old
event at Canterbury Park, beating among others
Carafon, who a week earlier had appropriated
an event at Warwick Farm. Princess Path led all the
way on Saturday and cut out the 4j furlongs in
58see., which is 3f sec. outside the Australasian
record put up by Woorak at Randwick in October,
1913, when that flier was two years old. Woorak
carried Bst. and Moorefield is not Randwick as far
as time-making is concerned.
* * * *
Owner S. H. Rawdon, incensed at the
action of the Williamstown stipes who fined his
trainer Skirving a tenner for taking a whip to the
unruly Hayston at the starting-post without per-
mission, has vowed to race no more in Victoria.
He will continue to send his Worses to Sydney, Adel-
aide and Tasmanian meetings, however. The in-
dignant sportsman, who is a member of the Williams-
town R.C., started the boycott by scratching his can-
didates for the Williamstown Cup, which proceeding
punishes early punters more than the club. Blague
and Hayston were both fancied.
*.*„.**
“Mujik”: This business of looking for
winners on weight, form, breeding, pull, etc., makes
me tired. Take the Melbourne Cup. It was as
easy as tumbling into a pub. And some people
knew it. There was the yarn that Shepherd King
was good goods because someone started a rumor
about the animal having been squashed by a tram,
and as a similar canard about Mentor and a train
had been circulated in ’BB, Shepherd King was bound
to prevail like the other animal. Then there was a
gag about Quinologist and there being 11 letters in
the moke’s name and these being No. 11 on the list
and so on. Punters who go on that sort of thing
don’t know the game. I have it down to a fine point.
A month ago I grabbed the card and saw it in one,
“Stead’s br. g. Sasanof” —16 letters; the Cup of
’l6. Therefore I sought out Ikey and put the last
shilling on. When the postponement was announced
memory remarked that the Franco-German war was
raging when the event was last adjourned. As there
is a Franco-German disturbance going on now and
Sasanof is winning, “the office’’ simply yelled for
more cash. Full details of How to Interpret Tips are
obtainable on payment of an annual subscription of
£625 2s. 4d. The volume is printed on tripe and
bound in ass’s skin.

And this is the parable of the obscure tip
and the missing quids:—•
When the choir had sung “Hail our Shepherd
King" at a Melbourne Church, the Righteous rushed
forth in search of the Children of Israel, who opened
up their bags and displayed gold and silver and the
paper currencv which maketh glad the heart of man
and Bill Higgs. And the Righteous reckoned it was
all over bar shouting, and Bar Von took their
ducats, and they trusted him, and wended their w»ay
to the grandstand, and watched him from afar
trafficking with sinners and publicans and the
Pharisees. And then a great shout went up from the
crowd, and the Righteous went and sat down by the
sodawater and wept, for they had fallen in. And
as they hearkened thev heard hymns of joy arising
from Zion and the popping of many corks. Where-
upon they arose and girded up their loins and wended
their way to the tabernacle, and gave the organist
the sack.
* * # -N *
Walter Lindrum last week beat Clark
McConachy (M.L.) in a 16,000-up billiard match at
Sydney by 3457 points. Lindrum ran up scores of
471 and 413, nine times exceeded 200, and on 38
occasions got over the century. McConachy’s best
figures were 223 and 32 centuries. Last time they
played Lindrum won by 5551 in 18,000.
* * * *
“1.G.”: How sporting is professional
sport! The just-concluded billiard-match between
Walter. Lindrum and the young Maorilander
McConachy in Sydney is a case in point. The talent
generally considered Lindrum all round the better
player, and it looked pretty much of a cert for him
from the word “go.” But he gave nothing away on
that a< ccount. Lindrum’s long suit is cuddling the
balls, about and making cannons in long strings that
are about as interesting to watch as the. manufacture
of sausages. The other chap is at his best as a
hazard-striker, and can make the pocket-fir.ding
business nearly as monotonous as Lindrum’s cannons.
Well, the conditions of the match were fixed so that
the unsophisticated Maoriland .youth was not allowed
to make more than 25 consecutive hazards, whether
off the red or the white, whether winners or losers.
Nobody could reasonably argue that such a restric-
tion is balanced by the rule which makes the cannon-
player use the cushion. It effectively shackled
McConachy. Once when, in making for double-baulk
at the end of his appointed 25 hazards, he holed the
red there was an argument about what penalty should
be imposed. There were other arguments, too, before
the business ended, and most of them went against
the stranger. McCorachy would probably have been
beaten by superior skill, anyway, which makes it all
the. more sinful that he should have been eaged as
he . was, talked; frequently into a state of jumpiness,
and generally subjected to the sort of. treatment that
is handed out .to. a lamb at the abattoirs.
Fritz Holland delivered liimself just under
the fniddle-weight limit at Sydney Stadium on Satur-
day to see what he could do to Joe Chip, and
Joe arrived with a heavy wallop to find out what
he could do to Fritz. And as Fritz is a wily old
bird and a very fine boxer he. perpetrated most.. Chip
is a scrapper 'without much science. If he hits he
hurts; if he Tnisses he misses hy a mile. And
mostly Fritz made him miss ’or blocked his biff.
In the tenth collision Chip put Holland down for
nine, but the bald head weathered the storm and
lived to see the journey through. He was very tired
at the end, but got the verdict and deserved it.
Chip made the fight, but didn’t know how to do
any more.
* * * *
Herb McCoy got another hiding on Satur-
day, this time from Llew Edwards. It was done at
Melbourne and decided the lightweight championship,
Edwards having been long enough in Australia to
be eligible for the decoration. The Englishman
is a better boxer than McCoy, though it is not
improbable that McCoy might beat men whom
Edwards would sooner not be worried with. Some-
times McCoy looks like a top-notcher, and on these
occasions probably is one; but at other times his
claims seem fearfully slender. He has fought some
of the finest battles seen in the Australian ring
among men of his weight—and some Of the very
worst. But though he may be getting stale or old
or lazy and has been relieved of the championship,
he still has it in him to argue the point with a
purposeful glove—on his nights* out.
Les Darcy may yet be sorry he left Aus-
tralia. Mike Gibbons has written to a Sydney man
declaring that he intended to come to Australia to
tackle Darcy. A cable message early in the week
told that Gibbons had outpointed Jack Dillon. If
that disturbance was on the level Gibbons is a
long way better than a lot of people thought. Any-
how it is safe to wager that if Darcy has gone to
America Gibbons will remain in his home country
and reap the advantage' of meeting the Australian
there. It would not pay anyone to bring him this
way while Darcy was off the premises.
* * * *
Simultaneously with the skipping of Les
Darcy comes news that Geo. Carpentier has won
the Military Medal for gallantry on active ser-
vice. Pug circles have a lingering hope that
after the war a match will be arranged between the
two. If it comes off, let’s hope that the man who
deserted his country will get a hiding.
* * * *
Barney Donovan,, who skied the towel
after many rounds in a strenuous life, held the light-
weight championship of Maoriland for 12 years. When
Billy Murphy was running round Auckland selling
papers Barney helped to keep the game alive at that
end of the Dominion, and was fit enough to deteat
Murphy for the title before that enterprising young-
ster hit out for these parts and America. In 54
contests only four losses were suffered by Barney.
Amongst these was one against Jack Burke, the lush
lad who lasted four rounds with J. L. Sullivan.
Barney was white all through, and decorated with
one eye. Last time this writer saw him he was
running a booth devoted to scrap with Jim Petten-
gel, now blind and selling matches in Sydney.
* * * *
Sydney Grammar banged Scots College
all over Rusheutters Bay oval last week and amassed
a total of 848 runs. Heslien (243) and Gostello
(140) put up 390 for the first wicket. Sundries
played a splendid innings for 88. When the smash
wa3 over Wing (Grammar) got loose on the tired
Scots and took seven for five, the whole blessed clan
collapsing for 74. The winners’ huge total jumps
into place in Australian school records behind 961
by Melb. 0. of E. Grammar and 916 by Sydney
Grammar.
PETER McALISTER, vIC.
E. L. Waddy played music on the pickets
at Manly (Sydney) on Saturday on behalf
of Western Suburbs against Middle Harbor, and
piled up 193 —the highest individual score in
this season’s cricket premiership. For many
years Waddy was one of the most aggressive
of Sydney cricketers, but since the break-up of
Central Cumberland he hasn’t bothered much, and
with Western Suburbs only aggregated 195 runs in
1914 and 93 in 1915. This season he has hit
270 runs in three innings.
** * *
“Tasman”: Australia has been famous for
the last 40 years as the home of brilliant left-
handed batsmen. Left-handers at cricket are not
particularly common; it is rare to find more than
a couple in a team. Yet the number of inter-
nationals who shape the wrong way round is remark-
able. Followers of cricket in the ’Bo’s and 90 s will
not forget W. Bruce (Vic.) and Harry Moses
(N.S.W.). Then came Joe Darling and Clem Hill,
who have a couple of notable successors in Warren
Bardsley and Vernon Ransford. It takes a bit of
nerve to pick the winner from such a bunch, but
at anv rate it is safe to say that none of the others
has put up a record in first-class cricket,, more par-
ticularly in test matches, which can beat Hill’s. He
scored six centuries in the tests, against Trumpei s
eight, but to appreciate his record one has to
remember the extraordinary number of occasions on
which he reached 96, 97, 98 and 99 without getting
three figures. The last time I saw him bat —-in the
fourth test against the S’Africans in 1911 —he hung
round for five or 10 minutes within two or three
of the hundred. At last he pulled himself together
and made the required smack. Hill has scored
more centuries in big cricket than any other Aus-
tralian.
* * * *
“Tasman” : The aldermen of Hobart spend
a good deal of their time talking about the neces-
sity for providing public recreation grounds—“lungs
where the city can breathe.” They had a chance to
do something in this line only lately. Owing to the
slump in cricket and football the Tasmanian Cricket
Association has had to surrender to the bailiffs, and
as a result the Upper Cricket ground, one of the
finest and most picturesque ovals in Australia, where
all the bigger matches have been played for the past
30 years, has had to close down. The City Council
has been given the opportunity of taking it over at
little more than the amount of the liabilities, which
don’t reach one-tenth of what has been spent m
construction and improvements. But the butchers
and bakers who mostly make up the council don tgo
nap on cricket, and the ground has been let for
grazing.
John R. Needham, dead at St. Kiida
(Vic.), was a warrior at bowls in yeais ag * with
rolled ’em up first with Fitzroy and , cC j
Hawthorn; but particularly he will be
as Father of the Champion of Champions compet and
tions in Victoria, which for a long while he and
with the trophy. The old man had sampled
years.

* * * *
A footballer who will play the game in
future with difficulty is J. J Rudduck, late ot
Dromana (Vic.) and a star of Richmond. Phi own
out here as unfit he went to England, got passed
into the Lancaster Regiment, and, as lieutenant, took
on a risky job about the time of the Pozieres busi-
ness, and lost his left arm in consequence. Jnow
that he is convalescent England isn’t wasting nun,
he is training recruits, while a younger brother is
getting himself ready to take the other s place m
trance.
• * * *
The drowning season for unskilled surfers
commenced the other day, a young man at Narra-
been being the first to go. It promises to be a
half-mast season, unless the venturesome bathei
realises that most Sydney beaches have been cleared
of their best life-savers by the war. Some ot the
life-saving clubs can hardly raise a team this j ear ,
and if the experts had difficulty in preventing
tragedies in past seasons there are likely .to be
double tragedies now that they are away taking the
life-line out to civilisation.
* * * *
If the U.S. keeps on buying cars at the
present rate, in five years’ time every tenth person
will own some sort of mechanical cockroach on
wheels. That means that every second family will
own some sort of car-shelter. Prices of the cheaper
makes are still climbing down. One trade prediction
is that it will be possible next year for the Yank to
get a serviceable car for something like £SO. A good
bicycle used to cost £25. The huge production ot the
factories has made the whirring marvel possible. A
million and a half cars will be made before 1910
ends, and a rumor says that Ford alone will make a
million in 1917.
* * * *
“Condamine”: This is a sure, safe and
sudden method of clearing the barrel of gun or rifle
of an obstruction. Hold the weapon muzzle down-
wards and onen the breech; fill the space between
the breech and obstruction with wafer ; extract the
business end of a cartridge put a little piece ot
paper in on top of the powder and pull the triggei.
It will not damage the gun in any way, no matter
what the blockade consists of. P.S. —Before pulling
the trigger put the blank cartridge in the gun.
“Trooper Bluegum”: I’m satisfied that
nothing else provides half the amusement we get but
of chameleon hunting in the Wilderness of Egypt. This
is about the only fun Light Horsemen have nowa-
days, since Abdul imshied to the bounds of Bir el
Alid. The mode of capture ■is like this. Half a
dozen horsemen with fixed bayonets and loaded rifles
extend to about six paces and scour the desert. On
sighting the quarry the boys dismount, approach
carefully aiu. silently, bend down and gently pick-
ing up the chameleon place him in a haversack. It
takes some doing. On the sand the. beasts are very
difficult to see, being exactly the color of their
surroundings. When placed on a brown blanket they
darken immediately. The cham. earns his keep by
patching flies with a tongue that shoots out like a
piston-rod, and is ordinarily half as long again as his
body. I have a pet that I call Billjim, and his
tongue licks a range-finder to a frazzle. Fiom his
snout to the root of liis tail he is five inches long,
but his tongue whips out like a streak of lightning
and snares flies nine inches off. The cook played a
dirty trick on him yesterday. Sooled him on to an
insect on some flypaper. The resultant mix-up was
like unto a twin cat remonstrating with a pepper-tin.

In S. Aus. the political Labor body calls
itself the United Labor Party. It was at
the first meeting of the brethren after the
Referendum that Brother Hill, M.L.A., passed
Brother Goode, Minister for Lands, one in
the nose in token of amity ; and the subse-
quent proceedings consisted chiefly of throw-
ing all the members of the Labor Ministry
overboard.
Of melancholy interest to Australia is tne
fact that Capt. Ilbery, the skipper of the
Waratah when she disappeared off the face
of the waters seven years ago, had his first
command in the Mikado, the old tea-dipper
which, turned into a hulk in her old age,
was burned to the water’s edge at Suva,
Fiji, the other day.

A large proportion of male undergraduates
now at the ’Varsity are A.I.F. men returned
to complete tlieir medical course; so when
a prominent socialist and anti-conscriptionist
was billed to address a meeting at the
-Shop” they grinned and conspired together.
It was arranged that he should be called for
in a car appropriately decorated and whirled
out to the lonely Werribee plains, there to
be dumped 10 miles from a railway station.
But there was a traitor in the baud, or
else they plotted too loudly. When they
arrived the Anti bird had. flown, and they
next saw him sitting on the platform, ready
to begin his lecture, and stolidly chewing
gum.
Dainty Lizette Parkes is a pale little girl
just now. She has been forbidden any more
sea-trips for awhile, and instructed to work
ovei'time sitting on the verandah. During
the last six months she has fitted in one trip
to Maoriland and back, a voyage to and from
Westralia, and some incidental Tasmanian
tours. It’s too much for a poor sea-traveller
who begins to look more wistful than usual
when a theatre orchestra starts to play a
sea-song.
Lady Stanley will presently be talking
about fishtail winds instead of fishtail trains.
She lias just joined the Malvern Ladies’
liifle Club, and for her first appearance wore
the trimmest of fawn shooting skirts over
smart little boots. She ran up a creditable
score first try and induced Captain Conant
to fire a few rounds. He potted G 5 out of a
possible TO. The new riflewoman carried
away a souvenir target dotted with neat
round bullet-holes. Of course, the committee
of the range rushed to make things pretty for
the visiting sharpshooter. A large Union
Jack was flung over the billiard-table for tea,
and Mrs. E. A. Gaden, from Darling Point,
hurried along with two huge bowls of wara-
tah blooms. There was a fine roll-up of mem-
bers. Mrs. Harry Hope Campbell was there
sniffing gunpowder, and Miss Ross Thompson
(Macedon). Miss .Jamieson and Miss Lang-
don all tried not to jump every time a shot
was fired. Mrs. Samson Greville, Mrs. Polack
and Mrs. T. Hale tried their skill, and Miss
Nina Ba,got (the doggy girl) was acting-
captain. The Stanley dame is turning her
small boy on to the club to make an Empire-
defender of him.
There were some interesting old clothes
raked up for the pageant-tableau of life on
Flagstaff Hill in 1850-54. William Stawell,
Redmond Barry, Johnny Fawkner and Gov-
ernor Latrobe were some of the figures who
reappeared for this occasion only. Nearly
everyone in the tableau wore something
really belonging to the period. Madge Free-
man, of Bendigo, had on a black net dress
trimmed with red satin ribbons, which was
worn at a Government House ball some time
between 1850 and 1860, as well as a charm-
ing straw bonnet which came from England
at the Same period. Mrs. Carter lent her the
relic. Someone else carried a real 1850 para-
sol, bought at the Federal Government House
show of antiques. Mr. Herbert Rose lent
Marion Jones a dress made of hair-striped
shot silk, and Miss Aileen Dent a lovely old
biaek-and-green brocaded silk. Elsie Wilson
wore a ducky little blue-and-white check that
delighted some Tasmanian belle of 1850. Mrs.
Scott Fergusson lent this lot. Miss Grieve en-
trusted to Mary Allen a real lace shawl of
1850, and asked Miss Jackson Morris to be
careful of an 1880 parasol. A hat that was
worn by Chief Justice Stawell decorated his
representative’s head, and the walking-stick
always used by Redmond Barry was lent by
his son. The uniforms of the police were all
authenticated by Mr. ex-Commissioner O’Cal-
laghan.
Dr. Helen Sexton is back again in Mel-
bourne incapacitated for- farther strenuous
war-jobs by an honorable limp. She had
been working one morning till she was
almost blind with weariness, and went out
into the snow along some sunk passages. She
took one step down instead of three and
smashed her leg badly. Hence the return to
civil practice. The clever woman cannot get
over her amazement at the volume of pri-
vate motor traffic in Melbourne. She says
that, despite her limp, she would scarcely
venture to use a taxi in these days of war-
economy. But the petrol-saving idea seems
to have no hold here. In Paris it is difficult
to get a taxi at all. All private motors
have been commandeered and petrol is con-
served like gold. “It is like coming to
another world,” says the doctor, impatiently.
“Australia hasn’t realised what’s happening.
I see large motor-cars whizzing along occu-
pied by one woman only and a chauffeur!
Such waste!”
Melba’s country home at Coldstream
begins to have an established look.
Approached by road from the Yarra Flats,
on the side remote from Melbourne, Coombe
Cottage appears well furnished with garden
and lush cow-paddocks, and the house has
that well-groomed appearance seldom seen
even in the most prosperous agricultural
regions. Suggestive, perhaps, of a glorified
amusement-park, with its trellises, pergolas,
milk-coolers, fowl-roosts, fussy curves and
wedding-cake frills; nevertheless the sort
of place in which a well-gilded singer might
spend her deed—well, her holiday-years in
comparative peace. The roof-porch or up-
stairs rose-garden, with its view of a dozen
mountain-ranges, 40 paddocks, several sheep
and a lot of hardy annuals in the garden
below, should be just the spot for a little
smoke after dinner; and there is plenty of
room at the Cottage for all the friends that
Melba could possibly want to be bothered
with over the week-end. The view towards
Melbourne is a drawback, however. Its
immediate foreground consists of the vil-
lage pub, old and whiskered, and the village
store, old and withered; and beyond these
flat and uninteresting sheep-paddocks. Still,
it must be very pleasant for the people at
the pub and the store to gaze at Melba going
out with the milk-pail in the dewy mornings
or gathering garden-slugs in the gloaming.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

No notice (except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

The Victorian Municipal Ass. met during
the first week in November and passed a
motion condemning the Government for sug-
gesting new taxation and ordering it to
effect economies. It also passed an imposing
array of other resolutions, requiring in-
creased Government expenditure. Can’t the
animal make np its mind which bundle of
hay it really wants?

-jkklAj THE WORLD knows
* A and admires what Australia is doing in the ''
War, and patriotic Australia will appreciate wliat Michelin
has done and is doing.
MICHELIN has given £40,000 to further the work of French Military
Aviation, and is manufacturing 100 Aeroplanes, which he is
gi vin g to the French Government, representing an approximate
cost of £ 100,000.
MICHELIN also proposed the establishment of the “ Flying Service
Fund ” for the benefit of British Aviators and their dependants,
giving £ 1,000 to start the fund—also gave £BOO to the Prince
of Wales* Fund—and has given to date £120,000 to the
dependants of his employees who are fighting for the Allies.
MICHELIN is manufacturing shells, and bombs for aeroplanes, etc,, and is
also helping by his large supplies of tyres to the British, French,
Belgian and Italian Armie®
MICHELIN has established and maintains a hospital of 320 beds for the
wounded, costing to date £3o,ooo—has paid shareholders in
War Loan—and has made advances to his staff who have, to
sth December, subscribed £202,012 in War Loan and £5,520
to Fund for parcels for the Troops. Many other things could
be added to this list..
Yes, we are all doing our utmost in the common cause. The Allies are
united in Arms, let us be united in Business.
We, therefore, ask for your support, and, apart from other reasons, the
quality of MICHELIN TYRES assures it,
MICHELIN—THE BEST
Therefore the Must Economical.

A Good Carpet
is not necessarily expensive.
"Where you rest, there decorate,” said Euskin.
Where does the average Australian take rest —is it not in
the Dining Eoom, the Living Eoom? Then let us give
consideration to the decorating of the Dining Eoom with
Carpets and Eugs. A Linoleum covering is necessary, if
the floor be not polished; and then, say, a Velvet Pile
Carpet Square. One measuring 9ft. x 9ft. costs £4/5/-.
One measuring 12ft. x 9ft. costs £5/5/-. After all, that is
very little!
An Axminster Square, measuring 10ft. 6in. x 9ft.—
quite a big square, that is—can be supplied for £6/10/-.
Because they are so rich and beautiful some folk get the
idea that Carpets are expensive—you can see that such is
not the case. May we send a Price List of Carpets ?
John Hicks & Company Ltd.
George and Ann Streets, Brisbane.
IN
BANANA LAND
Captain Lionel Rawson, of the King’s
Royal Rifles, recently killed in France, was
a son of Charles Stansfield Rawson, once of
Maekay, and a nephew of Viking Rawson,
one-time Governor of N. S. Wales. Before
the Boer war, young Rawson, mostly known
as Jack, was an assayer at the Aldershot
Smelting Works (Q.), and went with the
Queensland forces to S’Africa. After the
war he occupied a lucrative position on one
of the Rand mines, but when Bill broke loose
he moved to the Front.
“H.T.P.”: John Lionel Ching, mentioned
as a “trader up Rockhampton way” by
“J.F.H.” (B. 26/10/’l6), was dugong-fishing
in Wide Bay in the early ’Bo’s. He returned
to England in the Liguria about May, 1883.
He had a brother, an admiral or something,
in the Navy, and when the vessel reached
Plymouth he was met by a cocked hat and
a special launch. His Pong name was the
subject of much banter; but I do not know
whether that was why he rejoined the cocked
hat.
Railways-Minister Harry Coyne has dis-
covered early in his Cabinet career the dif-
ference between dictating Labor policy from
the Trades Hall and attempting to meet the
demands of other dictators. When he led
the tram men in the 1912 strike, part of his
job was to issue permits to those whom the
Union allowed to remain at work. Now
Coyne goes humbly, to the Miners’ Federa-
tion begging for coal to keep his engines
puffing.
An idle copper mine, valued at £120,000,
is not the sort of thing that you would ex-
pect to trip over just now’. Yet Stopford,
M.L.A. for Mt. Morgan, got up the other
night and declared that he knew where one
of them was to be had. It is some 10 miles
from Mt. Morgan, and for the last 30 years
has lain idle. A few men who knew some-
thing about the place and something about
mining tried to get hold of it a while ago.
The owners, the Scottish Australian Metal
Co., were willing to sell—for £120,000. Pro-
posals for working the mine on tribute were
met with demands which made the w’ould-be
miners gasp. Stopford’s suggestion for
bringing things to a head is easily the best
so far put forward. He proposes that the
Commonwealth and State should both tax
the concern on the value put on it by its
owners. That would bring in about £3OOO
a year. The property, which is a freehold,
of course, has an area of 1474 acres and is
situated about three miles from the new
Dawson Valley railway. A coal-mine is
handy and there is an abundance of timber
and firewood in the vicinity. Premier Ryan
has promised the Imperial Government
48,000 tons of copper. It looks as if he is
having a chance offered to him. Those
dogs-in-the-manger will yelp and drop the
bone if they are clouted hard enough.
On the authority of the Auditor-General
the Ryan Government faked the accounts
for the past year. The “invariable practice”
for many years past has been to charge
against the revenue the outgoings for June
(the last month in the financial year) even
when “the acquitted vouchers had not
reached the Treasury or the departments at
June 30.” For instance, vouchers for
£102.292 spent by the Railways were sent
to the Treasury on .June 29; “but the
abstracts were subsequently returned by the
Treasury, tyith a request that they be struck
out of June and included in the July expen-
diture.” The same thing happened in other
departments. The Auditor-General com-
ments on this
As a result of the course adopted by the Treasury
Department the year under review (1915-6) has not
borne the full year’s expenditure as compared with
the immediately preceding years. Had the practice
of previous years been followed, and the items above
referred to charged, the Consolidated Revenue Fund
at June 30 last would have disclosed a deficit of
approximately £lOO,OOO.
The immediate result of this fake has been
to throw so much 1915-16 expenditure into
1916-17 that the July expenditure this year
exceeded that of July. 1915, by no less than
£157,268. If the auditor of a private busi-
MARITAL PRIDE.
GOOD LADY : “Your husband should never beat you under any circumstances. When I
do anything wrong my husband leaves my punishment to the Supreme Being."
MRS. MANGLES (with dignity): “Well, mum, I thank ’Eaven Bill ’as enough self-respect
to ’ammer ’is own wife ’isself.”
ness charged the directors with issuing a
false balance-sheet something would happen.
If it could be proved that they did it for
their personal advantage what would hap-
pen would probably have a judge and a gaol
in it. But what is plain dishonesty in the
director of a public company is apparently
something else in a party politician. Why
it should be so Heaven only knows. Ryan’s
only defence is to protest that his Govern-
ment has not been extravagant. This of
course is merely a red-herring drawn across
the trail. The question is not whether Ryan
and his Ministers are extravagant but
whether they are honest.
“Boro-Belle”: Not since the days of the
late Brunton Stephens has the Queensland
Parliament had an official report drafted with
such literary flourish as that of the recent
Commission on Public Works. Portion of
the report is devoted to a description of the
porcupine country between Juandah and
Taroom, the history of Taroom, and the
spread of the spiked pest, and it is a strik-
ing bit of work. The compiler was J. T. H.
Bird, formerly on the Rockhampton Bul-
letin ; he is now of Hansard staff, and
was secretary to the Commission.
The Toombul Shire Council, among whose
members are certain British subjects named
Blinginger, Schulz and Wildermuth, recently
debated whether the shire should abandon
the names of Schneider, Franz and Zillmann
roads. Quite naturally, lviessrs. Blinginger,
Schulz and Wildermuth decided that Schnei-
der, Franz and Zillmann were remarkably
good names to hang on to.
“Itan”: Ten men in our Chamber of
Fossils have lived in the aggregate 760
years. The oldest is 82. The Assembly has only
one man who has managed to dodge past the
allotted span. Not one of the 10 M’s.L.C.
c#n boast of holding an elective position.
They represent one-third of the full strength
of the House, and with two others can form
a quorum. Their hostility to Democracy is
uniformly unrelenting; every measure which
goes to them with a spice of reform in its
composition is promptly conducted to the
back door. Yet thei’e is no certainty that
the referendum which Premier Ryan pro-
poses will turn this Chamber down. The
growth of the I.W.W. and the financial and
other sins of Labor Governments have given
this House a chance of which it hadn’t even
the shadow a dozen years ago.
“Boro-Belle”: Every year when the wet
season sets in up north the crabs and prawns
show what they think of rain water. At the
first approach of a fresh in the Pioneer River
the prawns make for the sea, while the
crabs climb out on to the river bank or
training walls. The Maekay kid, armed
with a stick and a sugar sack, has then the
time of his life, as the sea spiders offer no
interference with trade, so thoroughly do
they appear to be doped. It is said that
they are not edible when in such a condition,
but no deaths have so far resulted from crab
suppers in the rainy season.
A few ominous figures. In the metropoli-
tan district for 1914-15 the cost of charitable
relief was £1847, while for the year ending
June 30, 1916, the figures leaped to £7246.
Either Brisbane is producing three to four
times as many hard-ups as it used to or the
relief administration is becoming scandal-
ously extravagant. The total relief for
Queensland (including fares and allowances)
boomed up to £14,638, which was half as
much again as in the preceding year. No
wonder Treasurer Theodore meditates fresh
taxation.
Bananaland Lands Department has
decided that the easiest way to avoid
squabbles and scandals in land balloting is
to do the gamble with numbered marbles.
They are placed in a box, shaken up, and
an instrument, officially called an extractor,
is used by a disinterested person to draw
one, whose number is shown to the Land
Commissioner and at least one unsuccessful
applicant.
“Sergeant”: The note handed over at a
recruiting office by an 18-year-old applicant
for enlistment:—
Capt. . Dear Sir, —I hereby give you per-
mission to enlist my son Jim Wirefence for active
service, as he is no damned use at home.—Yours
truley, John Wirefence.
I hope young Jim wins the V.C. and shames
the old man.
Minister Hunter told a deputation which
asked him to clear State prickly-pear lands
that it would cost well on to £40,000,000
to do the job. And it doesn’t seem an over-
estimate. Arthur Morgan made an experi-
ment in that line. Tile clearing cost £l9
per acre, and the cleared land was only
worth a fiver at most.
“W.G.8.” : How the North does break up
the white man’s body and spirit both! At
Freshwater, a little furrowed hamlet outside
Cairns, only 14 men were left to be called up
under the recent proclamation. Of these 11
passed as medically fit and the other three
were invited to come up again presently.
Cairns is jubilant over the quality of the
paper made by Sydney mills from pulp
pounded by ex-Archdeacon Joseph Campbell
out of local weeds. The mills, too, are so
satisfied with results that they have asked for
more pulp, and a syndicate has been formed
to oblige them. It proposes to use blady
grass, Chinese burr, sida retusa and pan-
danus, and begin the job in January.
The Douglases of Wooloowin don’t slack.
Major George, the doctor, is in charge of
the health of Brisbane camps; Lieut. Bill
was fighting as Ypres before a bursting shell
hurled him into a hospital at Amiens; Pri-
vate Walter was knocked out for 24 hours
at Romani; and Sergeant Ike is in camp
getting ready.
A hard-doer presented himself at the
Cairns enlisting office the other day, and
was accepted for active service. He said he
had come from New Guinea, and his name
was Harry Goften. After he was passed
he applied for a refund of his boat-fare. He
said he thought he was entitled to it because,
before he could get to a port of call for
steamers, he had to walk 195 miles, and
“ well barefooted, too!” He got the
refund—which is one war item to give a
cheer for, anyway.
“Two Blues” : How is this fpr a run on the
unlucky 13? A Billjim enlisted on the 13th
of the month. He was allotted to the 13th
Battalion, and given the regimental number
463—-figures, added, make 13. With his crowd
he embarked at Alexandria for the Darda-
nelles on April 13, 1915. He was biffed on
the 13th day after landing, and was once
more back in Pharaohland on May 13.
Before the Anzacs sailed for France he was
invalided home. Thirteen of his pals came to
meet him, and 13 letters were waiting for
him. Finally, he was discharged on the 13th
of last month.
From an island-garden in the Pacific: —
Casement has gone to the place where he belongs,
and no one will regret it. No one wants to excuse
him, either—he knew what he was doing. But, for
the credit of Ireland, let me say that Casement was
hot 'in any sense of the word, a normal citizen, if
his family history is to be taken into account.
Ulster knows them—with reason—as the “Mad Case-
ments.” They are a wealthy and influential family,
and people still marry their sons and daughters;
but it is well known that he or she who marries a
Casement doe 3 so with a fine disregard of the future
of the coming generation. One could give a good
list of Casements who are, have been, or ought to
be in asylums. I knew a man who married one, a
charming girl, apparently as sensible as you or I.
She has been in an asylum for many years, outliving
her husbands and most of her own people; and her
children have been—well, troublesome, all their lives.
Casement was one of the unlucky beggars who are,
and are not, mad—everybody knows one or two.,
This was the real cause of the hesitation over his
execution. Yet insanity was one of the things that
counsel couldn’t prove.
We Voted on the Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We are proud of the way our people voted.
—Anti Press everywhere.
We like the noble cove who’s in the trench —
He’s battling for his country all the time; -
And we’ll admit it gives a sort of wrench
To know he’s unsupported in the grime;
But think of all the wickedness it meant
To sling our precious life and blood away;
And so with just the very best intent
We voted with the Germans on The Day.
We have no sympathy at all with Fritz ;
We hate to hear his evil-smelling name;
We’d like to see him get the damndest fits
And hope the Russian’s equal to that same;
But when you ask us straight—“ Why don’t you go
And do your little duty in the fray?”
We are not slaves to drive, we’d have you know—
We voted with the Germans on The Day.
We always Join in singing “Save the King”
And patriotic ditties of the kind ;
But let the folk in Europe have their fling,
And don’t annoy the coves that stay behind;
To build it up, the Country needs a few
W’ho’ll set its big activities at play,
It was with such a patriotic view
We voted with the Germans on The Day.
There’s quite a lot of empty billets now,
And lots of homes are mourning some sad loss;
But when the rod descends we have to bow,
And everybody has to bear his cross;
The Future hides a lot we never guessed—
But, as for me, I’m neither sad nor gay;
1 tried to do my best to stop the pest
By voting with the Germans on The Day.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

THE BROKEN HILL PROPRIETARY COMPANY Limitec
NJ OTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that the abovenamed Company is issuing a prospectus dated the 11th day
' of NOVEMBER, 1916, a copy of which has been filed with the Registrar-General in the State of
Victoria inviting subscriptions for an issue of £400,000 six per cent. Debentures for Seventeen years and
seven months from First day of December, 1916, at £9B per centum, balance of an authorised issue of
£1,090,000.
The said Prospectus states (amongst other things) to the effect following:—
Subscriptions will be payable at follows: —-
Deposit on application 6% of the face value of the Debentures
On allotment ....'. 10% „ ~ „ ~
On Ist day of February, 1917 20% ~ ~ „ ~
On 2nd day of April, „ 20% „ „ „ „
On Ist day of June, ~ 20% ~ „ „ „
On Ist day of August, „ 23% „ „ ~ »
Instalments may be paid in advance on allotment or on any of the above dates and will carry
interest from date of payment.
The Debentures now offered for subscription are the balance of a total Authorised Issue of £1,000,000
and will rank pari passu with the £600,000 Debentures already issued.
A cumulative sinking fund at the rate of £23,000 per annum for Seventeen years and seven months
(the first proportionate payment to be made on Ist July, 1917) will be provided to redeem this issue
by purchase at any time up to 30th June, 1921, of any debentures of this issue if the price exclusive of
accrued interest be par or below par or after the 30th June, 1921, by yearly drawings within fourteen
days from the 30th June in each year.
Every debenture so drawn shall become payable and be redeemed at its face value with accrued
interest thereon (if any) added on 31st December next after such drawing.
The moneys required for such purchase or redemption of Debentures shall be drawn from such
Cumulative Sinking Fund account. This fund will be kept separate and distinct from the fund provided
for purchase or redemption of the first issue of £600,000.
The debentures will be secured jointly with the said issue of £600,000 by a specific charge or mort-
gage to the Trustees for debenture holders on specific assets of the Company now representing those
specifically charged or mortgaged by a Trust Deed of the 27th July, 1914, and by a floating charge created
by the same deed on the general undertaking and assets of the Company exclusive of any of the Com-
pany’s uncalled or unpaid capital.
The Debentures will be to Bearer with interest coupons attached and will be issued in sums of £lOO.
Provisional Bearer certificate will be issued on payment of the amount due on allotment and may
be exchanged by Bearer for Debentures on payment of final instalment.
The Debentures will carry interest at the rate of SIX per cent, per annum and the coupons will be
payable half-yearly on the Ist days of January and July.
The moneys secured will at the holder’s option be -paid as to principal at the offices of the Common-
wealth Bank of Australia in Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide or London and as to interest either at any of
such offices or at the said Bank’s offices in Brisbane, Perth or Hobart.
The whole of the present issue is underwritten and the Directors will only proceed to allotment upon
the whole of the present issue being subscribed.
The Managers of the Commonwealth Bank of Australia in Sydney and Melbourne for the time being
will be the Trustees for the Debenture holders.
The subscription list will open in Melbourne, Sydney and Adelaide on the 27th day of NOVEMBER.
1916, and will close on or before the Ist ,day of DECEMBER, 1916.
Application Forms may be obtained from any branch in Australia of the Commonwealth Bank of
Australia, and from the undermentio'-'Ofl Brokers: —
TOLHURST, KEATS & GUMMING, Melbourne.

received at the Head Office or at any branch in Australia of the Commonwealth Bank of Australia.
Copies of the full prospectus and forms of application can be obtained at the Commonwealth Bank
of Australia and from any of the above-mentioned brokers and at the said offices; of the Company.
The requirements of the Companies Acts have not besn fully complied with, in this notice.
The application for Debentures will proceed only on one of the forms of application referred to in
and sent with the printed copy of the prospectus.
This notice is not to be regarded as an invitation to the public to subscribe for Debentures, and appli-
cations will only be received on the footing of the full prospectus and in the form issued therewith.
