Peter”: Kreisler, the Austrian violinist
who has been frequently mentioned in war
at latest he was in a dugout in
Galicia is now alleged to have been fiddling
quietly in Amurka ever since the war began.
Anyhow, lie was discovered in New York the
other day in the company of McCormack
and Paderewski, the three being associated
in a concert to raise cash for the dependents
of Granados, a recent victim of Hun Fright-
fulness. Granados, a Spanish composer
who had written a charming opera entitled
Goyescas,” was one of the casualties when
the Sussex was torpedoed in the English
Channel. The benefit for his six kiddies
was a great success. After a song by
McCormack and a fine bit of noise from
I aderewski and Kreisler, the audience broke
out in a profuse financial perspiration, and
it took a bunch of bank-tellers several hours
to mop the moisture up. The Granados
orphans are now better oft' by 13,000 dollars.
__ ‘‘ Jean S.”: In Rupert Julian, Julius
Knight will discover a convincing imitation
of what he used to be. Rupert, an Aus-
tralian, is now an imported ghost in Yankee
films circulating largely in these parts. The
other night I met his spectre in the trap-
pings of a Spanish bull-slayer, and I was
struck by the fact that, in addition to facial
resemblance, he was step for step and ges-
ture for gesture a wonderful replica of
Reaucaire at his best. Yet there was nothing
in Rupert’s portrayal to suggest conscious
plagiarism. It is apparently quite natural
tor him to behave like Julius. He seems to
represent one of Nature’s misguided efforts
to repeat a favored type.
A threatened revival of “The Orchid” in
Melbourne revives memories of the one con-
ventionally human character in the musical
comedy—the languid attache johnnie who
is put in charge of the orchid and instructed
by his father to guard the precious thills as
though it were a State secret. “I’ve got to
sit up all night with a flower-pot—and they
call this diplomacy,” says the tired young
man. He hasn’t been long enough in the
business to know that diplomacy may mean
taking particular care of a poodle.
Barnstormer”: The dear things will
never admit they don’t know. Mrs. Potts
1 omt and Mrs. O’Mosman were reclining
elegantly in their chairs at a suburban con-
cert. Someone sang Alfred Hill’s haunting
little Maori song Poi,” and the two
ladies rustled their programmes and began
to fuss. My dear, what does ‘Waiata Poi’
mean ! Ive forgotten for the moment.”
Sorry, dear, hut I quaite forget. My
I rencli is getting fearfully rusty !”
Fax”: . Nothing that is daringly new
ci ops up in seaside vaudeville shows nowa-
days. The “business” is a repetition or
compound variation of the “business,” of
long ago. Pelissier, the ruling spirit in
the London Follies, was a fertile inventor,
but he has gone where the good pierrots go
and left no successor. The old burlesques
and wheezes are revived or adapted; so the
stuff you get is pleasing but has no bite,
lou have heard it before, and as often as
i 1 • emin ds you of something which was
stolen from the Pelissier factory and used
tor an irrelevant turn in the second act of
a musical comedy. In which case you are
usually struck by the fact that such things
are better done in a quiet sort of way on
a small stage than by the broader-styled
comedians m a Williamson production.
A singular development of snobbery is
noticeable in picture-show ads these times.
Ihe personage who achieved an extraordi-
nary notoriety as Charlie Chaplin is no
longer alluded _to in the old familiar way.
Ihe haio of his alleged enormous salary is
round his name. He travels with a private
secretary and a valet, it is said. Yankee
journals have raised him to the plutocracy
and an interviewer who asked the distin-
guished Cockney whether he ever thought of
enlisting in Kitchener’s Army was reproved
for his impertinence. Therefore it seemed
Charles° dl ° P the Charlie ‘ He is now
Miles Faraday, a popular English actor,
has been injured at the Front bv being
struck with a fragment of a shell. This will
not discourage Australian pros., who have
so often been struck with the whole egg.
Daddy” Lohr, the old theatrical identity,
who went to Waiwera (M.L.) to save his
life is now settled there as licensee of the
Hot Springs Hotel, where he long-time
stayed as a guest. After recovering his
health Lohr talked of touring with a picture-
show, but lie decided that Waiwera had been
his salvation, and the most economical way
of enjoying its curative properties would be
to run the pub.
MY HOPELESS PASSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She playa her part with touching grace;
None is more fair than she;
And shyly sometimes from her place
She seems to smile on me.
But vain, ah! vain, are all my sighs
Emotions deep to tell;
To yearnings and to pleading eyes
She gives no heed—to me the prize
Is inaccessible.
More sweet is she than all the rest.
To win a moment’s thought
I d deem myself forever blessed,
No matter how ’twere bought.
But vain are gold, or diadem,
Or roses from, the mart.
She has no sort of use for them—
Not diamond nor stratagem
Can win for me her heart.
Ask her to sun ? Too well I know
The plea would be ignored.
When she is “off” I stay, although
Insufferably bored.
When ended is the final act
I linger on the scene,
Sensations tender to protract.
lam a fool! She is in fact,
An actress on the screen.
VICTORIA.
STARGAZER.


With old age comes inactive bowel
movement and sluggish liver. Through
weakness, nature is unable to perform
her proper functions, and requires assist-
ance ; without it there is constant suffer-
ing from constipation and its attendant
evils. Old folks should never use physics
that are harsh and irritating. With our
experience of the good results that have
been obtained by the use of Chamber-
lain’s Tablets we can unhesitatingly
recommend their use in preference to any
other medicine for aged people. Cham-
berlain’s Tablets are gentle and soothing
in their action, cannot possibly produce
a disagreeable effect, and are in every
way reliable and beneficial to the whole
system.
Chamberlain’s Tablets are the ideal
medicine for children. No more nasty
medicines that you have to coax the child
to take. After they have once taken
Chamberlain’s Tablets, and realise how
easy they are to take, they will be ready
for them again. They are perfectly safe
for the youngest child and their use will
not result in constipation.
When the quantity of food taken is too
large, or the quality too rich, sour stomach
is likely to follow, and especially so if the
digestion has been weakened by constipa-
tion. Eat slowly and not too freely of
easily-digested food. Masticate the food
thoroughly. Let five hours elapse between
meais, and when you feel a fullness and
weight in the region of the stomach after
eating, take Chamberlain’s Tablets and
the sour stomach may be avoided.
“That tired feeling” is not always
caused by the weather, as some people
would have you believe. The fault
lies in some derangement of your diges-
tive organs. Your stomach is out of
order, as your liver has become sluggish
and refuses to perforin its functions. All
you need is a dose or two of Chamber-
lain’s Tablets, and that feeling of weari-
ness will disappear.
Constipation brings many ailments in
its train and is the primary cause of
much sickness. Keep your bowels regu-
lar by taking Chamberlain's Tablets,
and you will escape many ailments
which make life a misery. Constipa-
tion is a simple thing, but like many
simple things it may lead to serious con-

Old age should be made a period of happiness"
but strict attention to health is necessary.
As age advances the stomach and
bowel muscles lose their elasticity and
no longer respond readily, while the
liver does not store up the juices that
are necessary to prompt digestion. The
result is constipation, dyspepsia, bilious-
ness, sour stomach, bloating, drowsiness
after eating, belching, headache and
frequently weakness from want of
assimilation of food, which mean un-
happiness at any age.
The use of harsh cathartics aggravates
the trouble, and makes constipation
worse, the bowels soon becoming
unable to perform their own functions
without the aid of drastic purgatives.
Chamberlain’s Tablets are without
doubt Australasia’s greatest household
remedy. Their mild and gentle action
recommends them especially for women
and old folks, who should neve.” take
drastic cathartics and purgatives such as
pills, powders, salt waters, etc.
For old and young, the strong and
the weak, there is no laxative that
equals
Jfor- the Stomach o Livep.
The fact that indigestion and constipa-
tion are fruitful causes of organic diseases
cannot be widely known, or these only-
too-eommon complaints would not be neg-
lected, as they frequently are. “It is
only indigestion” is a common remark,
and few realise that indigestion may be
termed “starvation of the body,” for food
that is not digested is not assimilated by
the blood and the body is not properly
nourished. Chamberlain’s Tablets will
relieve the most obstinate case of indi-
gestion. Many a long sufferer owes his
present good health to a coarse of Cham-
berlain’s Tablets.
Constipation resembles nothing so
much as the stopping of the sewerage
system of a city, and you know the- out-
break of disease that would result if
this should happen. Then try to realise
the danger to yourself if you allow
your bowels to become clogged. The
poisonous matter which should pass off is
absorbed into the blood, which is the
cause of numerous diseases. When you
are troubled this way there is just one
thing to do—get a box of Chamberlain’s
Tablets. They cleanse and invigorate
the stomach and bowels, and clear the
system of all foul accumulations and re-
store it to a normal condition.
Indigestion and dyspepsia wear out
body and brain, for owing to imperfect
assimilation of food they do not receive
the required nourishment. Constipation
ensues and the victim becomes weak and
haggard. Chamberlain’s Tablets stimu-
late the digestive organs to perfect action,
compelling complete digestion and assimi-
lation of food, and strengthen the bowels
so that they act naturally and regularly.

The New South Wales Department
of Navigation notifies launch owners,
builders and motor manufacturers that,
after the Ist January, 1917, any motor-
launch, new or old, coming under survey
for a passenger certificate for the first
time, should be fitted with motors using
paraffin, or other fuel having a flash-
point of not less than 73 degrees
Fahr., Abel’s close test. Although it is
desirable not to carry any petrol on board
these launches, a small quantity may, if
necessary, be allowed for starting pur-
poses, provided it is limited to. the
smallest quantity practicable for the
motor. No petrol should be carried in a
closed motor room, or in any closed space
used by the passengers or crew. The
petrol tank must either be placed on
deck and provided with a suitable wooden
covering, or placed in an isolated and
well-ventilated compartment.

COMFORT
*1 Superior resilience alone can reveal the maximum com-
fort of motoring on pneumatic tyres. Many a car owes
its reputation for luxurious springing and silky running to
the buoyant liveliness of its Avon Tyres.
ECONOMY
*j[ The same qualities of strength and resilience that
achieve safety and comfort necessarily cut down ultimate
costs —of* petrol, repairs to chassis and tyres as well.
Avon economy is the economy of endurance —the typically
British sort of tyre beauty that is more than tread-deep.

Miss Gladys Cooper Explains
Some Beauty Secrets.
The Popular London Star Favors Simple Methods.
I have been asked to give a few simpfe recipes that I know, either through personal
use or by observation, to be valuable to the toilet, and which are within the reach of the
average woman. In these days of £lOO facial treatments and elaborate and expensive
beautifying processes my suggestions may read like lessons in economy, but they are
not especially so intended. They are merely practical suggestions, in which the keynote
is “effectiveness.” All the materials or ingredients which I mention are either already
at hand in the home or may be readily procured from the chemist. Fortunately I do not
suffer from the ailments or troubles enumerated below, but some people who are not so
fortunate have told me their experiences, and with your permission I will set forth some
remedies which they have found to be efficacious.
Home-made Hair Tonic.
My acquaintances say that a good stimulant
to the natural. growth of the hair is boranium.
A very simple, effective and safe stimulating
lotion made up by mixing one ounce of bora-
nium with 1 pint of bay rum. This may be
applied to the hair roots occasionally with the
finger-tips with good results.
Complexion Renewals.
Complexion experts advise me that a normal,
healthy complexion is constantly renewing it-
self by dropping off tiny flakes of worn-out
tissue, thus revealing the fresh young skin
underneath. They say that when this process
is checked by age, expo-
sure or some of many
causes, the complexion
becomes dull and ugly.
The rational treatment
recommended is to help
the skin perform the
natural functions of
“shedding” worn-out tis-
sue. For this purpose, I
am informed, there is
nothing so good as pure
mereolized wax, used for
a few nights, just as you
would use a face cream.
It is claimed that it pos-
sesses a special affinity
for the effete -scarf-skin,
which it quickly removes
by absorbing it. The face,
I am assured, will soon
look much younger and
prettier under this treat-
ment.
The Curling Iron.
Don’t use a hot iron to
curl your hair. Some of
my friends make the cun-
ningest sort of curls
wherever they want
them simply by damp-
ing the hair with liquid
silmerine before retiring
at night. When the hair is dry in the morn-
ing it will be softly curly just where you
want it to be. This method is perfectly harm-
less, even beneficial to the hair, and the curls
last a long time. The liquid is quite pleasant
and neither sticky nor greasy.
How to Shampoo.
Most women, I am informed, do not know
how to use stallax properly when shampooing
with it. Unless the hair is naturally very
oily, a stallax shampoo may sometimes leave
it rather dry. But lam told if you will apply
olive oil freely to your hair and scalp just
before shampooing with stallax, the result is
most delightful. The hair will be left clean,
soft, bright and wavy, the olive oil having
properly balanced the action of this wonderful
hair cleanser.
Is Powder Necessary?
A great many women object to using pow-
der, for various reasons. The following
formula is a good one: Dissolve an ounce of
MISS GLADYS
Photo Wrather & Jiuy.
COOPER
cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of water, or
witch-hazel, and use it as a face lotion,
smoothing the skin with the fingers until it
is dry. This method is perfectly harmless.
I am told that a really beautiful, natural, vel-
vety bloom results and remains for many hours
quite unaffected by the most trying conditions
out of doors or in the ballroom or theatre,
and that it gives a much more natural appear-
ance to some skins than does ordinary powder.
Grey Hair.
I have observed many attempts of many
people to conceal grey hair. Some of these
experiments were amusing, some disastrous,
and some were success-
ful. Personally I believe
I shall let my hair turn
when the appointed time
comes, but if I were
going to try to evade it,
1 would give a trial to a
real old “grandmother”
formula that would prob-
ably do the work. This
formula, I am informed,
has been used with de-
grees of success for many
generations, and consists
merely of two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite
mixed with three ounces
of bay rum. It is ap-
plied to the grey hair
a few times with a small
sponge, and ladies tell
me it appears to darken
the hair to a natural
shade, not like a dye, but
gradually and naturally.
Miscellaneous Hints.
A few useful hints from
my friends on aids to
beauty, are as follows: —
The eyebrows and lashes
may be made to grow
stronger and darker by
applying mennaline, a
specially delicate pomade, with the finger-tips
at night.
Rouge is often obvious, and many women
therefore prefer to use powdered colliandum,
which gives a most natural tint.
The lips may be prevented from drying and
chapping by touching them with a stick
of prolactum, which also gives them a good
color.
Freckles are most effectively combated with
ammoniated mercury creams, of which jetta-
line is the best.
Note. —This interesting article op beauty
culture in general was written by Miss Gladys
Cooper at the request of the manufacturers of
Pilenta Soap—the best complexion soap in the
world. On sale by all chemists. [4

The Inky Way.
The I.W.W. journal Direct Action has
been having a hectic time during the last
few months. The arrest of editor Glynn
and King, one of his chief assistants, was
the beginning of the trouble; but I.W.W.
enthusiasts sprang to desk and press and
brought out the defiant paper, somewhat
tamed. Then the police raided the rooms
and removed a lot of material, including
the lists of subscribers and all the books.
They left the printing press, however, and
the paper emerged as usual in hopes that
the subscribers would find it. Later the
police came down and collared the press.
But arrangements have been made for the
S. M. Herald’s reptile contemporary to
crawl out of another press and bite the
capitalist again. In fact, the owners of the
wild, rebellious thing say optimistically that
they propose to raise £250 for a new plant
—“and we are going to get it.” The changes
have brought to light a new editor, J. A.
Kinman, who has improved the style of the
paper. There is less clanking of chains and
more real argument in its columns. Kin-
man is a dreamer and a revolutionary, but
it is doubtful if he has any real sympathy
with sabotage. He was born in Malta, his
father being a British officer, and ins great
enthusiasms are rebellion and chess.
* * * *
From the latest Italian report:—
We extended our lines westward of the Oppacchia-
sella-Castag-nevizza-road.
A petition is now being signed by cable
editors asking the Italians and Russians to
shift the fighting into easier country or use
numerals. Oh, for the sweet and simple days
of Hill 60 and Pub 742!
* * * #
War makes poetry as well as other
troubles. London Sunday Times reviews
18 new volumes of verses in one batch, and
10 of them owe their origin to the Kaiser.
One in the bouquet is Henry Lawson’s “Song
of the Dardanelles and Other Verses.” The
reviewer, who is quite familiar with Henry,
sums up his new volume of verses as “pure
Lawson with the authenticated Bulletin
swing, sense and force about them.” The
title poem and “My Army,” and “Young
Kings and Old” are singled out as “stirring
and straightforward lyrics.” Another talks
of the “full-blooded, go-as-you-please rhyming
with plenty of flashes of real poetry in it.”
And still another: “Here we have Mr. Law-
son hammering away at war verses with all
his well-known verve.” Harrap is the pub-
lisher, and the price half a crown-
* * * *
Paul Goldenstedt, who was bustling on the
Inky Way in Sydney for the D.T. when the
Devil said to Wilhelm “Up and at ’em!” has
since then been bustling with the Billjims.
News that he had climbed to the rank of cap-
tain with a camel corps cheered up his pals
last week. Soon after he enlisted it was dis-
covered that Goldenstedt was a German spy
or something, and for some time nothing
would shift the official determination to fire
him. Other proofs of his right to wear the Aus-
tralian uniform appearing to be of no avail,
the Intelligence Department was informed
that “Goldie” was born in Greenland of Irish
but respectable parents, and that removed all
suspicion.
* * * *
News of the death of a couple of young
Launceston Wayfarers came through last
week. “Tas” Dobson, who used to be with
the Daily Telegraph, but had been for
the last six years at Mackay (Q.), is listed
“killed in France.” “Bas” Kerslake, who
had been reported missing since the Romani
fight, is also posted killed. It appears
Kerslake went into action dressed only in
shorts and singlet, and without his identifi-
cation disc. A mate, seeing him fall, threw
over the body his own coat containing his
paybook, the result being that Kerslake was
reported missing and the live mate killed.
Before he became Trooper Kerslake lie was
a junior on the Examiner’s staff. Every
man of military age and fitness on the
Examiner’s literary staff has volunteered.
* * * *
Editor Boote, of Sydney Worker,
deserves most of whatever glory hangs to
the success of the anti-conscriptionists in
N. S. Wales, who decided the matter for
the other five States. Catts, who ran the
general anti campaign, was small potatoes
by comparison; yet he bathed in the lime-
light, and forgot to mention Boote’s work
when the fight was over. Before the battle-
proper started there was a struggle amongst
the people concerned in the management of
the paper as to whose views would prevail—
Boote’s or Lamond’s. Boote won. It is only
a few months since he went through another
campaign. He flung himself, almost with
vindictiveness, into the fight for early-closing
and swayed a few drayloads of unionist votes
in favor of 6 o’clock. Boote came from
Brisbane Worker to Sydney four or five
years ago.
THE INSPIRATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I possess a verse-struck barber
Who looks down upon my face
As my chin he deftly lathers
With a swift and easy grace,
Till he sees the inspiration
Gleaming brightly in my eye;
Then he shaves in eager silence
And he lets the chaige go by.
When with eyes glued to the ceiling
And my high brow creased in thought,
I attempt to look abstracted,
As a poet really ought,
He can see my great thoughts flowing,
And would deem it base and rash
To disturb me, when I’m going,
With a low request for cash.
He will say “ ‘Remarks on' Moses’
, You composed within this chair
Just a fortnight from to-day, sir!—•
I could see it by your air!”
And I let him keep his fancy
In his soul of verdant green,
For an inspiration’s handy
When you haven’t got a bean!
N.S.AV.

Dark.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
miIREE hours ago the sun went down
Beyond the trees, behind the hill;
The noise is striding from the town,
The dew is on the window-sill;
And this poor wavering candle-light-
This feeble glimmering timid spark
Beats golden-winged against the Night,
And saves me from the shrouding Dark.
All day the hideous Dark has hid
In reeking cells and curtained rooms,
And lain beneath each coffin-lid
In catacombs and granite tombs,
In festering drains, in dusty nooks,
In cupboards, in forgotten drawers;
Between the leaves of filthy books;
Behind the prison’s fastened doors;
In drunkards’ pockets and in caves,
In harlots’ cloaks and blind men’s eyes.
In wrecks which rot beneath the waves,
In homes wherein the first-born dies,
In opium dens and villains’ hats,
Within the snouts of murder-guns,
Beneath the wrinkled wings of bats
And in the robes of sorrowing nuns.
But now through every, airy place,
O’e'r moaning seas and frightened lands,
Feeling the horror ’gainst her face
A cool wind walks and wrings her hands.
A million million motes of black ,
Are flooded ’twixt the earth and sky,
Seething upon her lonely track
And whirling as she passes by.
And I do hate this horrid thing
Which bides outside my window-pane,
With every vice which it can bring
From every bed where it has lain.
And so I guard this yellow cone
And shield it from each prying breath,
Lest it should die and leave me lone —
For Light is life and Dark is death.
But now my failing beacon lacks
Its strength to climb the tottering wick,
The air is pungent with the wax
That boils within the candle-stick;
And swiftly through the closing gloom
Black fingers quench the waning spark—
The Dark, the Dark, is in my room !
Oh, Life is Light but Death is Dark!
MAOKILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
My Brown Boy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN my sister Muriel imagined
that her husband was neglecting
her ever so slightly it was her
method of management to- give him a shock.
Muriel is pretty, and as flirting comes
natural to her she would rouse his jealousy
and then soothe him by explaining, with her
head on his shoulder, that she had felt neg-
lected, and, of course, dear one, it was break-
ing her heart.
After that. Ralph would be an ideal hus-
band for six months. -
It was not till Edward took up black-and-
white that I had anything to really com-
plain of. He had suddenly determined to
make me proud of him, and had decided to
achieve fame. He had often suggested poli-
tical cartoons to a friend, and had sometimes
been paid for these inspirations. But as he
truthfully pointed out, had he drawn the
picture he would have got more money and
all the fame, which, as it was, went to the
artist who perpetrated the drawing.
He attended class twice a week and all
the other evenings he sketched me. He took
to bringing home journalists and artists for
dinner, and there were terrific discussions
on art and politics. Some of them were very
trying.
Then the fly got into our ointment. The
artist he was studying under said it was
necessary that he should take a course of the
nude. I did not understand him at first, and
when he explained that it meant he was
going to draw pictures of other women with
their clothes off, and draw them from life, I
put both feet down firmly
“Very well, dear,” ~e said sadly, “I’ll give
up my ideas of an artistic career. It is no
good being an artist unless I am one of the
best, and that I can never be unless I take
a course of the nude. I’d sooner chuck the
whole business than have trouble with you,
for you are my life.”
Of course, that settled it. I sniffed a while
and then inquired why he could not have all
men models. It seems that is quite useless;
so then I made him look out of the window
and said in a very hard voice:
“Very well, dear; if it is necessary to
your career, I will be your model myself. I
don’t mind.”
He reeled at the suggestion. It would
never do, he explained, me would have to
show his pictures and compare them with
others, and he couldn’t flood the city with
pictures of his wife in tire altogether. Any-
how, where was the harm? There would be
always a crowd there.
I gave in, but I said I did not like it.
Still, I couldn’t destroy his career.
Of course, eventually, he soothed me down.
He always does; and in due course he
brought home a beautiful picture of some
shameless cat. But it was a lovely thing—a
red chalk sketch, and the more I looked at it
the more I realised the danger of it. Why,
if th? creature was only half as pretty as
the picture he made of her it was impossible
for him or any other man with red blood in
him to avoid being attracted by her. Women,
if thev are pretty, always appeal to strong
men, especially if they have their clothes
partly off.
I determined to try sister Muriel’s remedy.
It seemed to me that Edward was away
from home much more than was necessary.
I knew he was working very hard, but still
he could have stayed at home more and
made heads of me. But no; always he maae
heads, as well as the other sort, of her, and
I knew he was being attracted in spite of
himself, and, perhaps, poor boy, unknown to
himself. It was certainly my duty to pro-
tect him from these piratical females.
So, for the sake of both of us, I deter-
mined to try Muriel’s remedy.
The most interesting man among the com-
pany he occasionally brought home was Reg
Larke. Reg was an artist, too, but had
thrown it up to deal in wool. He could
draw beautifully, and from the first time I
met him he was wild to do my head in red
chalk. This particular night he brought up
the subject once more, and asked Edward’s
consent. Edward said he would be delighted.
I listened breathlessly. Would Edward
stay at home anu draw my head, too?
Of course, he wouldn’t. I could have cried
with vexation. Then he gathered up his
portfolio and hurried off to his class, leaving
Reg and me alone.
Reg had brought his book and pencils, and
he began right away. But he had hardly got
started when Aunt Susie came in and looked
inexpressibly shocked at finding us so
engaged.
Aunt Susie is a regular dowager. She never
thinks good of people unless they are charged
with some crime. She suspects them till she
considers they have proved themselves inno-
cent. And proof always seems to her hard
to find.
When she was going she asked Reg. to see
her to the train. So he said good-night, and
the drawing was suspended, to be resumed
the next afternoon.
When she got him outside she slipped
back and whispered :
“I’ll come to-morrow to protect your good
name. It will never do to have this man
coming here by himself, making sketches of
you alone, you know.”
I opened my eyes very wide.
“Why,” I demanded, “what’s wrong? My
Brown Boy wouldn’t hurt a fly !”
Aunt Susie sat down with a bang. She
was appalled at my brazenness. And then
she gave me what she called a piece of her
mind, and if all the rest of her intellect is
like it, I wonder how she lives carrying so
much poison about her system.
But the poison sohked in and made me
unhappy.
Next day Aunt Susie arrived in time to be
told that word had come that Brown Boy
would not be able to take the sitting. So
Aunt Susie went forth to do some shopping
for the Dorcas Fund.
Half an hour later Brown Boy arrived.
And when I told him of mv fib he said he
preferred to work with only our two selves
present.
Then he set to work with his crayons, and
he seemed to work wonderfully quickly. He
knew exactly what he wanted and went
straight for it. Suddenly he stood up :
“Behold, it is done !”
I stepped beside him and gazed upon the
beautiful creation. It took my breath away.
I glanced at the tiny mirror and saw that
while preserving the likeness he had glorified
me.
“Do you like it?”
“Who could do otherwise? It is like and
it is not. If I were half as beautiful as that
I’d go on the stage and make a fortune as a
beauty actress.”
I laughed when I said it, but the laugh
had a catch In it.
“Let me make lots and lots of beautiful
pictures of you, and you shall live in art-
galleries for ever.”
And out of his slumbrous eyes came a
flash that made me thrill.
* * * *
“This is to be my masterpiece,” he said as
he posed me, twined my brown hair
round my head in a fashion of his own,
placed a rose in it, slipped my shoulder out
of the draping, and held me off at arm’s-
length- and examined me critically.
“You have the most beautiful arms God
ever put on a woman,” he said with that
sudden flash in his eyes that sent waves of
little thrills chasing one another all through
me.
I knew we were on dangerous ground. I
glanced at the half-finished picture and then
out of the window. Oh, good heavens, there
was Aunt Susie and just behind her sister
Muriel! Aunt was opening the gate.
“Quick, Brown Boy, lock the door —there’s
Aunt and Sis, and just try to imagine what
Aunt will think if she finds me posing to you
alone, in bare feet, both arms and one shoul-
der uncovered ! I’m lost if they get in.”
“They haven’t got a key, I suppose?”
“Of course not, but leave the key turned
in the door in case they try to fit another into
it. What a joke !”
We heard Aunt’s heavy step on the stairs
and just at that moment I noticed the fan-
light." It was wide open. To shut it was
impossible, so I stepped lightly and quickly
out of the range of any reflections, and drew
him with me.
They knocked and knocked as though they
knew I was in. Then they went downstairs
and borrowed chairs from Mrs. Hay. I
began to grow desperate. Suppose they
waited till Edward returned.
Then I nearly screamed. Muriel had
formed the mad idea of trying to get through
the fanlight, and she was so small and
athletic it was more than likely she would
succeed.
There were curtains over an alcove at the
end of the room and I hastily dived behind
them and drew the Brown Boy after me, and,
as I peeped out, Muriel’s head came through
the fanlight.
I put my mouth to his ear and whispered:
“Be prepared to drop from the balcony into
the garden. I’m sure she’s coming right in.”
Muriel was halfway in, and suddenly my
blood froze. There were his hat and gloves
on the chair. And Muriel halfway through
the fanlight was staring at them wonderingly.
She cast one long, suspicious glance around
the room. Her face grew suddenly dark, and
then I heard her say:
“It’s no good, Aunt; I’ll break my neck if
I go through. Let’s get home again.”
Then she drew back, and in the sudden
relief I found I had been clinging to him
with both arms, and that he was holding my
waist.
We heard the front door close and the front
gate clang. Still he did not release me and
I still clung to him. I shut my eyes—l
felt intuitively the rush of the storm.
(Continued on next page.)

I hen I looked into his half-closed eyes
and our lips met. I seemed to suddenly
.arow drowsy. All the strength went out of
me and I just hung limply in his arms
winie he choked and smothered me with
caresses.
Suddenly he dragged me forth from behind
those curtains out into the sun-swept room
and held me. at arm’s length against the
wall. My hair had broken loose and hung
about me in a cloud. The crepe draping had
sapped down and left me bare of arm.
shoulder and bosom.
His fingers held me so tightly that I knew
he was bruising my flesh. I didn’t care. I
liked it. It was a glorious pain. He shook
me.
“You make me mad —mad—mad ! This is
wrong. It’s unfair to you, to me and to—
him.
“Yes, it’s dreadful! Why do you do it?”
You must make me treat vou properly,”
he gasped.
I let my face droop within easy reach of
his:
I his is very beautiful —I’m not complain-
ing.”
Stop, stop, for God’s sake —you drive me
mad I’m angry with you. angry with my-
self.”
“I like you best when you are angry,” I
laughed, with just a tinge of hysteria in it.
He literally hurled me to the sofa, sprang
to the chair, grabbed his hat and, before I
could say a word, was gone. He even left
bis gloves—l have them yet.
I heard the front door close with a crash
heard the gate clang, and then I buried my
lace in the cushions and I was going to crv
but a light step on the stair startled me.
Mster Munel came in. bent over me with a
grave face, kissed me on the lips and stole
away without a word.
I got up, bathed my face, dressed, and set
to work to cook Edward’s dinner. And
among my pots and pans over my dainty little
ele ctric cooker I dreamed mad 'dreams.
V\ hen Edward came home I shut my eyes
while he kissed and embraced me—it was
wonderful how much alike those two men
kissed.
Later on, when Edward had gone to class,
I shivered as I thought of the Brown Boy
coming back to finish his masterpiece.
* * *
But that was two years ago—and he
never returned. He avoids me. And I—
well, I m too proud to run after any man.
CLARA EJAY.
A Natural Jumper.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHENEVER I see a fanatical punter
whispering behind his hand to a
reverent friend I think of my own
brief reign as a turf tipster. I did not seek
any distinction along that line—it just fell
upon, me because our stable had been rented
by a couple of able-bodied men with an im-
pressive-looking horse. One of the able-
bodied was the discoverer and trainer of the
impressive-looking horse, and the other was
the local butcher who had a flourishing
suburban business. The flourishing business
was keeping the whole circus, including the
two grooms, who assisted the trainer; but
the grand secret was allowed to leak out that
the impressive-looking horse was “a natural
jumper ’ and would make the whole gang
rich by running away with the Grand
National Steeplechase at Flemington. Its
name was Blue Ensign.
I never quite realised what credulous
people I lived and worked amongst • until I
casually asked a betting friend if he had
ever heard of the horse that was lodging
with us. His eye lighted up at once and he
gave me the sort of respectful glance a
curate might win by casually letting slip
a State secret. “No, old man, I never heard
of it. Do you know anything?”
I told my friend the full circumstances
and he was obviously impressed. He begged
me not to mention the animal too much and
to let him know anything further. From
that time on he treated me as an established
authority on the turf. I was handicapped
through not knowing any of the usual race-
course patter about weights and gallops;
but the very fact of having the animal at
m.v back door outweighed every other con-
sideration. My reputation grew rapidly
and I found that wherever a few sports
wore gathered together the conversation
always got round to horseracing—and me.
It was very gratifying to one who had never
served an apprenticeship to bookmakers to
have hard-faced old sports feeding out of
the hand.
Perhaps my greatest triumph was scored
on the evening Dr. Mick took me along to
tbo \ elocipede Club. When everybody had
become flushed and talkative, Dr. Mick
started to draw medical diagrams on the
bar counter by way of illustrating a story of
Ins. When he was in the dead centre of his
yarn, he mentioned quite casually that I
had the Grand National winner stabled down
at my house. After that none of the com-
pany had any time for him. The club bar-
man leant as far over the counter as he
could and the Velocipede Clubmen crowded
around and begged me to tell them all about
it. I told them all I could remember —how
the butcher was keeping the trainer and
the trainer was keeping the horse and two
grooms, and how the hdrse was expected to
turn round and keep the lot of them. To
me it sounded about as interesting as some-
thing out of “The Child’s First Reader” ; but
the sports drank it all in and asked for
more. I then recounted the loud, blasphe-
mous arguments that went on day and night
about the diet of Blue Ensign, and how the
trainer walked round the brute last thing
at night with a hurricane lantern and looked
at its legs. Everybody listened with atten-
tion, and a couple of strangers who had
come in for drinks and seemed to be in-
clined to kick up a row about not getting
them sidled over and joined the group. They
seemed quite sorry when I ran down,
and, as they had missed the first part of my
lecture, begged me to go over it again. Dr
Mick also begged me to go over it again. I
did so and then we agreed (all shouting
together) that it would be wise to keep
the whole thing quiet so that the bookmakers
might be caught on the hop and utterly
ruined. They seemed to be so sure they
were the only people who knew that I
hadn’t the heart to tell them of the stream
of visitors who came to the stable in our
back paddock and talked about the horse
from morning till night. Apparently these
people had nothing at all to do but talk
about horses and swear about horses. They
chewed some of Blue Ensign’s straw while
they argued and cursed, and when they got
tired of standing they sat on bags of oats
that the infatuated butcher had bought for
the pampered brute that was going to win
the big jumping event of the year by jump-
ing naturally in a wonderful way of its own
which it had taught itself in Maoriland. It
was understood that the trainer had detected
Blue Ensign in the very act of jumping
naturally in Maoriland and had resolved on
the spot to devote the rest of the year to
training him for the Grand National. The
financial collaboration of the suburban
butcher was a necessary evil, and nobody
paid much attention to him. When the
idle sports were in full session out in the
stable he merely led the chorus. The rugged
horse masticated its feed in a perpetual
storm of argument and blasphemy, and the
day of the Grand National approached.
When the first betting-market quotations
were published I noticed with some astonish-
ment that the bookmakers had in some
amazing manner missed hearing about the
Blue Ensign peril. Blue Ensign was rated
by the pencillers as a 30 to 1 chance —and
when the Ring offers 30 to 1 about an'
animal it isn’t nervous about that animal’s
chances of winning. Apparently all the
yelling and cursing had gone unheard —the
secret had been kept. It cheered me up
greatly. There had been times when it
seemed to me that I wasn’t guarding it any
too well. In fact, there had been days when
I seemed to do nothing else but tell the
friends of friends that Blue Ensign was a
good thing. From what I could hear they
all invested money on it —on the strength of
my expert and exclusive advice.
I went out to Flemington Flat on Grand
National Saturday with one of the sports
who had risked some of his hard-earned
cash. We perched on a fence down at the
far end of the straight—-the part where the
races just begin to get exciting, and you
haven’t the remotest idea what is winning
because you are behind the field. With his
trained eye he saw Blue Ensign sail past
the first time (it was just a blur to me),
and he mentioned gloomily that “the cow
seemed to be cloutin’ his fences a treat.’’
The tiring field was strung out when it
thundered past next time —and Blue Ensign
wasn’t there! He wasn’t even the limping
riderless thing that a mounted policeman
captured when the crowd had over-run the
steeplechase coui’se like a swarm of ants.
“Don’t know what happened to that
natural jumper of yours!” growled the sport
as we climbed down and prepared to go
home. Away in the distance the crowd was
roaring like, the sea as two unknowns fought
out the finish. The race had lost all its
interest for- us Blue Ensignese, and I won-
dered how many of my unfortunate dupes
were scattered about the course.
We didn’t find out anything about the
race until we struck a glum sport on the
railway station. “Blue Ensign !” he shouted
in answer to my mate’s question. , “Oh.
that blanky thing! Why, he clouted
everything he came to with his nose, his
four feet and his tail! Only did four or
five jumps—his jockey pulled off to save
his own neck! No, I wasn’t on him! I
went broke on Fireworks.”
I wondered whether I would ever survive
the disgrace.
That night a blizzard of explanation blew
in our liorse-lodger’s stable, and quite a lot
of Blue Ensign’s straw was eaten by human
visitors. Blue Ensign’s trainer was full of
optimism, and made it quite plain to every-
body that if 14 things hadn’t happened and
seven, others had, Blue Ensign would have
won in a canter —in a hlanky cantez*. He
was a jumper all right —anybody could see
lie was a jumper. But they had happened
to strike a day when he didn’t feel like
jumping. So the race had been won by
something that had learnt how to jump at
a business college. The sporting butcher
sat heavily on a bag of bran and listened
to it all with great attention. He wore the
wistful look of one who is trying to per-
suade himself that he has had good value
for his money.
I ventured into town full of apprehension
on Monday. To my immense surprise my
sporting friends bore me no ill-will. On the
contrary, they buttonholed me to tell me
exactly why Blue Ensign hadn't won. Every
sport had a different explanation. Browiz
smith said he hadn’t got away well. Bill-
wag reckoned lie had been bumped. Dodds
(a constant pessimist) felt sure the jockev
had backed something else. Oddman (opti-
mist) knew the horse had blundered at the
in st fence and then lost heart. He gave it
great credit for standing up at all. Fyshe
passionately denied that Blue Ensign had
hit anything at all with his talented feet.
He described with his hands how it had
fairly sailed over the fences. But the mis-
take it made was in staying too long in the
air instead of coming down promptly and
resuming the galloping part. It seemed to
hover if you could understand what he
meant. Nobody could, but that didn’t stop
fiyshe talking. It was very soothing to
have these deluded sportsmen explainin <r
away my mistake, and I saw for the first
time that, where racing is concerned, Hope
is a drug self-administered.
i f m ee , k , later 1 was told that the butcher
had filed Ins schedule. And next day in
the city an old sport, who had been at the
game too long to bet, told me the trainer of
Blue Ensign made a comfortable living by
lucking up natural jumpers and then pick-
ing- out natural payers, so that he always
had a bag of chaff to sit on and a stable to
curse away the days in.
The horse may be a noble animal, but it’s
a pity it can’t speak and tell the truth about
itselr.
